==PAGE:6==
Abstract
Hearing impaired listeners commonly experience challenges to interpret complex acoustic
scenes even if they wear hearing aids. The problem is that most current hearing aids have
a beam former that enhances the auditory object directly in front of the listener whereas
objects from the side are suppressed. However, due to the dynamical nature of auditory
scenes it would be more feasible to have a hearing aid that adaptively steers the directivity
of the beam former towards that auditory object that the listener is attending to. In recent
years researchers have attempted to develop auditory attentional decoders (AADs) that
can predict the speaker the listener is attending to. A particularly appealing candidate
for a real world AAD is the auditory-inspired state space model by Akram et al. (2015).
This AAD was shown to have a high temporal resolution while making robust decod-
ing based on magnetoencehpalography (MEG) in an auditory scene with two co-located
competing speakers. The current project attempts to expand this model towards more
realistic auditory scenes with more than two (spatially separated) speakers. Furthermore,
this project applied the state space model to electroencephalographic (EEG) data. The
results showed a decoding accuracy close to chance level, i.e. the tested framework was
not successful in the considered real world conditions with spatially separated speakers. In
an auditory scene with spatially separated speakers the processing of the attended speaker
is performed predominately in cortical regions located contralaterally to the location of
the speaker. Hence, an attentional switch by the listener causes the cortical responses to
alter if the location of the new target speaker is dierent from the location of prior target
speaker. This indicates that the EEG channels that best capture the neural processing of
the attended stream depend on the location of the attended speaker. This is problematic
for the tested AAD because the linear combination of EEG channels that it uses to infer
the attentional state of the listener is constant and hence might miss some of the task-
relevant information. The decoding performance of the tested AAD might be improved if
the linear combination of channels can be adaptively changed based on the location of the
attended speaker. A method for inferring the location of the attended target via adaptive
channel selection is discussed together with other potential improvements of the system.

1
Motivation
Hearing is a key stone in the social and cultural development of humans since hearing is
the foundation for the development of language and hence communication [Seeley et al.,
2010]. It is known that the human brain requires a high-delity spectro-temporally feature-
representation of sounds in order to interpret these acoustic signals correctly and that
these spectro-temporally feature-representations are reduced in hearing impaired listeners,
which will aect their ability to interpret the acoustic signals properly. Especially in a
complex acoustic scene, like a cocktail party, hearing impaired humans are challenged with
interpreting the acoustical scene probably because the acoustic signals energetically mask
one another [Shinn-Cunningham and Best, 2008]. This struggle might lead to a strong
feeling of isolation for hearing impaired listeners [Seeley et al., 2010]. Thus, in recent years
a broad range of algorithms have been developed to improve hearing impaired listeners
quality of life by ameliorating their ability to communicate in an everyday setting, like a
cocktail party. The idea is that a neuro-steered hearing aid can use electroencephalographic
(EEG) signals to adaptively steer its beam former towards the speaker that the listener
is attending to.
The beam former can then suppress non-target auditory streams and
enhance the target stream based on the fact that speech signals contain many pauses that
are statistically uncorrelated between dierent speakers. However, the main problem is
that the target stream can not be easily identied since it depends on the attentional state
of the listener. The mentioned algorithms attempt to address the problem of extract the
attentional state of the listener and hence tries to extracting the target-speaker in a complex
acoustic setting. These algorithms are denoted as auditory attentional decoder (AAD)
algorithms. The current state-of-the-art AAD algorithms have some practical limitations.
First, many of the current AAD algorithms are tested on either magnetoencephalographic
(MEG) or electrocorticographic (ECoG) recordings, which is problematic because both
of these measurement techniques are not practically applicable [Roman-Gonzalez, 2012]
[Alickovic et al., 2016]. Second, nearly all the current AAD algorithms have a temporal
resolution in the order of minutes, which is too slow to detect the dynamic switch of
attention that listeners undergo in a cocktail-party-like scenario. The latter limitation was
addressed by Akram et al (2015) who used a biophysically-inspired state space model to
track the attentional state of the listener from MEG recording.
In this project the framework by Akram et al (2015) was applied to EEG to evaluate
its decoding performance based on these neural recordings. Furthermore, the stimulus
presentations were altered to evaluate the performance of their framework in more realistic
conditions. This included a condition with more than two speakers, which required an
expansion of their model.

CHAPTER 1.
MOTIVATION
==PAGE:18==
2
Clinical Background
The senses are the sources of information to the central nervous system (CNS). The in-
formation from the senses are obtained by specialized sense cells. The input to the sense
cells can come from within the body or the external environment [Porth, 2015]. The sense
cells are specialized so that a specic type of sense cell only transfers a specic external or
internal stimulus to electric impulses. These impulses are then carried by neuron to the
CNS where they will be processed and analysed. The sense cells are located either densely
in specialized sense organs (like the ear) or spread throughout the entire body. Typically,
the sense cells are grouped based on the type of stimuli its sensory receptors respond to.
An example sense cells that respond to mechanical pressure or disturbance, which have
mechanoreceptors on their surface [Sand et al., 2008]. Evolutionary, the human body has
specialized its sense cells to only extract the essential features of the external environment.
For example, the external environment possess electromagnetic waves with wave lengths
that the human eye can not see and there exist sound waves with frequencies outside the
audible frequency range of humans [Sand et al., 2008].
The information obtained by the senses are the foundation for thoughts, emotion and
learning. Hence, sensation is indispensable for the creation of ones own identity [Sand
et al., 2008]. However, not all sensory inputs are registered consciously. The unconscious
analysis of sensory inputs is important since these informations are used to maintain home-
ostasis. An example of an unconscious analysis is that the medulla oblongata uses sensory
signals from baroreceptors and chemoreceptors in the blood to regulate the blood-pressure
and pH-level of the blood [Seeley et al., 2010].
In general, the human body maintain
homeostasis without conscious involvement [Porth, 2015]. The conscious awareness of the
stimulus is called perception (or sensation) and is generated when nerve impulses from the
sensory cells reaches the cerebral cortex [Porth, 2015]. However, not all action potential
that reaches the cerebral cortex is consciously perceived.
Actually, many of the nerve
impulses that reach the cortex are ignored [Seeley et al., 2010]. This is essential since hu-
mans would probably not be able to function if all sensory informations were consciously
registered [Porth, 2015]. Instead of registering all sensory information, humans have the
ability to selectively attend to certain sensations and hence increase the awareness of the
sensory inputs that create this sensation [Seeley et al., 2010]. The specic sensory percep-
tion of nerve impulses from a sensory organ (e.g. the cochlear) is generated based on the
cortical regions to which the impulses are conducted [Sand et al., 2008]. Furthermore, the
sensory information that reaches one cerebral hemispheres can be shared with the other
hemisphere by commissural nerve bres, however, the information sharing is generally not
equal between the two hemispheres [Seeley et al., 2010]. The left hemisphere is mostly in-
volved in analytical skills (such as mathematics and speech) whereas the right hemisphere
is involved in spatial perception and the recognition of faces [Seeley et al., 2010].

==PAGE:19==

CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
2.1
The Periphery Auditory System
The organ involved in hearing (and equilibrium) is the ear, which is divided into three
parts; the external ear, the middle ear, and the inner ear, see Fig. 2.1. The external and
middle ear is only involved in hearing, whereas the inner ear is involved in both equilibrium
and hearing. Hearing is the foundation for the development of language, hence this sense is
Figure 2.1: This gure sketches the three parts of the human ear and their anatomical relations. The gure is
from [Seeley et al., 2010].
the most important for the social and cultural development of humans [Seeley et al., 2010].
Hearing is central in our everyday life and maybe this fact is the reason why a hearing
impairment will lead to the strong feeling of isolation, stonger than for an impairment of
any of the other special senses [Seeley et al., 2010].
2.1.1
The External Ear
The external ear consists of the auricle (the pinna) and the external auditory canal, see
Fig. 2.1 The auricle is the eshy part of the ear, which is located superciel to the head.
The shape of the auricle increases the collection of sound wave and helps directing these
waves towards the external auditory canal [Seeley et al., 2010]. The sound waves (that
by the auricle were directed into the external auditory canal) are conducted through the
external auditory canal towards the tympanic membrane.
The tympanic membrane is
the separator between the external auditory canal and air-lled middle ear and it is a thin
semitransparent nearly oval membrane [Sand et al., 2008]. The sound waves that reach the
tympanic membrane will impose oscillations of the tympanic membrane at their inherent
frequencies [Seeley et al., 2010].
2.1 The Periphery Auditory System
==PAGE:20==
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
5
2.1.2
The Middle Ear
Posteriorly to the tympanic membrane is the middle ear (or the tympanic cavity). The
middle ear is a small air-lled mucosa-lined cavity within the petrous portion of the tem-
poral bone [Porth, 2015], see Fig. 2.1. Medially, the middle ear is separated from the inner
ear by two membrane-covered openings; the round and oval windows.
The vibrations of the tympanic membrane is transmitted through the middle ear to the
liquid-lled inner ear by three tiny bones called the auditory ossicles; the malleus, the
incus, and the stapes, see Fig.
2.1. The transmission of vibration from the tympanic
membrane to the auditory ossicles is accomplished by an attachment of the handle of the
malleus to the tympanic membrane. Hence, vibrations of the tympanic membrane cause
the malleus to oscillate with the same frequency. The head of the malleus is connected
to the incus by a synovial joint and the other end of the incus is connected to the stapes
again by a synovial joint. Hence, oscillation of the malleus will spread to the foot plate of
the stapes. The foot plate of the stapes ts into the oval window, where it is held in place
by a exible annular ligament [Seeley et al., 2010]. This mechanical linkage implies that
the vibrations of the tympanic membrane will be conducted to the foot plate of the stapes,
which makes the oval window vibrate. The vibrations of the oval window introduce oscil-
lations in the perilymph of the inner ear (the perilymph is a liquid, which mostly consists
of water).
One might wonder what the function of the middle ear is since the vibrations of the oval
window's membrane essentially ends up oscillating at the inherent frequency of the vi-
brations of the tympanic membrane. To understand the function of the middle ear it is
important to know that water and air have dierent densities and compressibilities. The
dierence in density (and compressibility) between the two types of uids does that sound
waves travelling in air are reected when hitting an air-to-water-interface. The function
of the middle ear is primarily to help modifying the sound energy so that most of this
energy is transmitted from the air at the tympanic membranexternal to the perilymph
of the inner ear (which primarly consists of water). The middle ear accomplishes this by
being a pressure-amplier. The amplication is achieved by two mechanism [Seeley et al.,
2010]. The rst mechanism is that the surface of the tympanic membrane is 20 times
larger than the surface of the oval window, hence the pressure at the oval window will
be 20 times as large if the mechanical force is identical at the tympanic membrane and
the oval window because pressure is force per unit area [Seeley et al., 2010]. However, the
mechanical force are not equal at these two membranes due to the second mechanism. The
second mechanism is a mechanical force amplication produced by rotation of the auditory
ossicles. These two mechanism will together increase the pressure 25-folded, which ensures
an eective transmission of (sound) energy from the air in the external auditory to the
liquid in the inner ear [Sand et al., 2008].
2.1.3
The Inner Ear
The Anatomy of the Inner Ear
The structure of the inner ear is embedded in the temporal bone. The inner ear consists of
the vestibule, the cochlea and the three semicircular canals (see Fig. 2.1). The cochlea is
the sensory organ for hearing (while the vestibule and semicircular canals are responsible
2.1 The Periphery Auditory System
==PAGE:21==
6
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
for the sense of equilibrium) [Sand et al., 2008]. These channels and chambers are denoted
as the bony labyrinth and they are cavities in the temporal bone. The bony labyrinth
is lined with endosteum. Luminal to the bony labyrinth is the membranous labyrinth.
The membranous labyrinth has the same shape as the bony labyrinth but it is smaller.
The intersection between the the endosteum and membranous labyrinth is covered with
a very thin layer of perilymphatic cells. The perilymphatic cells produces a uid called
perilymph, which has a chemical composition very similar to that of the cerebrospinal
uid [Seeley et al., 2010]. The perilymph is located in the space between the bony labyrinth
and the membranous labyrinth. Inside the membranous labyrinth is another uid called
endolymph. Endolymph is a clear uid that dierence in composition from the perilymph
by having a high concentration of potassium and a low concentration of sodium [Seeley
et al., 2010]. The membranous labyrinth of the cochlea is divided into three parallel uid-
lled tunnels; the scale vestibuli, the scala tympani, and cochlear duct, see Fig. 2.2b.
These tunnels are wrapped around a bony core; the modiolus. The upper tunnel, the scala
vestibuli, extents from the oval window to the helicotrema at the apex of the cochlea. At
the helicotrema, the scala vestibuli has an open connection to the lower tunnel, the scala
tympani. The scala tympani terminates at the round window, see Fig. 2.2a. The scala
vestibuli and the scala tympani are lled with perilymph and hence they are the walls
between the bony and membranous labyrinths. The last tunnel is called the cochlear duct
(or scala media) and spans from the base to the apex of the cochlea. The cochlear duct is
the endolymph-lled membranous labyrinth of the cochlear. The cochlear duct is separated
from the scala vestibuli by very thin membrane, the vestibular membrane (or Reissener
membrane) and from the scala tympani by the brous membrane, the basilar membrane,
see Fig. 2.2b [Seeley et al., 2010]. The vestibular membrane consists of a double layer
of squamous epithelium, which makes it so thin that it has little or no mechanical eect
on the transmission of sound waves through the inner ear. This implies that the scala
vestibuli and the cochlear duct mechanically can be thought of as a single chamber, even
though, their chemical compositions are dierent.
The basilar membrane is somewhat
more complex and it is physiologically more interesting when it comes to the mechanics
of hearing [Seeley et al., 2010].
The basilar membrane has an acellular and a cellular
portion. The acellular portion consists of ground substance, collagen bers and sparsely
dispersed elastic bers. The cellular portion is composed of a simple squamous epithelium.
At one side the basilar is attached to the bony spiral lamina, which are projections from
the modiolus. At the other side, the basilar membrane is attached to the lateral wall of
the bony labyrinth. The width of the basilar membrane, from the spiral lamina to the
spiral ligament, increases from 0.04 mm at the base of the cochlear to 0.5 mm at the
apex [Seeley et al., 2010].
The collagen bers of the basilar membrane are orientated
across the membrane and they span the entire width. The mass of the collagen is nearly
identical along the longitudinal axis of cochlea, which makes the collagen bers shorter
and thicker near the oval window compared with those near the helicotrema [Seeley et al.,
2010]. On top of the basilar membrane the organ of Corti (or the spiral organ) is located.
The spiral organ is a highly modied structure, which contains specialized sensory cells
and a supporting epithelium.
The sensory cells are called hair cells due to around 80
very long microvilli, often referred to as stereocilla, at the apical surface of the sensory
cells [Seeley et al., 2010], see Fig. 2.2d. In each cochlea there is approximately 15.000 hair
cells [Sand et al., 2008] [Hudspeth, 2014]. The hair cells are organized in four rows that
longitudinally span the entire length of the coclear duct. The hair cells are divided into
two groups: the inner and outer hair cells. The inner hairs cells are the row of hairs that
are located closest to the spiral lamina. The inner hair cells are primarily responsible for
2.1 The Periphery Auditory System
==PAGE:22==

7
Figure 2.2: This gure sketches the auditory sensory organ; the cochlea. The gure is a combination of gures
from [Seeley et al., 2010] and www.slideshare.net.
hearing [Seeley et al., 2010]. The steriocilia of one inner hair cell form a conical group
denoted as a hair bundle. The hair bundle is formed such that the length of the stereocilla
increases gradually from one side of the hair cell to the other. Within the hair bundle the
distal tip of each stereocillium is connected to the side of tallest adjacent stereocillium by
a tip link [Hudspeth, 2014]. The link tip attaches to a gate on a K+-channel to form a
mechanoreceptor, which is this case is a mechanogated K+-channel.
The outer hair cells are the three rows closest to the spinal ligament and they are separated
from the inner hair cells by a gap in the basilar membrane. The function of the outer hairs
is to regulate the tension of the basilar membrane [Seeley et al., 2010]. The hair bundles
of the outer hair cells are similar to those of the inner hair cells but the hair bundles
of the outer hair cells dier by being arranged in a curved line. The tips of the longest
stereocilia of the outer haircells are embedded wihin an acellular, gelatinous shelf; the
tectorial membrane [Seeley et al., 2010], see Fig. 2.2c. The tectorial membrane is attached
to the spiral lamina and it is important for the generation of action potentials in response
to sounds [Seeley et al., 2010].
The hair cells are a secondary sense cells since they have no axons. Instead, the basilar
2.1 The Periphery Auditory System
==PAGE:23==
8

regions of each hair cells are covered with synaptic terminals to bipolar sensory neurons
[Seeley et al., 2010]. There are approximately twice as many aerent somata in the cochlear
ganglion as there are hair cells [Seeley et al., 2010].
The axon of these sensory neurons join to form the cochlear nerve. The cochlear nerve
then joins the vestibular nerve (the sensory nerve from the equilibrium organ) to become
the vestibulocochlear nerve (VIII), which transmits the action potentials to the CNS [Sand
et al., 2008].
The Physiology of the Inner Ear
So far we know that sound waves introduce vibrations onto the tympanic membrane and
that these oscillations will be conducted to the footplate of the stapes which will make the
oval window vibrate. When the stapes is forced into the scala vestibuli it causes displace-
ment of the perilymph. The displacement of the perilymph causes the same displacement
in the endolymph of the cochlear duct since the scala vestibuli and the cochlear duct me-
chanically can thought of as a single chamber. Since both the perilymph and endolymph
are nearly incompressible and the cochlea is embedded in bone, the displacement of the
endolymph causes the basilar membrane to bend downwards and the membrane of the
round window to bend outwards. The bending of the round window's membrane happens
because this membrane is elastic and the uids are nearly incompressible. The displace-
ment of the uids and the bending of the membranes are reversed when the stapes is pulled
towards the middle ear [Sand et al., 2008]. This implies that the vibration of the stapes
causes simulations oscillations of the endolymph.
These waves in the endolymph then
causes distortions of the basilar membrane. The waves of the basilar membrane happens
because the helicotrema is too small to sucient transmit all the mechanical energy of
the waves in the perilymph to waves in the perilymph of the scala vestibuli [Seeley et al.,
2010]. The round window acts as a mechanical release for waves within the cochlea, which
is important since this relief of pressure in the perilymph prevents compression damage
to the organ of Corti. This mechanical release is also important for audition because a
solid round window would reect the waves and hence interfere with later waves [Seeley
et al., 2010]. The distortion of the basilar membrane moves as a traveling wave toward the
apex of the cochlea. The travelling wave along the basilar membrane causea the hair cells
to be displaced relative to the tectorial membrane since the tectorial membrane reamins
stationary [Sand et al., 2008]. When the wave makes the basilar membrane go upwards it
will cause a bending of the stereocillia at the apical portion of the hair cells. This bending
of the sterocillia opens the mechanogated K+-channels of hair cells. This opening results
in an inux K+ due to an electrical gradient pointing towards the hair cells. The electrical
gradient comes from an intracellular charge of -140 mV compared to the endolymph. The
chemical gradient over the K+-channels of hair cells is approximately zero because the
concentrations of K+ in the endolymph and in the cytoplasma of hair cells are nearly
the same (both have a high concentration of K+) [Seeley et al., 2010]. The inux of K+
causes a depolarization of the hair cells. In an unstimulated hair cell the resting potential
is around -60 mV. The resting potential is taken in comparison to the perilymph which
has a chemical composition similar to other extracellular uids [Seeley et al., 2010]. This
implies that the enocochlear potential (the voltage dierence between the endolymph and
perilymph) is + 80 mV. In the resting state approximately 15% of the mechanogated K+-
channels are open.
Hence, a negative stimulus (one that makes the basilar membrane
bend downwards) can hyperpolarize the hair cells by slacken the tension of the tip link
2.1 The Periphery Auditory System
==PAGE:24==
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
9
which closes more K+-channels. In comparison, a positive stimulus opens additional K+-
channles and hence depolarizes the hair cells. The depolarization in response to a positive
stimulus causes voltage-gated Ca2+-channels to open [Seeley et al., 2010]. The opening
of the Ca2+-channels results in an inux of Ca2+, which creates a positive feedback loop
where Ca2+-channels gradually open as the cell further depolarizes. The depolarization
continues until a membrane potential of 10 mV is reached. The depolarization of the hair
cells has two consequences;
1. An increased release of neurotransmitters into the synaptic clefts between the hair
cells and the aerent neurons. The release of neurotransmitters causes the aerent
neurons to increase their action potential frequency. The release of neurotransmitters
are also eected by hyperpolarization of the hair cells. The hyperpolarization causes
a decrease in release of neurotransmitters and hence result in a decreased action
potential frequency in the aerent neurons [Porth, 2015].
2. An opening of voltage-gated K+-channels in the basal portion of the hair cells that
causes an outux of K+ into the perilymph due to an electrochemical gradient. This
outux results in a repolarization of the hair cell.
The response of a mechanoreceptor is very brief in the range of microseconds [Sand et al.,
2008] [Hudspeth, 2014].
It might seem that the travelling wave stimulates the entire basilar membrane equally.
However, this would mean that frequency discrimination is impossible.
The frequency
discrimination comes from the fact that the amplitude of the traveling waves along the
basilar membrane are largest in a specic region and that this specic region is depending
on the frequency of the sound. The frequency specicity is due to the changes of the colla-
gen bers along the basilar membrane, see Sec. 2.1.3. Recall, that the changes gradually
makes the basilar membrane wider and limber towards the helicotrema [Sand et al., 2008],
hence, leading to a spatial gradient of stiness of the basilar membrane. As a result of
this stiness gradient, high frequency vibrations of the endolymph cause large displace-
ments of the basilar membrane near the oval window while low frequency vibrations of
the endolymph increase the amplitude of displacement near the helicotrema [Sand et al.,
2008], see Fig. 2.3. The partial spectral analysis that the basilar membrane preforms
is a frequency-to-place mapping, which is denoted as the tonotopical organization [Plack,
2014]. In areas where the amplitude of the vibrations of the basilar membrane are minimal
the increase in release of neurotransmitter will be too low to reach the threshold for the
formation of an action potential. In areas where the amplitude of the vibrations of the
basilar membrane are small the increase in the action potential frequency of the aerent
neurons will be present but small. However, this increase is not nearly as large as it is in
region with optimal movement of the basilar membrane [Seeley et al., 2010]. Vibrations
of the endolymph, containing only one frequency, will eect a very narrow and marked
area of the basilar membrane and hence only a small set of the sensory nerve bres will
be activated. This specic tonotopic organization makes humans able to discriminate fre-
quencies [Sand et al., 2008]. The frequency-to-place mapping of the basilar membrane is
the limiting factor of the frequency selectivity of the entire auditory system, thus the tun-
ing properties of the basilar membrane is the limiting factor of humans ability to separate
sounds based on frequency [Plack, 2014]. A normal hearing human is able to resolve tones
that dier in frequency by only 0.2% [Hudspeth, 2014].
As mentioned the inner hair cells are primarily responsible for hearing while the outer hair

90 % of the aerent neurons synapse with inner hair cells, leading to each inner hair cell
having approximately 10-30 synaptic connections [Seeley et al., 2010] [Hudspeth, 2014].
The remaining 10 % of aerent neurons synapse with the outer hair cells. The outer hair
cells also synapse with some eerent neurons. The innervation by these eerent neurons
cause a contraction of action laments within the stereocilia of the outer hair cells leading
to a shortening of these stereocilia [Seeley et al., 2010].
Since the outer hair cells are
attached to both the basilar membrane and the tectorial membrane, this shortening of
the stereocilia results in a ne-tuning of the tension of the basilar. The shortening of the
stereocilia will also allow the distance between the basilar and tectorial membrane to be
n-tuned. This outer hair cells mediated mechanism is called the cochlear active process
because outer hair cells convent electrical energy into mechanical energy that substain the
travelling wave [Seeley et al., 2010]. The cochlear active process is essential for hearing
because it counteracts the dissipative eects caused by the viscosities of the uids while
also enhancing the oscillations of the basilar membrane more than a thousand-fold [Hud-
speth, 2014]. Another type of eerent neurons are able to adjust the sensitivity within
the inner hair cells. The innervation by these eerent nerves cause the K+-channels to
move sideways along the plasma membrane. The movement is generated by a contraction
of actin laments attached to the channels (inside the hair cells). The movement of the
K+-channels tightens or loosens the tip links depending on the direction of the movement.
This implies that the (inner) hair cells can be tuned to very specic frequencies by the
means of eerent neurons.
The frequency selectivity is fundamental for audition because it plays a key role in many
other aspects of auditory perception such as music and speech perception [Hudspeth,
2014] [Plack, 2014]. However, the sharpened frequency tuning is not identical to all fre-
quencies for an intact cochlea. The sharpening depends both on the frequency and the
sound pressure level. It has been shown that at the base of the basilar membrane the
vibrations are sharply tuned and that this tuning is level depended. Meanwhile, the mo-
tions near the apex do not show either of these two properties. Furthermore, it has been
shown that lters used to model the tonotopic organization of the basilar membrane have
an Q10 that increases with characteristic frequency (from 2 at 300 Hz to about 5 at 10
kHz) [Plack, 2014]. This fact leads to a possible hypothesis for the dierence in sharpen-

ing. The hypothesis is that the 'auditory lters' with low charactistic frequencies are sharp
enough without tuning (due to their small bandwidth) while this is not the case for 'au-
ditory lters' with high charactistic frequencies. To sharpen the 'auditory lters' at high
frequencies (and hence increase frequency selective at high frequencies) the movement at
the base of the basilar membrane must be actively amplify (by the outer hair cells). This
amplication is accomplished by a compressive non-linearity [Plack, 2014], see Fig. 2.4.Ac-
tually, the compressive non-linearity exists at the apex but it is less prominent [Roberles
and Ruggero, 2001] [Plack, 2014]. It can from the input/output functions of Fig. 2.4 be

seen that frequencies near and at the CF lead to a nonlinear compressive response behavior

and more assymetric and at very high sound pressure level the excitation is very broad
with a downwards frequency-shift of peak response. This downwards shift is denoted as
the basalward shift of the travelling wave [Plack, 2014]. The basalward shift does that a
denition of the characteristic frequency is necessary. The general denition is that the
characteristic frequency of a 'auditory lter' is the center frequency of the 'auditory lter'
in a response to a sound with a low sound pressure level [Plack, 2014].
The non-linear compression of cochlea will be reduced or absent for a hearing impaired,
which reduces frequency selectivity signicantly [Plack, 2014]. This degraded tuning of a
damaged cochlea is often a big problem when designing hearing aid because a simply am-
plication of sound would not compensate for the loss of frequency selectivity and hence
not increase speech intelligibility [Roberles and Ruggero, 2001] (the easiest way to test
frequency selectitivety is by during a masking study like the notched-noise experiment,
which also can be used to extract the 'auditory lters'). It is remarkable that even when
the non-linear compression of the cochlea disappears, it has only minor eects on the
phase transfer function of the 'auditory lter' [Roberles and Ruggero, 2001]. In [Roberles
and Ruggero, 2001], it was shown that the slopes of the phase responses are steepest just
above the CFs of the 'lters' and that the curves are more or less linear in these regions.
Lastly it was shown that the lower the CF (of the 'lter') the steeper the slope. From
signal processing it is known that the negative of the time derivative of the phase is the
group delay [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007]. Hence, in the case of linear phase responses
the group delay will be a constant. Hence, the lower the CF of the 'auditory lter' the

The fact that non-linear compression aects dierent part of the basilar membrane dier-

2014]. Another interest feature of the auditory nerves is their ring patterns in response to
acoustic clicks. The ring patterns are commonly portrayed by a post-stimulus-time (PST)
histograms [de Boer, 1980]. A PST histogram is generated by discretizing the post-stimulus
time and count how many times action potentials that are observed in each segment in
response to multiple clicks. Hence, the PST histogram approximate the probability (by
a relative frequency) of an action potential generation in the given (aerent) neuron as a
function of post-stimulus time. otherwise there will be statistical uctuations in the PST
histograms (see Sec. 3.1). In Fig. 2.5 PST histograms for three aerent neurons are shown.

2.2
The Periphery Auditory System from a Mathematical
Point of View
As mentioned in Sec. 2.1.3, the structure of the basilar membrane causes dierent regions
of the membrane to be excited by dierent frequencies. This tonotopic organization is
the origin of the frequency selectivity of the auditory system and it does that the basi-
lar membrane can be modelled as a lter bank. The frequency response of the basilar
membrane can be modelled in multiple ways, including the transmission-line model (an
equivalent electrical circuit for the mechanical system) and a gamma tone lter bank.
Both of these models require that the continous system is discretized into segments. It
is assumed that each of these segments have a specic resonance frequency and that this
resonance frequency must be a function of distance along the basilar membrane, based on
the knowledge from Sec. 2.1.3. In the following the resonance frequency of a segment is
the frequency at which the segment of basilar membrane absorbs most of the travelling
wave's energy because the absorption of mechanical energy causes the basilar membrane
to vibrate.
In the following each segment of the basilar membrane will be modelled as a gammatone
lter and hence the entire basilar membrane is model as a gammatone lter bank. Before
discussing the gammatone lter the concept of Equivalent Rectangular Bandwidth (ERB)
will be introduced.
2.2.1
Equivalent Rectangular Bandwidth
The Equivalent Rectangular Bandwidth (ERB) is used to quantify the bandwidth of lter.
The ERB is the width of a rectangular lter that encapsulates the same amount of energy
as the lter D and has the same peak gain, DMax [Plack, 2014] hence

2.2.2
Gammatone Filters
In literature, the revcor function and the gammatone function have been used interchange-
ably. However, the revcor function is a continuous representation of a dataset obtained
from an experiment where the ring pattern of a primary auditory nerve bre is correlated
with the waveform at the input to the ear. The theoretical result of this reverse correlation
procedure is an estimate of the impulse response [Patterson et al., 1987]. In comparison
the gammatone function is an phenomenological model that some physiologists used to
nd an analytic expression for the revcor function [Patterson et al., 1987]. In 1972, Johan-
nesma was the rst to introduce what is known as a gammatone lter. He used the impulse
responses of the gammatone lters to characterize the impulse responses of the auditory
nerve bres in cats and he found that the gammatone lter provided an excellent t to
these impulse responses [Patterson et al., 1987] [Patterson et al., 1995]. The analytical
expression for the impulse response of an auditory gammatone lter with centre frequency
fc is given by
g(t) = a−1tn−1e−2πbtcos(2πfct + ϕ),
t > 0
(2.4)
where a is a normalization factor used to make the peak gain equalt to one [Yang et al.,
2015]. The parameter n is the lter order which determines the slope of the skirts of the
lter. Furthermore, the parameter n controls the relative shape of the evnelope such that
the envelope becomes less shewed as n increases (if b is xed) [Holdsworth et al., 1988].
b determines the lter bandwidth and hence the duration of the impulse response due to
the time-frequency scaling property. An increase in b lead to wider lter and hence short
impulse responses [Holdsworth et al., 1988]. fc is the centre frequency of the lter and ϕ
is the carrier phase which determines the position of the ne structure of the sinusoidal

2.2.3
Gammatone Filter Bank
The gammatone lters can be used to construct a gammatone lter bank that mimic the
frequency partitioning of the basilar membrane.
It is accomplished by convolving the
impulses responses of each of gammatone lters (in the bank) with the acoustical signal,
which leads to a set of ltered outputs (notice, that these convolutions can be accomplished
in parallel to increase the time-eciency). The outcome of the gammatone lter bank is
often plotted in a three dimensions space (with the rst axis being time, the second axis
being the channel number and the third axis being the amplitude output). This plot is
denoted as a cochleogram and it represent the motion of the basilar partition in response
to the stimulus. An example of a cochleogram is shown in Fig. 2.7. It can be seen from
Figure 2.7: The upper plot shows the magnitude sprectrum of the gammatone lter bank that is used to generate
the cochlograms below.
The lower plots shows two cochlegrams.
The left cochlogram is obtained
without time-delay compensation and the right cochlogram is obtained with time-delay compensation.
The stimulus applied to generate both cochlograms is an impulse at time 0.
lower left plot in Fig. 2.7 that the minimum phase characteristic of the gammatone lters
cause a right shewness of the low frequency channels of gammatone lter bank due to the
time-frequency scaling property and the fact that widths of the lters (b = 1.018 · ERB)
scale with frequency (this frequency scaling of the widths is observable in upper plot of
Fig. 2.7). This shewness is a natural property of cochlear processing since there is a phase
2.2 The Periphery Auditory System from a Mathematical Point of View
==PAGE:32==

17
lag in the ring pattern of the auditory nerve bres. Furthermore, it should be noticed
that the widening of lters as a function of frequency captures the fact that the human
auditory system has a 'poor' spectral resolution at high frequencies.
It has been shown that the phase lag does not matter in timbre perception and hence it
would be advantageous to remove the skewness from the cochleogram. The reason is that
the skewness has no known advantageous eects on the model's ability to predict perceptual
changes. The skewness can be compensated by a straightforward method [Patterson et al.,
1987]. The method is to align the impulse responses of all the gammatone lters (in the
lter bank) so that their envelopes peak at the same time point. This can be achieved by
introducing (channel specic) time delays, td = n−1
2πb , to each of the outputs of the lter
bank, for derivation see appendix E.9. The cochlogram of a gammatone lter bank where
these time delays have been incorporated is shown in the lower right plot of Fig. 2.7. As
seen from this plot, the lead td does not lead to a complete allignment of the peaks of
gammatone lters' impulse responses. If a complete alligment is desired the oscillating
components should be adjusted until the ne structures peak coincided with the envelope
peak. This can be accomplished by introducing phase corrections, ϕd = −2πfctd [Patterson
et al., 1987].
These phase corrections align all lters to have a peak in their impulse
response at t = 0, however, this second alignment procedure was not be implemented in
this project (consideration regarding the implementation is given in App. E.10).
2.3
Spatial Hearing
Spatial hearing (or spatial localization) refers to the ability to identify location of a sound
source in terms of direction and distance [Plack, 2014]. Human perceives the surrounding
environment as three dimensional and hence the location of the sound source (with respect
to the head) can be identied by three parameters; the radius, r, the azimuth θ and the
elevation δ. The radius, r, is the measure of distance in meters whereas the azimuth θ and
the elevation δ are angles in radians. Both θ and δ are assigned zero when the source is
located at the median plane at a high equal to the ear canals [Plack, 2014]. The plane that
passes thought both ear canals is called the horizontal plane. The elevation is dened such
that for all δ ∈]0, π[ the source is located above horizontal plane and for all δ ∈] −π, 0[ the
source is located below horizontal plane. The azimuth (θ ∈[0, 2π[) is dened clockwise
hence if θ = π
2 and δ = 0 then the source point directly toward the right ear. In the
following the distance judgement are neglected and hence only the sound direction will be
explain.
Spatial hearing relies primary on binaural hearing, however, monaural cue like pinna ef-
fects can help resolve the spatial localization. To perceive an auditory object, see Sec. 2.4,
as being spatial located away from the median plane requires the stimulus to be dichotic.
The dichoticity is naturally generated by Interaural Level Dierences, ILDs, and Interaural
Time Dierences, ITDs, and hence these two phenomena are essential for the perception
of spatial localization [Duraiswami and Raykar, 2005] [Schreuder et al., 2010]. It is known
that the speed of sound in air is more or less constant and that it does not change in-
stantaneously [Plack, 2014] [Sand et al., 2008]. These two factors do that sounds that are
diracted around the head will have a dierence in arrival times at the two ears. These
time dierences are the ITDs. The ITDs are only a cue for low-frequency components of
a sound. The reason is that the wavelength of a low-frequency tone are longer than the
head dimensions which introduces diraction. In the case of a high-frequency tone the
diraction will be small due to reection by the head. Furthermore, a pure high-frequency
2.3 Spatial Hearing
==PAGE:33==
18
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
tone will also lead to a phase ambiguity [Plack, 2014]. The cues that the high-frequency
components will use are the ILDs, which are introduced to due the reection by the head.
The reection decreases the energy at the ear located most far way. This decrease in energy
is equivalent with a decreased sound pressure level and hence the ILD is introduced. The
fact that the ILDs and the ITs dominate dierent frequency ranges are known as Duplex
theory [Carlile, 1996].
It was mentioned that a pure high-frequency tone will lead to a phase ambiguity, the rea-
son is that for an adult human the longest distance across the head is around 21.5 cm
which implies that the maximal ITD is
21.5cm
1cm/(30µs) ≃0.65 ms. Hence, if a pure tone has
a frequency of more than
1
2·0.65ms ≃750 Hz, then there will be a phase ambiguity. To
illustrate the phase ambiguity assume that a pure tone has a frequency of little above 750
Hz and that this tone is played at θ = π/2 and δ = 0 then the phase dierence between the
ears does that the tone peaks at the left ear just before it peaks at the right ear. The lead
of the left ear might cause the tone to be perceived as comes from the left, even tough, it
actually comes from the right.
The phase ambiguity might suggests that the ITDs are not useful features for sound lo-
calization in the real world, however, this suggestion is wrong. Actually, the ITDs are the
most important cue for the human spatial hearing [Plack, 2014]. The reason is that most
natural sounds have low-frequency components and furthermore most natural sounds has
envelope uctuations. As mentioned in App. D, the envelope uctuations dier from the
ne structure by being slower. This implies that the envelope uctuations can be used to
obtain ITD even if the carrier frequencies are above 750 Hz [Plack, 2014].
The second problem with ITDs is that some location will have the same ITD. Imaging a
cone with its tip insert into one of the ears then all sound sources located on the surface
of the cone generate the same ITD [Plack, 2014]. These ambiguities can be resolved by
moving the head. However, even if the head is xed it is not true that all sources on the
cone have the same ITD. The reason is that incoming sound waves are modied due to
scattering by the head and the torso. These modication are dierent depending on direc-
tion [Duraiswami and Raykar, 2005]. Especially, the pinna helps modifying the incoming
sound [Plack, 2014]. The combined scattering eects generated by upper body and the
head can be encapsulated in terms of a total spectral ltering. These spectral lters are
denoted as the Head Related Transfer Functions, HRTFs, which are complex frequency
response functions that depend on sound source location [Duraiswami and Raykar, 2005].
The HRTF has a specic set of spectral peaks and notches in the high-frequency region
above 4000 Hz [Plack, 2014]. These peaks and notches are generated by resonances in
the cavities of the pinna and they are hence depending on the angle at which the sound
wave strikes the pinna. The size of pinnal cavities are the reason that only high-frequency
components of the signal are modied by the pinna since they are the only components
with a wavelenght short enough to be eected. This is one of the reason why the HRTF
are frequency depend. The dependency of the HRTFs on the shape of the pinna (and
torso) does also that the HRTFs are individual [Seeley et al., 2010]. The pinnal eect are
particularly important in determining the elevation of sound sources [Plack, 2014], how-
ever, the pinna cues also helps resolving the frontback ambiguities when high-frequency
components are presented in the sound.
The physiological features that lead to the HRTFs are lost when wearing headphones,
which lead to lateralization. Lateralization is the perception of a sound source as being
located inside the head [Plack, 2014]. The lateralized sound image can be externalized
by applying recorded HRTFs. The HRTFs are, as mentioned, complex frequency response
functions, which implies that they have impulse responses (see App. E.2 for the deni-
2.3 Spatial Hearing
==PAGE:34==
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
19
tion of impulse responses). These impulse responses are denoted as Head Related Impulse
Responses HRIRs and they can be recorded experimentally [Ferracuti et al., 2013]. Sub-
sequently the HRTFs can be obtained with a Fourier transform [Duraiswami and Raykar,
2005]. If both the left and right HRTF at a specic sound source location are known then
the externalization to this specic sound source location is generated by presenting the left
ear with a convolution of the auditory signal, s, and HRTFleft and presenting the right
ear with s convoluted with HRTFright [Ferracuti et al., 2013]. As noted, the HRTFs are
participant individual, however, it is possible to get a sensation of externalization by ap-
ply HRTFs measured with an articial 'dummy head' that includes a pinna model [Plack,
2014].
2.4
Listening in a Competing-Speaker Environment
Most normal-hearing listeners have the remarkable ability to attend to a single conver-
sational partner in everyday social settings. It is remarkable because the listeners can
eortlessly separate dierent sound sources, dierent talkers and other ordinary sounds,
and attend to only one of these sources in a complex environment [Shinn-Cunningham,
2008] [Bregman, 1990]. This behavioral capacity of human listening was rst examined by
the auditory psychologist Colin Cherry in 1953, who looked at selective attention in regard
to auditory communication signals [Cherry, 1953]. In his study he used two spoken mes-
sages and presented them diotically and dichotically. He showed that it was possible for
the subject to separate speech in the diotic case but that it was easier in the dichotic case.
Cherry pointed out that it makes intuitive sense that the subject experienced no diculty
in attending to the wanted message and reject the unwanted message in the dichotic case
because a listener in a crowded noisy room instinctively turns one ear towards the target
speaker. Cherry also showed that the subjects did not notice detailed aspects (such as
individual words, sematic content or langauge) of the unattended message but that certain
statistical properties (such as if the unattended speaker changed from male to female) was
recognize. This asymmetry might come from the fact that attention exogenously can be
drawn to an appearing object if the object has a saliency that is high enough to win the
biased competition whereas a disappearing object does not have a saliency unless it is the
focus of attention [Pavani and Turatto, 2008]. This suggest that it is not possible to listen
(and perceive) two concurrent speech streams [O'Sullivan et al., 2014].
In the following years, hearing research mostly focused on how information is coded in the
auditory periphery with relatively little emphasis on how central processing capacity limits
perception. However in the 1970s and 1980s, Albert Bregman described the challenge of
Auditory Scene Analysis [Bregman, 1990] [Plack, 2014]. In his work, Bregman articulated
many of the rules governing the perceptual organization of sound mixtures, which is nearly
synonymous with the idea of auditory object formation [Shinn-Cunningham, 2008]. This
lead to many psychoacoustic studies that tried to quantify the principles that he articu-
lated. The general discussion in these studies centered on how auditory scenes are parsed
and not on the role of attention. During the same period, neuroimaging studies demon-
strated the importance of attention in sculpting what auditory information is encoded in
the cortex. These neuroimaging studies attempted to nd the cortical regions responsible
for controlling attention, however, they ignored how attentional performance depends on
early stages of sound encoding (e.g. in the cochlea, brainstem, and midbrain). This cre-
ated a debate around whether attention to speech operates at an early stage of processing
based on the physical characteristics of the stimulus or at a later stage during semantic
2.4 Listening in a Competing-Speaker Environment
==PAGE:35==
20
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
processing,. This debate still continues, even though newer hypothesis on the subject have
started to argue that selective attention to speech actually operate as a multi-stage pro-
cess [Power et al., 2012].
The fact, that auditory researchers have tried to understand how humans (and other an-
imals) solve the cocktail party problem for over a half century and still only have little
knowledge about the neural mechanisms and processes behind the separation of sound
sources, illustrates the intrinsic complexity of this process [Shinn-Cunningham, 2008], [Al-
ickovic et al., 2016]. This complexity belies the ease with which humans attend to one
speaker in a crowded environment. The reason is that what seems to be a trivial problem
is actually a mathematical ill-posed problem if one tries to analyze the signals reaching the
listener's ears. It is ill-posed because the concurrent complex sounds will be completely
mixed upon entering the ear. The challenge is to re-segregate these sounds and then se-
lectively attend to the desired speaker while tuning out the others [Mesgarani and Chang,
2012]. This ability is astonishing, especially, since the most sophisticated machine learing
algorithms cannot yet reliably extract meaning from a single source in a cocktail party
scenario [Ding and Simon, 2012a] [Alickovic et al., 2016] [Plack, 2014].
As mentioned, there has historically been a great gap between hearing science and other
aspects of neuroscience when trying to understand the cocktail party problem. This gap
has gradually been closing over the last two decades. One of the key realization that helped
bridge this gap came from a study by Shinn-Cunningham in 2008. Shinn-Cunningham re-
alized that even though there had been an increasing interest in how central limitations
interfere with auditory perception there was no comprehensive framework to explain the
human selective attention [Shinn-Cunningham, 2008]. Shinn-Cunningham tried to close
this gap by arguing that it was possible to extend the theories of visual attention to under-
stand how humans solve the cocktail party problem. The rst theory Shinn-Cunningham
translated was that visual attention is argued to focus attention on an object in a com-
plex scene [Shinn-Cunningham, 2008]. Thus, Shinn-Cunningham argued that the brain
processes auditory objects. Unfortunately, just as in vision science, it is dicult to dene
an object [Feldman, 2003]. The problem of giving a precise dening of an auditory ob-
ject is that only a few absolute rules governing auditory object formation exist. However,
the intuitive understanding of an auditory object can be extraction from a cocktail party
scenario. In a cocktail party humans can perceive the individual speaking on the left, the
chiming doorbell and a shattering plate. Each of these is an example of an auditory object.
This implies that an auditory object is a perceptual entity, which is perceived as coming
from one physical source.
The theory of auditory object formation relies much more on the temporal structure of
the complex scene compared to visual object formation [Shinn-Cunningham, 2008]. The
auditory information is conveyed in the spectro-temporal content of sound whereas visual
stimuli is much more static. Thus, the spectro-temporal features can be used to parse
the acoustic scene into auditory objects. The fact that the information in sound evolves
through time means that the perception of objects in an auditory scene often emerges
gradually. The gradual emergence of auditory objects eects the ability to selectively at-
tend since a more pronounced auditory object is easier to focus on. This implies that the
ability to selectively attend to an auditory object in the complex auditory scene is also
emerging gradually and will become more specic over time.
The auditory object formation is hypothesised to operate at two relatively long time
scales [Bregman, 1990].
The shortest of the two time scales is denoted as the 'local'
scale, or simultaneous grouping, because it binds sound energies which are concurrent or
close together in time and/or frequency.
This step is also refered to as integration of
2.4 Listening in a Competing-Speaker Environment
==PAGE:36==
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
21
simultaneous components by Bregman [Bregman, 1990]. On the 'local' scale sounds are
grouped based on common onsets and osets, harmonic structures and continuity of fre-
quencies over time. More generally, sounds that have correlated uctuations in amplitude
modulation tend to be grouped into the same short-term auditory object [Plack, 2014]. It
has been shown that the key modulations driving the local object formation are typically
below about 7 Hz, which is slower than the modulations that determine perceptual prop-
erties of sound. Hence, the local object formation emerges over 10s of msec [Cusack et al.,
2004] [Chait et al., 2010]. This low modulation frequency is corresponding to the syllablic
rate in everyday spoken English which have onset/oset envelopes below 10 Hz range [Gi-
raud and Poeppel, 2012]. The short-term object formation is robust in speech due to the
rich spectro-temporal structures of speech signals. It has been shown that spatial auditory
cues have relatively weak inuence over the local time scale suggesting that spatial cues
are quite noisy in the real world due to eects of reverberation as well as interference from
other sound sources. The reason is that reverberant energy and energetic masking distort
the ITDs and ILDs quite signicantly, while aecting the amplitude modulation and har-
monic structure less. This can explain why moderate reverberation often degrades spatial
cues signicantly without interfering with perception of other sound properties [Ruggles
et al., 2011].
The local spectro-temporal grouping cues (both strong and weak) might seem to be fun-
damentally dierent from one another. However, in a more global sense, they are sim-
ilar because they all reect statistical correlations in the short-term spectro-temporal
structure, which typically arise when sound energy is generated by a common natu-
ral sound.
This implies that the multiple cues (correlated envelope structure, related
harmonic structure, and related localization cues) all together support the same local
grouping of sound rather than competing with one another. The local grouping is hence
driven mostly by the gestalt principle of common fate.
Common fate means that the
sound components generated by the same source is likely to be temporal correlated.
