The second time scale is the forming of coherent streams [Bregman, 1990], which is the
grouping of locally grouped energy bursts into auditory objects that extend through time.
This is why humans perceive ongoing speech as a stream even though there are discon-
tinuities across local spectro-temporal cues. These higher-order features and regularities
work on the order of seconds to be perceived [Cusack et al., 2004] [Chait et al., 2010]. The
assembling of short-term objects into a stream is facilitated by the continuity of higher-
order perceptual features (such as spatial location, pitch and timbre) of the short-term
objects presented in a sequence. All of these features contribute to hearing the individual
short-term objects as a single ongoing source. It is noticeable that the spatial auditory
cues have a strong inuence of how we group together short-term auditory objects into
a coherent stream. This is probably why there was an counter-intuition for short-term
auditory objects with respect to spatial cues since it is not just the location of a singal
short-term object but the location of a sequence of these objects that is important for
speech segegration [Best et al., 2008]. An illustration of the sequential streams composed
by short-term auditory object is given in Fig. 2.9d.
Fig. 2.9a is the complex reference tone. If the fourth harmonic of this complex tone is
increased in frequency but has the same onset as the other harmonics of the complex tone
then the complex is precevied as a single auditory object with a higher pitch, see Fig. 2.9b.
However, if the onset of the increased fourth harmonic is dierent from the other harmonic
of the complex tone then the fourth harmonic is perceived as a second auditory object,
primarily due to simultaneous grouping, see Fig. 2.9c. It is also possible to generate two
2.4 Listening in a Competing-Speaker Environment
==PAGE:38==
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
23
auditory objects by utilizing the long-term scale. If a precursor sequence to the fourth
harmonic is present then the complex tone and the fourth harmonic is perceived as two
sequential streams, see Fig. 2.9d. The time scale of the stream formation suggest that
the grouping mechanisms operating over a long time period, which implies that it happens
in the cerebral cortex [Plack, 2014]. Even though the fundamental frequencies and the
harmonics are important on the local time scale, since is can be used to identify and sep-
arate dierent voices in speech based on pitch and timbre it is also important on the long
term scale when dealing with speech. The reason is that human speech will have a smooth
variation in fundamental frequency (even when signing), see App. D [Plack, 2014]. The
smoothness of the fundamental frequency is known as good continuation and it implies
that no abrupt discontinuities exist. It has been shown that by articially introducing a
rapid alternation between a low and high fundamental frequency in an otherwise natural
voice causes the perception of two speaker, hence two auditory objects [Plack, 2014].
The above description might create the illusion that the construction of auditory objects is
a hierarchical processing where the spectro-temporal features are grouped based on local
structure before collected into a stream using the longer spatial and/or temporal scales,
however, the truth is more complex. The higher-order features and the top-down attention
can alter how objects are formed on the local scale, which implies that the auditory objects
are formed through heterarchical interactions [Shinn-Cunningham, 2008].
The use of prior information about the spectro-temporal structure of natural sounds to
group together acoustic energy into auditory objects is an important part of solving the
cocktail party problem. However, even when auditory objects and stream formation takes
place accurately, the listener still face the challenge of selecting the proper object for analy-
sis. This problem of auditory object selection is critical in the context of the cocktail party
situation because it is impossible to process all auditory objects in a complex auditory
scene due to a limited neural processing capacity [Shinn-Cunningham, 2008]. Hence, in
real life situation, the listener will focus selective attention on one source for detailed anal-
ysis and suppress other competing sources [Fritz et al., 2007] [Plack, 2014]. This implies
that the process of selective auditory attention is the second key to solving the cocktail
party problem [Shinn-Cunningham, 2008] [Shinn-Cunningham and Best, 2008]. Selective
attention is not specic to the auditory modality. Research has suggested that inputs form
dierent sensory modalities can bind together to form an object which comprises informa-
tion from dierent sensory modalities. An example of such a cross modal binding is the
combination of an auditory object with visual inputs such as gestigulations and lip move-
ment. Such a cross modality object might increase speech intelligiblity [Shamma et al.,
2010]. It has been shown that if a listener is presented with that sound 'ba-ba' while the
lips of a speaker are silently mouthing 'ga-ga' then the cross modal binding while do that
the listener hears the utterance 'da-da' [Plack, 2014].
In vision, attention is described as a biased competition between the neural representations
of perceptual objects [Desimone and Duncan, 1995]. The biased competition view argues
that an interplay between the inherent salience of the objects in the visual scene (exoge-
nously guided attention) and the inuence of volitional top-down attention (endogenouly
guided attention) by the listener is important in determining the visual object that is to
be the focus of attention. It is a biased competition because the top-down attention favor
one object over the other from a perceptual prespective and it takes place ubiquitously and
automatically when there are multiple objects in a scene [Desimone and Duncan, 1995].
This implies that the perceptual unit of attention in vision is the visual object and that
only one visual object is attended to at one time. Evidence from physiological studies on
auditory processing has suggested that the same principles is applicable in the auditory
2.4 Listening in a Competing-Speaker Environment
==PAGE:39==
24
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
modality [Chait et al., 2010], [Mesgarani and Chang, 2012]. Hence, the biased competition
model from vision can be applied to explain the auditory modality [Shinn-Cunningham,
2008].
As mentioned, humans have the ability to selectively guide attention to one auditory object
in a mixture by applying a top-down control mechanism to dierent acoustic dimensions.
This top-down control inuences the formation of auditory objects. Likewise, it is possi-
ble for many bottom-up factors to aect the inherent salience of an auditory object and
thereby draw the attention to the object even though the selective attention in the rst
place was on another object. The bottom-up factors include unexpectedness (a sudden
door slam) and uniqueness (the listeners name). These features (or statistics) of the ob-
ject make it stand out from the other objects in the complex scene [Kaya and Elhilali,
2014]. The salience of auditory objects can cause attention to suddenly go from being
driven voluntarily to being stimulus-driven. The salience of an object is postulated to be
learned through experience. However, it is hard to measure the true impact of bottom-up
salience since this phenomenon is strongly correlated with top-down factors [Shuai and
Elhilali, 2014]. These correlations between formation and selection implies that these two
processes are heterartical. It is unlikely that there is one particular site in the auditory
pathway where the object emerges but more likely that the object emerges gradually and
imperfectly as one traverses up the auditory pathway. If this is true then it makes perfect
sense that past and current studies have been unable to pinpoint exactly what neural pro-
cessing stages support object formation. The gradual formation of objects is supported by
the weak eects of attention on the representation in the midbrain [Varghesen et al., 2015]
to the strong eects in cortex [Choi et al., 2013]. The selective attentional also seems to
happen at every processing stage [Shinn-Cunningham, 2008].
When humans attempt to get a perceptual understanding of a complex scene there exist
many dierent forms of perceptual interference. The simplest form of perceptual interfer-
ence is energetic masking, which occurs when competing sources overlap spectro-temporally
with the target source, making the target imperceptible. The energetic masking is a dis-
ruption of the target and it occurs when the non-target stimuli activates the cochlea at
approximately the same location and at the same time as the target. However, since speech
and other natural sounds are relatively spectro-temporally sparse there will be time-points
in which it is possible to capture a clean glimpse of the target source (these glimpses dis-
appear if there are too many source, too strong reverberation or a continuous unstructured
noise presented in the auditory scene) [Warren, 1970]. If these clean glimpses of the target
are available then humans have the ability to perceptually ll in the inaudible portions of
the target stream. This phenomenon is called phonemic restoration [Warren, 1970]. Phone-
mic restoration was rst shown by Warren in 1970 where he showed that when speech is
interpreted by silent gap the speech intelligibility suers. In contrast, if the gaps were lled
with noise the speech intelligibility increased compared to the silent gap case. This implies
that the human brain does the phonemic restoration based on top-down knowledge and
hence this ability will be inuenced by cognitive, lexical, linguistic and semantic skills of
the listener [Warren, 1970]. Due to phonemic restoration, energetic masking is rarely the
perceptually inference that lead to a decrease in speech intelligibility. This fact lead to the
introduction of the term informational masking. Informational masking is a theory that
covers all perceptually masking in an auditory scene which is beyond energetic masking.
The concept of informational masking had lacked mechanistic explanantions until [Shinn-
Cunningham, 2008] argued that failure of object-based attention could explain why this
phenomenon occurs. To understand Shinn-Cunningham's argumentation, it is important
to remember that only one auditory object is analysed in detail at any given time and
2.4 Listening in a Competing-Speaker Environment
==PAGE:40==
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
25
the remaining objects are in the perceptual background. The fact that humans only are
able to attend to one stream is counter-intuitive since humans in general are able to un-
derstand multiple talkers in a cocktail party setting. An explaination could be that we
shift attention between speakers in order to obtain glimpse of each speaker. These glimpse
can be used to make sense of each speaker based on phonemic restoration. However, in
order to do this reconstruction of sentences, humans would need to rely on short-term
sensory memory in order to help with phonemic restoration. Since, a mental replay of the
clean glimpse is required to regain focus on the given sentence. Typically, the switching
of attention takes about 100200 msec and is associated with a cost related to switching
attention especially in complex auditory scenes [Cherry, 1953] [Best et al., 2008] [Larson
and Lee, 2013]. The cost is primarily associated with the time it takes to disengage and
reengage attention. Another cost factor i that auditory streams is build-up over time. This
build-up comes from the listener's ability to improve their focus on the perceptual fore-
ground by honing the attentional lter more nely when they maintain focus on a single
stream [Best et al., 2008], [Bressler et al., 2014]. Hence, after an attentional switch the
listener needs time to hone the attentional lter optimally and thus information may be
lost during honing-period. However, the short-term sensory memory degrades with time
and hence there will be a trade-o between the time between switches and short-term
memory capabilities [Shinn-Cunningham, 2008].
Let us return to why the theory of object-based attention can be used to explain informa-
tional masking. The informational masking generally happens if there is similarity between
target and maskers (denoted as stimulus similarity) and/or if there is some kind of random-
ness in the masker and/or target (denoted as stimulus uncertainty) [Shinn-Cunningham,
2008]. In respect to stimulus similarity, it is possible that this phenomena can aect the
formation of objects since similarities can group the maskers and target to be parts of the
same larger perceptual object. Furthermore, stimulus similarity can aect the selection of
the correct auditory object even when the markers and target are perceptually segregated
into distinct objects. Stimuli uncertainty mostly interferes with object selection either be-
cause the listener is uncertain on how to direct top-down attention or because the saliences
of new events (e.g. randomly varying maskers) draw exogenous attention. This implies
that even though stimulus similarity and uncertainty inuence the perception of a complex
scene it seems that they do so by interfering with the processes that underly object-based
auditory attention. In line with this the object-based auditory attention can fail during
all three phases; the short-term object formation, the streaming formation and selective
attention. The short-term object formation fails whenever the local structures are insuf-
cient to separate the sources in the scene from one another. The insucient separation
can come from energetically masking. Moreover, the short-term object formation can fail
if the target are not structured enough to support the formation of an auditory object. An
example could be that the target contains ambiguous or conicting cues. The failure in
binding the short-term objects into a stream occurs if the multiple sources in the auditory
scene have similar higher-order features. The failure at the streaming level results in a
target stream that is corrupted by sound elements from the maskers. This might interfere
with perception since the combination of target and masker streams might exchange key
elements in the target stream with unimportant features from the masker streams [Shinn-
Cunningham, 2008]. Lastly, it is possible that attention lead to failures of perception since
only one stream can be in the perceptual foreground at any time. The failure of attention
is consistent with the biased competition model in which the volitional top-down attention
can be overruled if the saliency of a suddenly appearing object is strong enough. This
implies that object selection is a probabilistic competition which depends on interactions
2.4 Listening in a Competing-Speaker Environment
==PAGE:41==
26
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
between top-down and bottom-up biases. Alternatively, the object selection can fail if
the listener does not know what feature to attend to or if the features of the target and
maskers are not suciently distinct to ensure proper target selection [Shinn-Cunningham,
2008].
Both object formation and object selection require a high-delity spectro-temporally
feature-representation of the sounds in complex auditory scene. Thus, it can be a real
challenge for hearing-impaired listeners to communicate in a complex acoustic scene [Shinn-
Cunningham and Best, 2008]. Thus, to help hearing impaired listeners struggling at the
cocktail party, it is essential to understand (in more detail) how sensory and central fac-
tors interact to extract a target object in a cocktail party scenario because such knowl-
edge might inspire more ecient and generalizable solutions than current engineering ap-
proaches [Cooke et al., 2010] [Akram et al., 2015]. The solution would not only ameliorate
their ability to communicate in an everyday setting but also improve their quality of life.
2.5
Neural Mechanisms Supporting Object Formation and
Attention
Currently, multiple hypotheses and models try to understand and explain the neural un-
derpinnings of auditory object formation. One of the rst hypotheses was the population
separation hypothesis, which suggests that sound elements segregate into separate streams
whenever they activate well-separated populations of auditory neurons. These neural po-
lutation can be selective to frequency, hence streams are seperated if they do not overlap in
frequency (or other sound attributes) [Shamma et al., 2010]. Later, it was shown by [Elhi-
lali et al., 2009] that sounds can bind into one perceptual objects even if they excite distinct
neural populations. Elhilali et al investigated the interactions between top-down selective
attention and bottom-up saliency in a behavioral neurophysiological study. Their results
showed that when a listener attends to a target stream the attention enforces top-down
selectivity on the neural activity to be more correlated with the attended stream com-
pared to the unattended stream because both power and phase coherence are enhanced
exclusively at the frequency of the target rhythm. They also showed that an increase in
acoustic saliency of the target object would increase power and phase correlation of the
neural signal, which implies that bottom-up processes also inuence the perceptual de-
tectability. Thus, the enhancement showed that there exists a tight coupling between the
bottom-up salience and the higher-level cognitive representation of auditory objects. Their
results also demonstated the importance of the temporal coherence in mediating auditory
object formation and that the top-down attention allows the neural representation of the
target signal to grow over time even though the sensory target signal is unchanged, thereby
increasing detectability performance of the listener with time. Lastly, Elhilali et al showed
that the location of the modulated neural representation is consistent with core auditory
cortex and that there was an increased synchronization between distinct underlying neural
populations. These ndings suggest that the neuronal mechanisms of core auditory cortex
is a contributor in analysing auditory scenes. The data, they obtained, undermined the
attention models based on neural amplication, instead they argued that a new model
should be based on temporally coherent or locally synchronous neural activity. Elhilali
et al suggested that the voluntary attention, that build-up the perceptual streams, might
involve top-down projection neurons. The hypothesis is that these neurons are able to
alter the response proles of the cortical neurons by their receptive eld plasticity leading
to a shift in the state of cortical circuits. The new state of cortical circuits alters the ring
2.5 Neural Mechanisms Supporting Object Formation and Attention
==PAGE:42==
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
27
patterns so that they has a temporal coherence with the attended stimuli. Secondly, they
suggest that the neural mechanism behind the increased temporal coherence may arise
from general sharpening of temporal tuning of the neural populations. This temporal co-
herence theory was later substantiated by nding of [Teki et al., 2013]. Teki et al showed
that temporal coherence activity within a group of frequency channels caused these chan-
nels to bind together while uncorrelated channels were suppressed.
Before [Elhilali et al., 2009], it was also hypothesised that streams are formed automat-
ically and pre-attentively at some stage between the cochlea and the primary auditory
cortex [Shamma et al., 2010]. However, [Shamma et al., 2010] showed that the fundamen-
tal aspect of auditory stream formations was the temporal coherence of responses of neural
populations in the auditory cortex that are selective to various sound attributes (like fre-
quency, timbre, pitch and spatial location). Shamma et al postulated that there exists
a temporal-coherence analysis stage in the brain that computes the correlations among
the outputs of the dierent feature-selective neurons. They found that the dynamics of
stimulus-induced uctuations in spike rates in the auditory cortex were slow, below 20 Hz.
Based on this Shamma et al hypothesised that the correlations are computed in the brain
over a relatively long time windows ranging in duration between 50 and 500 ms. This range
is consistent with the sound-presentation rates over which the formation of streams usually
occurs and it is also consistent with syllabic rate and the tempo in music. Shamma et al
also hypothesised that when attention is focused on a particular sound feature (such as
timbre) then all other sound features that were induced by the same sound source will be
grouped together into one perceptual stream based on the temporal coherence between the
cortical responses all of the features introduced. This hypothesis is based on the reasoning
that temporally coherent sound features are generated by the same source. If this hypoth-
esis is true then it might be that the brain unconsciously extract all auditory object in the
auditory scene and that attention is used to compensate for a limited cortical processing
capacity by only allowing the attended stream to be perceived consciously and hence only
allowing a fully analysis of this object. Lastly, Shamma et al proposed that attention is
crucial in stream formation because they showed that top-down attention can force the
auditory system to start the binding process and modulate the neural representations of
the acoustic features and/or the temporal coherence patterns among these features. This
Temporal Coherence Theory (TCT) works as long as one distinctive feature of the target
stream is suciently salient to be attended to by the listener. An example is that the
pitch of a female speaker typically will be salient if the competing speaker is male. The
TCT also supports the elegant synergy between object-based and feature-based attention
in stream formation, which was introduced by [Shinn-Cunningham, 2008], see Sec. 2.4.
The TCT has received support from both psychoacoustic and functional Magnetic Reso-
nance Imaging (fMRI) studies.
As mentioned, the TCT depends on a neural coincidence mechanism to detect the coher-
ence of dierent channels and exploit them when they occur. This would imply a higher
temporal resolution than fMRI can give. In the search for evidence of a neural coincidence
measuring mechanism, [O'Sullivan et al., 2015] did a study that used EEG to investigate
the neural computation of temporal coherence.
The high temporal resolution of EEG
did that they could study the inherently dynamic neural process in the human cortex.
However, this was at a cost of poor spatial resolution because the electrodes recorded dis-
entangling temporally overlapping responses from multiple simultaneously active neural
sources [O'Sullivan et al., 2015]. In order to address this problem, they applied a linear
regression method to extract localized neural correlates of individual features of interest.
O'Sullivan et al (2015) did both active and passive listening conditions and they found
2.5 Neural Mechanisms Supporting Object Formation and Attention

The gure is from [Akram et al., 2015].
an early eect of coherence during passive listening, which lasted from approximately 115
msec to 185 msec post-stimulus. Similar results were found in the active listening condition
where the onset was the same while the responses were larger and longer (up to around 265
msec). These results provide evidence for early and pre-attentive neural computations of
temporal coherence, which can be enhanced by top-down attention of the auditory object.
Moreover, it was shown that there were no signicant dierences between the topographies
of the responses from the active and passive listening conditions, which suggest that neural
mechanisms performing these two kinds of computations are the same.
The fact, that top-down attention up-regulates activities, makes the TCT seems like a log-
ical theory when trying to explain the mechanism of how human can attend to an auditory
object. However, the theory does not say anything about how the object is represented in
a neural population. In order for the TCT to work, the theory requires that the neural
code separates the attended object from competing objects. In regards to separation of
the neural representations of dierent objects, it has been shown that neural oscillations
are the key [Gross et al., 2015]. It was shown by [Ding and Simon, 2012b] that slow os-
cillations in the brain entrain to the syllabic structure of attended sound. In more detail,
Ding and Simon attempted to quantify a relationship between ongoing MEG recording
(the neural encoding of speech) and the continuous speech stimulus. They showed that
the neural encoding is based on the slow temporal modulations of speech (below 8 Hz)
in a broad spectral region (between 400 and 2,000 Hz). In general, the envelope of the
sound is important for speech perception while the ne structure of the sound is critical for
music perception [Heng et al., 2011]. Furthermore, Ding and Simon suggested that neural
encoding of speech is encoded in the neural activity of auditory cortex by a phase-locked
temporal code that seems to be comparable with the slow modulations of speech (below
10 Hz) that convey syllabic and phrasal informations [Ding and Simon, 2012b]. These
modulations are important because they make it possible to separate competing stream,
see Fig. 2.10 for an illustration. Lastly, Ding and Simon suggested that these modula-
tions can be used to gate faster acoustic uctuations of speech (such as spectral cues and
pitch cues) that contain phonetic and prosodic information. Additionally, they showed
that the activity was substantially stronger for the attended stream when the stimuli was
presented dichotically. This eect was denoted as attentional gain. It was also shown
that the neural coding of slow temporal modulations was more pronounced in the right
hemisphere regardless of the direction of the speech stimulus, which indicates that the
right hemisphere is intrinsically dominant in processing the slow modulations in speech.
Even though, there was an intrinsic right hemisphere dominance in dichotic listening the
eect of attentional gain was more salient in the hemisphere contralateral to the attended
2.5 Neural Mechanisms Supporting Object Formation and Attention
==PAGE:44==
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
29
side. This hemispheric lateralization eect is probably due to an anatomical asymmetry
between the left and right aerent pathways to each hemisphere [Porth, 2015]. Similar
results were shown in an EcOG study by [Mesgarani and Chang, 2012]. They showed
using the method of stimulus reconstruction that the reconstructed spectrogram was more
correlated with the spectrogram of the target speaker than the non-target speaker (this
is a commonly used method [Bialek et al., 1991] [Mesgarani et al., 2009] [Pasley et al.,
2012]). Mesgarani and Chang also demonstrated that the human auditory system restores
the representation of the attended speaker while suppressing irrelevant competing speech
and that the speech restoration occurs at a level where responses of the neural populations
still show precise phase-locking to spectro-temporal features of speech. This suggest that
the cortical representation does not reect the external acoustic environment but instead
reects the perceptual aspects relevant for the intended goal of the listener. Lastly, Mes-
garani and Chang showed that the neurons in the posterior superior and middle temporal
gyri were most robust in regards to encoding the attended speech.
Further evidence that the slow oscillations seem to gate the information ow by enhancing
and suppressing the sensory stream in the auditory scene was provided by [Lakatos et al.,
2013]. Lakatos et al searched for the physiological mechanisms underlying auditory se-
lective attention in monkeys and they found that attending to rhythmic auditory streams
entrains ongoing neural oscillatory activity at the level of primary auditory cortex, A1.
These neural oscillations reect the rhythmic excitability uctuations in the neuronal en-
sembles of the A1. Additionally, Lakatos et al showed that the temporal structure of the
attended stream was reected in the neural oscillation but that the phase depended on the
attended frequency content. The ignored stream was not tuned to dierent frequencies.
They also showed that neuronal ensembles, that processes the attended frequency content,
are entrained with their high excitability phases whereas neuronal ensembles tuned to
other frequency content (two octaves higher or lower) are entrained with opposite phase.
The phase dierences make it possible to enhance the neuronal ensembles tuned the at-
tended frequency content while suppressing the neuronal ensembles tuned to unattended
frequency content. This will result in amplication, sharpening and stabilization of the
attended sensory information. This implies that the mechanistic role of delta and theta
oscillations in A1 are to enhance and stabilize the attended auditory stream at key points
in time and at key frequencies. Furthermore, it suggest that the enhancement and stabi-
lization of the attended stream are accomplished by the existence of a spectro-temporal
lter mechanism, which models and predicts both temporal and spectral properties of the
attended streams by means of phase and frequency adjustment. The mechanism incorpo-
rates information about both the timing and frequency content of the relevant auditory
stream and by these means enhances the neural representation of attended stimuli along
these two feature dimensions. This spectro-temporal lter provides a mechanism to segre-
gate the attended stream from both temporally and spectrally overlapping streams since
temporally overlapping stream can be separated based on suppression of ignored frequency
content. Likewise, spectrally overlapping stream can be segregated based on the fact that
neuronal activity at non-attended time points will be suppressed.
Similar results was shown by [Golumbic et al., 2013] who supported the claim that low-
frequency speech tracking, phase coherence, serves a role in gating and constraining the
transfer of sensory responses (represented by high gamma power) to higher-order regions
according to attentional demands or processing capacity. Golumbic et al showed (using
EcoG recordings in surgical epilepsy patients) that the neural activity dynamically tracks
speech streams using both the phase of low-frequency neural activity (17 Hz) and the
high gamma power activity (70150 Hz). However, their data suggested that speech track-
2.5 Neural Mechanisms Supporting Object Formation and Attention
==PAGE:45==
30
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
ing in these two bands reects distinct neuronal mechanisms for auditory speech encoding
because they diered in both the course of response time and topographic distribution
over the cortex.
Golumbic et al showed that the low-frequency phase tracking signi-
cantly tracks both speakers (at least in the case of two simultanously speakers), however,
if attention was applied to one stream then there was a bias towards enhancing the at-
tended stream.
The site in which this happened was denoted as the modulation sites.
The modulation sites were located in and near STG, i.e., low-level auditory cortices. In
contrast, the selection sites that ignores unattended stream were shown to have a wider
spatial distribution, which included both low-level auditory areas as well as higher-order
language processing and attentional control regions (such as inferior frontal cortex, ante-
rior and inferior temporal cortex and inferior parietal lobule). Golumbic et al postulated
that these results were a selective representation of the input as it is transmitted from
sensory regions to higher-order regions. This postulation is supported by the fact that the
amplitude-modulated sites has a relatively constant magnitude in the response to the at-
tended stimulus throughout the epoch [Ding and Simon, 2012b]. Meanwhile, it was shown
that as the sentence unfolds the speech tracking of the attended talker in the selective
sites is further rene, indicating that the representation of the attended stimulus in these
regions is dynamically sharpened using accumulated spectrotemporal regularities.
One
think that should be noted about the experiment by Golumbic et al is that listener had
a contribution from a visual input, hence it is important to test that the same results is
true if the visual cue is omitted.
The above discussion shows that cortical oscillations seem to be likely candidates for seg-
mentation and coding of continuous speech because speech eects the auditory cortex by
entraining the phase of low-frequency oscillations (the the delta and theta bands) and
the amplitude of high-frequency oscillations (the gamma band). The reason is that these
bands match the rhythms of continuous speech and that continuous speech is organized
into a nested hierarchy of quasi-rhythmic components (prosody, syllables, phonemes) with
dierent time scales [Giraud and Poeppel, 2012]. It was shown by [Gross et al., 2015]
that this alignment between neural oscillations (in MEG) and speech rhythms is more
precise when a listener focuses on intelligible rather than unintelligible speech. Moreover,
they reported evidence of a nested hierarchy of neural oscillations in the delta, theta and
gamma bands that match the frequency of relevant linguistic components in continuous
speech, which might explain why these cross-frequency interactions are stronger for intelli-
gible than for unintelligible speech. Gross et al also showed that the neural oscillations at
dierent frequencies had a pronounced asymmetries between left and right hemispheres,
even though, all types of oscillations were present in both hemispheres. In the right hemi-
sphere, the delta and theta phase-locking was stronger, whereas the strength of the gamma
amplitude tracking was lateralised to the left. In regards to the phase-locking of auditory
theta oscillations Gross et al showed that edges in speech increased the alignment of these
oscillations to the speech envelope. In addition, they showed that following a speech edge
the coupling between delta, theta, and gamma oscillations increased within the auditory
cortex and that it happened before their coupling to the speech envelope. Hence, the phase
resets of cortical oscillations due to temporal alignment with the speech edges can extract
windows of high neural excitability in order to optimise the processing of important speech
events. This leads to the conclusion that segmentation and coding of speech in the human
brain relies on a nested hierarchy of entrained cortical oscillations that partly is controlled
by a top-down attention.
For completness, it should be mentioned that even though selective attention causes por-
tions of the brain to entrain to the attended input stimulus, it will also lead to other
2.5 Neural Mechanisms Supporting Object Formation and Attention
==PAGE:46==
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
31
oscillatory eects like alpha ringing. Alpha rigning is not caused by phase-locking and
hence these oscillations reect a dierent response of neuronal structures [Pfurtscheller
and da Silva, 1999]. The alpha ringing is an increase of power in the alpha band (8-12
Hz) that is assumed to be due to an increase in synchronization of the underlying neural
populations. This is why this type of response is also denoted as Event-Related Synchro-
nization, ERS [Pfurtscheller, 1992]. The increased synchronization is thought to reect
changes in the state of neural regions that encode and process inputs. Thus, these changes
can lead to a change in enhancement of the attended sensory information or suppression of
unattended sensory information. The reason that the ERS are frequency band specic is
that synchronization of the neuronal network can oscillate at dierent frequencies. When
the neuronal populations display resonant behavior to stimulus (e.g. a sound) they tend
to recruit neurons in the larger cortical areas, which lead to low frequency oscillations in
the EEG [Pfurtscheller and da Silva, 1999]. It has been suggested that alpha rigning can
be used as a feature in extraction of the attended speaker [Jung et al., 2000]. However, it
was shown by [Horton et al., 2014] that envelope responses were far better indicators of
attention than changes in alpha band power.
2.6
Auditory Attention Decoding Algorithms: State of the
Art
In this section we will look into the mathematical approaches that have been used to es-
timate selective auditory attention and we will mainly focus on speech segregation. The
section is structured around methods based on EEG. However, in some cases these ap-
proaches have utilized models from MEG or ECoG and in such cases the MEG or ECoG
model will be described shortly.
In Sec. 2.4, it was mentioned that the syllabic temporal structure of a speech stream en-
trains cortical oscillations in the low frequency bands and that attention modulates these
cortical oscillations to follow the syllabic temporal structure of the attended stream stonger
than the unattended stream in a complex auditory scenario. This implies that if the am-
plitude envelopes of the streams are uncorrelated, then it would be possible to extract the
attended stream from MEG or EEG responses. It has been shown that when a listener
selectively attends to one of two speakers then the attentional modulation eects will be
similar in both EEG [Power et al., 2012] and MEG [Ding and Simon, 2012a].
[Ding and Simon, 2012a] showed in a MEG experiment that the attended stream can be
extracted from a speech mixture even if the attended stream is spectro-temporally over-
lapping with the other streams (hence not resolvable at the auditory periphery). Ding
and Simon argued that the extraction was possible because the two streams are individ-
ually encoded in auditory cortex and hence the attended stream can be extracted based
on top-down attention and bottom-up neural adaptation. More importantly, they demon-
strated that using an envelope decoder (both at the single-subject and single-trial level)
the reconstructed envelopes depended strongly on the attentional focus of the listener and
that the reconstructed envelope resembled the envelope of the attended speech in 92 %
of the trials. It has since been demonstrated that the attentional modulation of cortical
responses is similary strong in EEG and hence it is possible to decode the attended stream
of a listener in a mixture of melodies [Choi et al., 2013] or speech streams [O'Sullivan et al.,
2014].
[Power et al., 2012] used the the idea from [Ding and Simon, 2012a] to generate the the
auditory evoked spread spectrum analysis (AESPA) method. The AESPA response (also
2.6 Auditory Attention Decoding Algorithms: State of the Art
==PAGE:47==
32
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
known as the temporal response function (TRF)) is an estimate of the impulse-response
function of the brain, which maps the amplitude envelope of the speech, s(t), on to the
neural activity recorded by EEG, x(t) [Akram et al., 2015]. Power et al found the AESPA
response by during a least-squares t of the response model
x(t) = TRF ∗s(t) + ϵ(t)
(2.7)
where ϵ(t) is a Gaussian noise. Hence, the AESPA response is the 'lter' that the brains
applies to transform the auditory input onto neural activity that are recordable with EEG.
In [Power et al., 2012], the amplitude envelope of the speech signal was resampled to the
sample reate of the EEG data before it was low-pass ltered with a cut-o frequency of 20
Hz. The argument for the lowpass ltering was that speech intelligibility depends mostly
on the envelope frequencies between 2 and 16 Hz [Power et al., 2012]. Lastly, the envelope
was transformed by taking the logarithm with base 10 (because the intensity processing
varies logarithmicly with stimulus intensity) and normalized such that each sample had a
value between -1 and 1. To compare the model prediction with the EEG signal, the EEG
signal was lowpass ltered with a cut-o frequency of 20 Hz due to the assumption that
there exist a linear relationship between EEG and stimulus envelope. The application of
the AESPA model showed a clear dierence in the AESPA responses to attended versus
unattended speech streams. Hence, the model seems to be a potential method for segre-
gation sound in a real time application. However, Power et al did not mention whether or
not they used single trial and hence it is assumed that they used a mean of responses (since
their results show the mean responses). The fact that the AESPA method is insensitive to
neural activities that are not correlated to the speech envelope might actually be a good
thing because it implies that the essential feature of speech segregation is identied. A
simplication that might cause problems is the linear feedforward relationship between the
stimulus envelope and the EEG response. This is clearly unrealistic since the brain consists
of neural networks, which create non-linear relationships by dierent feedback mechanism.
Nevertheless, from a mathematical modelling point of view, it was ground-breaking that
this type of forward modelling worked.
Alternatively to the forward modelling, one might look into a backwards model like
the one proposed by [O'Sullivan et al., 2014]. Their model is a stimulus-reconstruction
method (a regression method) that maps the neural activity back to the stimulus. The
method was inspired by similar method in ECoG [Mesgarani and Chang, 2012] [Golumbic
et al., 2013] [Pasley et al., 2012]. The stimulus-reconstruction in ECoG had shown to
be exquisitely sensitive to selective attention in a multispeaker environment [Mesgarani
and Chang, 2012] [Golumbic et al., 2013] (e.g [Mesgarani and Chang, 2012] showed that
the reconstructed speech sprectrograms (from the ECoG responses in a two-speaker sce-
nario) were dominated by the salient spectral and temporal features of the attended
speaker). [O'Sullivan et al., 2014] used the approach from ECoG to estimate the envelope
of speech from EEG. The method by O'Sullivan et al was oine and it started o by ob-
taining the amplitude envelopes of the speech signals by Hilbert transforms. Subsequently,
the speech envelopes were low-pass ltered below 8 Hz because it had been shown that
speech envelope frequencies between 2 and 8 Hz are linearly relatable to the EEG [Pasley
et al., 2012] [Golumbic et al., 2013]. It might seem surprising that the potential eects
of using the high gamma band (75-150 Hz) is not utilized in EEG [Zion-Golumbic and
Schroeder, 2012]. However, this is due to the fact that the skull and intervening tissue
(between the signal generation parts of the brain and the electrodes on the scalp) cause a
low-pass ltering (and attenuation) of the brain signals [Hassanien and Azar, 2015]. The
next step of the approach by O'Sullivan et al was to do the stimulus-reconstruction. Let
2.6 Auditory Attention Decoding Algorithms: State of the Art
==PAGE:48==
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
33
X(t, n) denote the recorded neural data from a single trial at a given (discrete) time point,
t, in a given electrode, n, then the regression model is
ˆs(t) =
N
X
n=1
τmax
X
τ=1
d(τ, n)X(t −τ, n)
(2.8)
where ˆs(t) is the estimated speech envelope,d is a linear reconstruction model and τmax
is the length of the linear reconstruction model in samples. Notice, that the estimated
stimulus is found as the sum over all channels and that each term in this sum is a con-
volution between the reconstruction model for the given channel and the neural response
in the given channel. This implies that the decoder d essentially is a multivariate impulse
response. To nd the optimal d of Eq. 2.8, [O'Sullivan et al., 2014] minimized the sum
of squared errors. This LS-method was used to train 60 decoders (30 for attended stream
and 30 for the unattended stream), however, to have only two decoders, (one for attended
stream and one for the unattended stream) they did a simple average of the decoders from
the multiple trials under the given condition. They used the average over 29 trials in a
leave-one-out cross-validation to test the classication performance. The classication was
based on the Pearson's correlation coecient, r, which was found between the estimated
speech envelope and the actual attended speech envelope, rattended. Similarly, runattended
is the Pearson's correlation coecient between the estimate of speech envelope and the
actual unattended speech envelope. O'Sullivan et al argued that the classication could
be based on one of two criteria;
1. it is a target if rattended > runattended
2. it is non-target if runattended > rattended
The argument for these criteria was based on the fact that [Ding and Simon, 2012a]
had shown that attended and unattended speech envelopes can be extracted separately
from the neural activity. However, OSullivan et al showed best results by applying the
rattended > runattended criterion. They also experimented with a subject-specic decod-
ing and showed that there was a slightly (but signicantly) increase in performance of the
subject-specic approach (when applying it to the attended decoders). A possible explana-
tion for this is that the neural responses are slightly dierent among dierent subjects. The
subject-specic approach (when applying the attended decoders) had a mean classication
accuracy of 89 %, which is astonishing because they did not use any method to reject or
subtract muscle and blink artefacts. Furthermore, OSullivan et al showed a classication
accuracy of 82%89% in single-trial (1 min) EEG epoch, which is comparable to the 92%
that [Ding and Simon, 2012a] obtained. However, it should be mentioned that a direct
comparison is dicult since MEG and EEG are sensitive to dierent aspects of the elec-
tromagnetic activity of the brain [O'Sullivan et al., 2014]. It is, however, possible to state
that the backward model by [O'Sullivan et al., 2014] was better than the forward-model
proposed by [Power et al., 2012]. The reason, that these two model is directly comparable,
is that both groups used the exact same data in their analysis of their models. The high
performance of the model by O'Sullivan et al might come from the fact that the stimulus-
reconstruction method is able to enhance relative contribution from each electrode while
simultaneously suppressing their irrelevant information (in each time point). In regards to
classication OSullivan et al postulated that their model was able to decode from data as
short as 10 sec, which means that it close to an applicable real-time AAD. However, the
2.6 Auditory Attention Decoding Algorithms: State of the Art
==PAGE:49==
34
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
model might be improved by some kind of regularization.
In a later study by [Das et al., 2016], a sliding window implementation of the AAD al-
gorithm proposed by [O'Sullivan et al., 2014] was implemented to improve the speech
intelligibility obtained by their hearing aid in a cocktail party scenario with two speakers.
The implementation of the AAD made it possible to make a neuro-steered hearing aid
using EEG signals to adaptively steer a Multichannel Wiener Filter (MWF) based beam
former. The MWF is a acoustic noise reduction algorithm. The algorithm uses an array
of microphones to exploit spatial characteristics of the acoustic scenario, which makes it
possible to extract the sound from a target direction while simultaneously suppressing the
background noise from other directions. The MWF algorithm is based on the fact that
speech signals contain many pauses that are statistically uncorrelated between dierent
speakers. The silent gaps in the target stream makes it possible to extract the statistics
of the noise background by applying a Voice Activity Detection (VAD) mechanism, which
can identify the time segments during which the target speaker is silent. However, to ap-
ply this algorithm, the target speakers must be identied. The identication might in the
future be accomplished by an AAD algorithm. Although [Das et al., 2016] showed that the
target-speaker can be extracted by the AAD algorithm there were a couple of setbacks.
Firstly, the window length had a large impact on the AAD accuracy, where large win-
dow length improved the AAD performance which is problematic because it decrease the
response time of the neuro-sheered hearing aid. Secondly, they assumed that the speech
envelopes of the two sources were known. Hence, to apply the AAD algorithm in a real
system, a method for extracting these envelopes is needed.
[Biesmans et al., 2016] did also applied the method by [O'Sullivan et al., 2014] as their
AAD, however, the decoders were trained slightly dierent to avoid the variance-bias trade-
o related to the choice of the regularization parameter. Biesmans et al showed that their
decoders were advantageous compared those of [O'Sullivan et al., 2014] and hence that
their training procedure was advantageous. After training the decoders [Biesmans et al.,
2016] applied the same decoding scheme as [O'Sullivan et al., 2014] (the one based on
Pearson's correlation coecient). Biesmans et al used this AAD to show that it is possible
to increase the performance of the AAD paradigm by using an auditory-inspired linear
lter bank followed by power law compression to extract speech envelopes. Surprisingly,
this model for envelope extraction was not outperformed by more detailed models of the
auditory periphery such as those proposed by [Yang et al., 1992], [Zilany et al., 2009]
and [Meddis et al., 2013] (the later can not be applied in real-time). Actually, this was a
good thing because the lter-bank and power law compression are computationally cheap
and thus applicable for implementation in future neuro-steered hearing aids. The evnelope
extraction (that showed best results) starts of by a linear lter bank. The linear ltering
is inspired by the basilar membrane in the cochlea, which splits the auditory signal into
frequency subbands. A widely-used model for this behaviour of the periphery auditory
system is to apply a gammatone lter bank, see Sec. 2.2.3, to the audio signal y(t). In
their study Biesmans et al used a gammatone lter bank that consisted of 15 perceptu-
ally uniform gammatone lters with an ERB = 1.5 and had center frequencies ranging
from 150 Hz to 4 kHz (the upper limit was chosen to be equal to the bandwidth of the
audio signal). After the audio signal had been partitioned into these 15 signals, ys(t),
the envelope of these subband-signals were found using a power law compression |ys(t)|β,
where β = 0.6. This power law compression models the compressive response behaviour
of the lters around their characteristic frequency, see Sec. 2.1.3. These signals were then
lowpass ltered to obtain the subband-envelop-signals. The method so far has resulted in
a matrix s(t) ∈RT,15 of subband envelopes. However, the AAD procedure requires ex-
2.6 Auditory Attention Decoding Algorithms: State of the Art
==PAGE:50==
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
35
actly one envelope, hence, some recombination of these subband envelopes is necessary. In
their work Biesmans et al only considered a weighted linear combination, s(t) = wT s(t),
where w ∈R15 is a vector of envelope weights. The question was how to nd the envelope
weights. They came up with three options, either they could apply equal weights, or they
could choose to assign the weights according to the respective band importance from the
calculation of the speech intelligibility index or lastly they could use a mathematical opti-
mization procedure. Surprisingly, their results showed that the classication performance
was not signicantly dierent in any of the mentioned envelope-weightings and hence ei-
ther the uniform weighting or the BI weighting is preferable in a real application based on
a computational complexity consideration. After the envelop signal was obtained it was
bandpass ltered between 2 and 9 Hz. Similarly, the EEG recording was ltered between
2 and 9 Hz. These lterings were followed by a downsampling where both the EEG and
speech envelopes were downsampled to 20 Hz. In their study, all lters were FIR lters.
The results of their study were a decoding accuracy of 87.5 % on trials of 60 seconds and
of around 77.0 % for 20 seconds.
As shown by [Biesmans et al., 2016], it might be advantageous to look for biological-
inspired models. Another example of such model was given by [Kaya and Elhilali, 2014].
The model by Kaya and Elhilali attempts to model bottom-up auditory attention by ex-
tracting salient auditory event from sounds. The predictive part of their model consists of
Kalman lters (and hence their model is essentially a state space model). The extact model
will not be described because it models the bottom-up attention whereas this section is
on top-down attention. Anyhow, their model predicted a high likelihood for saliency when
there was a salient event present (in the situation where a salient event was to be detected
in speech the accuracy of the model was 92%), which suggest that attentional decoding
algorithm might be improved if they are based on biological inspired predictive coding.
This postulation is supported by [Bastos et al., 2012] who argued that predictive coding
is the most biologically plausible mechanism for making these inferences because the state
spaces are capable of modelling the complex neurocircuit. This is why the model in Sec.
4.2 is a state space model.
All of the above AAD alogrithms have some common limitations. Firstly, the achievable
temporal resolution of these algorithms is of the order of minutes, which is too slow to
detect the dynamic switch of attention by the listener in a cocktail-party-like scenario.
Hence, an appropriate decoder needs to have a dynamic estimation framework with high
temporal resolution. Secondly (most of) decoders rely on ad-hoc assumptions and simpli-
cations, which in turn overshadow a reliable statistical interpretation of the data. These
limitations were coped by [Akram et al., 2015], who introduced a biophysically-inspired
state-space model that tracks the temporal dynamics of the attentional state of the lis-
teners using MEG observations and the speech envelopes of the two competing speakers.
The model consisted of three stages: the rst was the forward model, which applied sparse
TRFs to map the neural activity on to two speech envelopes (one for each speech streams).
The TRFs were trained in a pilot study. The second stage describes the dynamics in the
state space, which in this case is the attentional state of the listener. They modelled the
state space by a non-stationary Bernoulli process that assumes that the cognitive state of
attention is a continuous process in time (which makes sense from the discussion in Sec.
2.5). The nal step was the inverse problem of decoding the attentional state based on
neural activity and speech envelopes. This inverse model was made robust by employing
von Mises-Fisher circular statistics, see Sec. 3.1.1. This distribution made it possible to
infer the state-space parameters by applying a MAP estimation. The MAP estimate was
obtained by applying a nested EM algorithms in order to increase eciency [Akram et al.,
2.6 Auditory Attention Decoding Algorithms: State of the Art
==PAGE:51==
36
CHAPTER 2.
CLINICAL BACKGROUND
2015], see Sec. 3.5. Akram et al showed in a simulation that their state-space model had a
desirable decoding performance as long as the SNR was above -15 dB. Using actual MEG
data, they showed that if one used a multiple-trial analysis, the decoder is able to capture
attentional switches. However, their results showed that the switching time found by the
decoder on individual trials was not consistent across dierent trials and hence their model
failed to detect the exact switching time. The reason might be that there was an actual
advanced or delayed of the attentional switch by the listener. Akram et al also showed
that the behavioural results were signicantly improved when during the multi-trial analy-
sis compared to the single-trial analysis. However, this was expected from the formulation
of the state space model since the solution of the model has an inverse proportionality be-
tween the variance of the state variable and the number of trials. In summary, their results
showed that their state-sapce model was able to decode attention with high accuracy at
every time point and hence can predict attention on the order of seconds, which is a clear
improvement over the state-of-the-art temporal resolution of minutes. Moreover, this tem-
poral resolution is comparable to the empirically estimated speed of attention switching in
humans [Akram et al., 2015]. Another advantage is the ability to make condence bounds
on the estimated attentional state (due to its probabilistic nature). All of this suggest that
the model by Akram et al (2015) is a potential application for being an AAD in real time,
however, to apply this model as a real world AAD one would need to test that the model
can be applied on EEG.

Let X and Y be univariate discrete random variables (a discrete random variable is dened

and let {xi}i=1..M and {yj}j=1..L be the discrete sample spaces of X and Y , respectively.

avoid ambiguity) and hence in the following p(X = xi, Y = yj) will be substituted by
p(X, Y ).

Due to

probability distribution over X [Bishop, 2006]. Another important probabilistic rule is the
product rule of probabilities [Madsen, 2008]
p(X, Y ) = p(X ∩Y ) = p(Y |X)p(X) = p(X|Y )p(Y )

where ∩denotes set intersection and p(Y |X) is the conditional probability. The conditional
probability is the probability of observing Y if X is known [Bishop, 2006] (notice that the
conditional probability is undened if P(Y ) = 0 [Bertsekas and Tsitsiklis, 2002]). From
the product rule it is clear that if two random variables X and Y are independent, hence
p(Y |X) = p(Y ) and P(X|Y ) = P(X) then P(X, Y ) = P(X|Y )P(Y ) = P(X)P(Y ). The
denition of independent random variables implies that independent random variables
are uncorrelated. However, even if two random variables are uncorrelated they are not
necessarily independent, hence independency is stronger than uncorrelated [Madsen, 2008].
The product rule can be used to obtain Bayes' theorem (also known as Bayes' rule) [Bishop,

Bayesian statistics (statistics that apply Bates' theorem to generate generative models),
p(Y ) is called the prior probability. The reason is that prior to an observation of X, p(Y )
is the only available information to infer on Y . Similarly, p(Y |X) is called the posterior
probability because it can be used to infer on Y posteriorly to an observation of the vari-
able X. Lastly, p(X|Y ) is called the likelihood function (of the random variable Y ) since
it describes the likelihood of the random variable X given the random variable Y [Bishop,
2006].
Another case of random variables are the continuous random variables. The theory on
continuous random variables is analogue to the one used to describe discrete random
variables. The main dierence is that continuous random variables are described by prob-
ability density functions whereas discrete random variables are described by probability
mass functions. Let p(x) be the probability density over the continuous random variable
x. Given the probability density function of x it is possible to nd the probability that x
is in the open interval (a, b). This probability is dened as

probability density function [Bishop, 2006] [Bertsekas and Tsitsiklis, 2002]. The fact that
a discrete random variable has probabilities in discrete point does that p(x) is denoted as
the probability mass function in the discrete case [Bishop, 2006].

If we now return to the Bayesian statistic then the probability density function is often
governed by a set of parameters Ω. Let us assume that all assumptions about the parame-
ters (prior to any observation of data) can be captured in the prior p(Ω) and let D be the
observed data set (that are to be used to infer the parameters). Lastly, yet the D-driven
evidence to which parameters are optimal be captured in the likelihood function p(D|Ω).
Notice, that the likelihood function not necessarily is a probability density function over
the parameter set Ω, since
R
p(D|Ω)dΩmight not be equal to one. If both the prior and
likelihood are given then the inference of the parameters given data can be obtained by
Bayes' theorem
p(Ω|D) =
p(D|Ω)p(Ω)
R

Hence, Bayes' theorem implies that the posterior distribution is proportional to the prod-
uct of the prior and the likelihood. However, a big critic of the Bayesian paradigm is that
the selection of prior distribution, p(Ω), often is done based on mathematical convenience
rather than actual belief, see Sec. 3.1.1. The alternative to using the Bayesian statistic
is to obtained the parameters with the frequentist paradigm. The frequentist paradigm
obtains the parameters by maximization the likelihood function, p(D|Ω), which is why
the frequentist procedure is known as maximum likelihood [Madsen, 2008]. The maximum
3.1 Probability Theory
==PAGE:55==

CHAPTER 3.
MATHEMATICAL BACKGROUND
likelihood yields similar results to the Bayesian paradigm if the prior is 'at' in the neigh-
bourhood of the peak in the likelihood function, however, if this is not the case the two
paradigm lead to dierent results [Bishop, 2006]. In machine learning, it is common to
convert the problem of the maximum likelihood into the minimization of an error function
and it common that the error function is similar to the negative log-likelihood [Bishop,
2006] (for more information on maximum likelihood see Sec. 3.2).
The above discussion implies that the probabilistic model must have an inference state in
which a training set D is used to learn the model parameters, Ω. The model can then
be used to make a subsequent decision in a decision stage.
Hence, the application of
probabilistic model (or generative models) requires two distinct stages. The decision stage
requires some statistical tools design to do making decision. These tools are gradually
introduced through out this section.
Alternative to the generative models (that have their inference and decision state seper-
ated) other schemes use discriminant functions to combine the inference and decision
stages.
A discriminant function is a mapping from the input x onto the target t and
the mapping is obtained based on a training set. The main dierence between discrimi-
nant functions and probabilistic models is that discriminate functions require a training
set {xn, tn} where both input variables and their corresponding targets are given, hence
it is supervised learning [Bishop, 2006]. An example of a discriminate function is given in
Sec. 3.2. In contract, probabilistic models are based on unsupervised learning and hence
tries to infer the probability density function based on {xn} without knownlegde of the
(true) underlying density function [Bishop, 2006]. The advantages of the discriminate func-
tion is that the complexity of the model is much lower than for the probabilistic models,
however, the probabilistic models allows the to inference of the marginal density over the
input space, p(D), which might be used to do outlier detection (novelty detection) [Bishop,
2006].
As mentioned, generative models need some tool to make decision. If it is assumed that
only one distribution is inferred in the inference stage and that the most likely point un-
der the probability distribution equals the best decision then the probabilistic concept of
expectation is needed. Expectations (or weighted averages) is one of the most essential
concept in probabilities [Bishop, 2006]. If E[f] denotes the expectation of f(x) then E[f]
can be interpreted as the average of the function f(x) over the probability density function
p(x) [Bishop, 2006]. The mathematical denition of expectation is [Madsen, 2008] [Bert-
sekas and Tsitsiklis, 2002]
E[f] =

3.1.1
Probability Density Distributions
Probability distributions are important for modelling prior and posterior distributions. In
general, probabilistic models are constructed by choosing a suitable set of distributions in
order to model the observed phenomena and then inferring the unknown parameters of
the distribution using a nite set of observations of the random variables (for an example
see Sec. 4.2). The process of inferring the parameters is known as density estimation and
it is based on unsupervised learning since the true density is unknown [Bishop, 2006]. In
the following four parametric probability distributions are presented.
Bernoulli Processes
Let x ∈{0, 1} be a binary random variable and let the probability p(x = 1|µ) = µ
where 0 ≤µ ≤1. Since the variable is binary then it must apply that if x ̸= 1 then
3.1 Probability Theory
==PAGE:58==

43
x = 0 and furthermore it must apply that p(x = 0|µ) = 1 −p(x = 1|µ) = 1 −µ. The
probability mass distribution over such a discrete random variable is called the a Bernoulli
distribution [Bishop, 2006] and it takes the form
Bern(x|µ) = µx(1 −µ)1−x
(3.17)
Bern(x|µ) is clearly a probability distribution since Bern(x|µ) ≥0 for x = 0, 1 and
P

mean and variance of the Bernoulli distribution is µ and µ (1 −µ), respectively. If D is an
independent and identically distributed (i.i.d.) data set of size N where all samples are
drawn from a Bernoulli distribution then

classical coin ip experiment with a coin that has µ = 0.5. The coin is ip three times,
N = 3, and by chance it lands on head all three times, m = 3 (it is assumed that x = 1
corresponds to the coin landing on head). Based on Eq. 3.18 µML = 1 which is clearly a
overt since the true µ = 0.5. To avoid the overtting a Bayesian treatment of the problem
will be developed (the smaller the data set the larger probability of overtting) [Bishop,
2006]. To developed the Bayesian treatment it is important to realize that p(D|µ) can be
rewritten as the binomial distribution. The binomial distribution gives that probability of
observing m given N and µ and hence xing m and N the maximum likelihood solution
for µ can be found. The binomial distribution is dened as

subsets that can be generated from a given N-element set [Bertsekas and Tsitsiklis, 2002].
An real example is that the binomial coecient describes how many dierent committees of
k persons that you can generate if you can choose between N dierent peoples [Bertsekas
and Tsitsiklis, 2002].
Since the binomial distribution is the likelihood of the i.i.d set
of Bernoulli samples, the mean and variance is the sum over the individual means and
3.1 Probability Theory
==PAGE:59==

variances, respectively [Madsen, 2008].
To achieve a Bayesian treatment a prior distribution over the parameter µ is needed. For
mathematical convenience, it is common to choose the conjugated prior to the likelihood
function. The conjugated prior simplies the Bayesian analysis by making the posterior
distributions having the same functional form as the prior [Bishop, 2006]. In order to
obtain conjugacy to the Bernoulli distribution the prior must be the beta distribution
Beta(µ|a, b) = Γ(a + b)
Γ(a)Γ(b)µa−1(1 −µ)b−1
(3.20)
where Γ(x) is the gamma function dened by
Γ(x) =

(3.21)
The posterior distribution of µ can now be obtained by Bayes' Theorem where the conju-

This implies that
p (µ|m, l, a, b) ∝µm+a−1 (1 −µ)N−m+b−1
(3.22)
it is a proportionality because all terms independent of µ are omitted. It is clear that
the posterior distribution has the same functional dependence on µ as the conjugated
prior which is due to the conjugacy properties of the prior with respect to the binomial
distribution. This implies that the posterior distribution also is a beta function and hence
it takes the form
p (µ|m, l, a, b) =
Γ(N + a + b)
Γ(m + a)Γ(N −m + b)µm+a−1 (1 −µ)N−m+b−1
= Beta(µ|a + m, b + N −m)
(3.23)
Notice that the prior increases the number of observation x = 1 and x = 0 by a and b
respectively. Hence, the hyperparameters in the conjugated prior can be seen as a ctitious
prior observations of x = 1 and x = 0. Notice, that these ctitious prior observations are
not restricted to be integers.
Fig. 3.1 shows the Bayesian treatment of the three coin-ips experimented where the prior
is chosen so that it assumes that the coin has equal chance of landing on either heads or
tails. It can be seen that the introduction of the conjugated prior changed the optimal
estimate of µ from 1 to 0.8. The new best estimate is called the maximum a posterior
(MAP) estimate because it is the maximum of the posterior distribution, p (µ|m, l, a, b).
Figure 3.1: The left part of the gure shows the likelihood. From this likelihood it is clear that the maximum
likelihood solution, µML = 1. However, by introducing the conjugated prior in the middle. The MAP
solution is µMAP = 0.8 (indicated with a dashed green line on the right plot). Notice, that the prior
(the middle plot) is chosen so that it weights both possible outcomes equally.

Multinomial Variables
The Bernoulli process can be used to describe a process that has two possible mutually
exclusive stages. However, in this report more than two stages will be needed. In the fol-
lowing, a mass probability function for I possible mutually exclusive states are introduced.
Let x be an I-dimensional vector in which one element xi = 1 and all other elements are 0.
This is known as the 1-of-I scheme and it is a particularly convenient representation when
describing I possible mutually exclusive states [Bishop, 2006]. An example is that if I = 3
and the state is the second state then x = [0, 1, 0]T . Notice that with this representation
PI

Now, let each of the I states be assigned a probability, µi, hence µi is the probability
that xi = 1.
The fact that µi represent probabilities lead to the following constrains
ui ≥0 ∀i ∈{1, 2, ..., I} and P

ties, {µi}, are stored in the vector µ = [µ1, ..., µI]T then the mass probability distribution
function that governs x given µ is
p(x|µ) =

Eq.

process can either be modelled by a Bernoulli process or by the 1-of-2 scheme with the
generalization of the Bernoulli distribution [Bishop, 2006].
The Gaussian Distribution
The Gaussian (or normal) density distribution is a widely used distribution of continuous
random variables and it is commonly applied in both the eld of statistic and decision
theory [Madsen, 2008]. In the case of decision theory, it is often used as to model uncer-
tainty [Wickens, 2002]. The Gaussian distribution of a D-dimensional random variable x
is dened as
N (x|µ, Σ) =
1
(2π)D/2 |Σ|1/2 e−1
2 (x−µ)Σ−1(x−µ)T
(3.25)
where µ is the D-dimensional mean vector and Σ is the D × D covariance matrix. Each
diagonal element σ2
i of covariance matrix, Σ, is the variance of the Gaussian distribution in
the ith dimension. Sometimes the inverse of the variance are used β =
1
σ2 . This quantity is
called the precision. Lastly, it should be mentioned that |Σ| denotes the determinant of the
covariance matrix, Σ. It can be shown that the mode is coincided with the mean [Bishop,
2006].
Let us consider the one-dimensional Gaussian to show an important feature of the Gaussian
distribution. If x is a Gaussian random variable with mean µ and variance σ2 then it can
be 'standardised' into a new random variable z given by
z = x −µ
σ
(3.26)
The new random variable z is normally distributed with mean E[z] = E[x]−µ
σ
= 0 and
variance V ar[z] = V ar[x]
σ2
= 1. The Gaussian distribution with zero mean and unit variance
3.1 Probability Theory
==PAGE:61==
46
CHAPTER 3.
MATHEMATICAL BACKGROUND
is called a standardized Gaussian and it is so important that it denoted as φ(x), hence
φ(x) =
1
√
2πe−x2

which does that the CDF of the standardized Gaussian is
Φ(z) =

−∞
φ(x)dx
The importance of Φ(x) comes from the fact that many statistical calculations requires
the evaluation of the area under the Gaussian distribution, which only can be obtained by
numerical computations. Hence, by using the transform in Eq. 3.26 all one-dimensioanl
Guassian can be transformed into the standarized Gaussian governed by φ(z) and the area
under this distribution can be found by Φ(z). Another important statistical quantity is
the inverse of Φ(z). In this report, Z(p) := Φ−1(p). Notice, that this function takes a
probability p and converts it to a standard score, z, hence it can be used to nd the value
of z for which p% of the density of φ(z) is below [Wickens, 2002] [Bertsekas and Tsitsiklis,
2002].
One reason, that Gaussian distributions are commonly applied in practice, is the central
limit theorem. The central limit theorem states that under certain mild condition the
distribution of a sum of a set of random variables will become increasing Gaussian as the
number of random variables, N, in the sum increases [Wickens, 2002]. In practice, the
convergence to a Gaussian as N increases is very rapid [Bishop, 2006]. Hence, Gaussian
distributions can be used to model noises in a variety of engineering, physical, and statis-
tical contexts because these noises often are additive eects of many independent random
factors [Bertsekas and Tsitsiklis, 2002]. Empirically, the Gaussian approximation of noise
signals in engineering has been found to be true, which is convincingly explained by the
central limit theorem [Bertsekas and Tsitsiklis, 2002].
If we return to the D-dimensional Gaussian distribution N(x|µ, Σ), then it can be shown
that this distribution is well dened if and only if the covariance, Σ, is positive denite,
hence if all its eigenvalues λi is strictly positive [Bishop, 2006]. The constrain that Σ
must be positive denite ensure a proper normalization of the multidimensional Gaussian
distribution because a positive denite matrix has a positive determinant [Bishop, 2006].
Gaussian random variables have some important properties. One of which is that a lin-
ear combination of Gaussian random variables is a Gaussian random variable [Wickens,
2002]. Another set of important property is that if two sets of random variables are jointly
Gaussian then both marginal distributions are Gaussian and furthemore the conditional
distribution of one set on the other will also be a Gaussian [Bishop, 2006] [Bertsekas and
Tsitsiklis, 2002], the second set of important properties is illustrated in Fig. 3.2. Fig. 3.2
indicates that the mean of p(xa|xb) is a linear function of the xb, which can be shown to be
correct for an arbitrary dimensionality. Furthermore, it can be shown that the covariance
of the conditional distribution, p(xa|xb), is independent of xb. This is an example of a
linear-Gaussian model [Bishop, 2006].
So far, the parameter µ and Σ has been assumed known. It is possible to infer these (based
on i.i.d. data) as the maximum likelihood solution. However, this will lead to an systemat-
ically underestimated of the covariance matrix (compared to the true covariance matrix).
Instead of using the frequencist approach that leads biased estimates of the covariance it
is possible to develop a Bayesian inference for the parameters of the Gaussian distribution
by introducing priors over the parameters µ and Σ. In the following it is assumed that
the mean vector, µ, is known and hence the problem is to infer the Σ from a set of N i.i.d.

The major limitation of the Gaussian distribution is that it is intrinsically unimodal and
hence it can not be used to approximate multimodal distributions. However, if the un-
derlying distribution is multimodal a mixture of Gaussians can be used. The mixture of
Guassians utilizes the concept of latent variable and hence it is a probabilistic state space
model (for more information on state space modelling see Sec. 3.4). In the case of mixture
of Gaussians the latent variables are the discrete set of mixing coecients (the latent vari-
ables can also be continuous as in the case of probablistic PCA). In this report the mixture
of Guassians will not be utilized, however, a mixture of von Mises-Fisher distributions will
be (see Sec. 4.2.3).
Von Mises-Fisher distribution
Even though Gaussian distributions are widely used they can sometimes be inappropriate
as probability density models for continuous variables.
One practical condition where
Gaussian distributions are not applicable is if the random variables are periodic [Bishop,
2006]. To gain intuition on the subject of periodic random variable the discussion will
start of by the univariate variables.
Let θ ∈[0; 2π) denote an univariate periodic random variable measured in radians and let
D be the set {θn}n=1,...,N. The intuitive model over the periodic random variable would
be to choose some direction as the origin and then model the parameters as a standard
univariate Gaussian distribution. However, the maximum likelihood solution of parameters
govering the Gaussian would vary with the arbitrary choice of origin and thus the intuitive
model is not suitable. The problem with the Gaussian distribution is that it is non-periodic,

The von Mises distribution is the key model for modelling circular (and spherical) data
and it plays the same role in statistical inference on the circle as the Gaussian distribution
does on the line [Mardia and Jupp, 2000]. From Eq 3.30 it is clear that the distribution
is a 2π periodic unimodal distribution that is symmetric around θ0 and with the mode
located at θ = θ0 and that it has its antimode at θ = µ + π. In [Mardia and Jupp, 2000]
it is stated that the ratio of the density at the mode to the density at the antimode is
given as a function of κ, explicitly as e2κ. This ratio-relation implies that a large κ is a
results of data-clustering around the mode whereas a small κ is caused by dispersion of
the data. Notice that if κ = 0 then the von Mises distribution is the uniform distribution.
Furthermore, it can be shown that if the concentration coecient, κ, is large then the von
Mises distribution becomes approximately Gaussian [Bishop, 2006].
The von Mises distribution can be expanded to hyperspheres of arbitrary dimension, p
[Bishop, 2006]. If the (p-1)-dimensional hypersphere is denoted as Sp−1 then it was in
[Mardia and Jupp, 2000] shown that an appropriate generalization to the Sp−1 space
is the von Mises-Fisher distributions [Mardia and Jupp, 2000].
The von Mises-Fisher
distributions are given by [Mardia and Jupp, 2000] [Akram et al., 2015]
p(x|θ0, κ) =
κ
2
p/2−1
1

κp/2−1
(2π)p/2 Ip/2−1(κ)
eκθT
0 x
(3.33)
where κ(≥0) is the concentration parameter and θ0 is the mean direction that satises
||θ0|| = 1. Lastly, Ip/2−1 is the (p/2-1)-order modied Bessel function of the rst kind
(dened by Eq. 3.32). Notice that the von Mises-Fisher distribution is symmetric around
the mean direction θ0 and that its mode is located in this direction (under that assumed
that κ > 0). Furthermore, it can be shown that κ determines the clustering around the
mean direction so that a high value of κ leads to high clustering around the mean direction
θ0.
In the case where θ0 is known the conjugated prior for the concentration parameter, κ, to
the likelihood of the von Mises-Fisher distribution is [Akram et al., 2015]
p(κ) ∝κd(W/2−1)
