3.2
Linear Regression and Regularization
Previously, it was mentioned that the density estimation is unsupervised learning. This
type of learning is fundamental for the inference of probability distributions in state-space
model (see Sec. 4.2). However, the state-space model will also require TRFs, which are
obtained by a supervised learning procedure known as linear regression. In general the goal
of regression is to nd a highly generalizable mapping that maps from a D-dimensional
continuous input space onto the one-dimensional target space.
In the following xn is a variable from the input space and tn is the corresponding variable
in the target space. The assumption behind linear models for regression is that the target
value t is a sum of an additive noise, ϵ, and a deterministic function, y(x, w), where the
deterministic function is given by
y(x, w) = w0 +
M−1

ϕ(x) = x. With this choice of bias function vector the regression model is denoted as
linear regression because the model is a linear function of input variables. This implies
that if x0 := 1 then the model is

Eq. 3.40 is known as the ℓ2-regularized SSE and it can be seen that the ℓ2 regularization
encourages weight values to decay to zero unless it is supported by the data. Further-
more, the equation shows that the larger the regularization parameter the more support
parameters need from data if they not are to decay to zero. In order words, the larger
the regularization parameter the more inexible, or biased, is the obtain solution. Hence,
the choice of λ is a trade-o between the model exibility and the degree of tting to
noise. This trade-o is known as the bias-variance trade-o and it indicates that there
is an optimum for the regularization parameter that does that the model is exible but
without overtting too much [Bishop, 2006].
It is from Eq. 3.40 clear that the MAP solution for the parameters w is a closed form
solution. The MAP-solution is given by [Bishop, 2006]

3.3
Stochastic Processes
In the following, the concept of autoregressive (AR) models/processes are introduced.
An AR models is discrete stochastic process. Before getting into modelling a physical
phenomenon as a stochastic process two concept are needed.
The rst is a denition
of stationarity in regards to stochastic processes. In this project a stationary process is
dened as stationary if and only if both the mean and the variance are constant (and
nite) [Madsen, 2008]. The second important concept is the second important concept
3.3 Stochastic Processes
==PAGE:68==
CHAPTER 3.
MATHEMATICAL BACKGROUND
53
regarding stochastic process is Markov process. The stochastic process, {Xn}, is a kth
order Markov process if
p(x(N)|x(N −1), ..., x(1)) = p(x(N)|x(N −1), ..., x(N −k))
(3.42)
where {x(n)} is the realization of the stochastic process (hence it is time series).
An
important Markov process is the rst order Markov process
p(x(N)|x(N −1), ..., x(1)) = p(x(N)|x(N −1))
(3.43)
In words, the rst order Markov process is characterized by the fact the dependency of the
realization of the stochastic process at time N, x(N), on all past realization, {x(n′)}n′<N,
are captured in time the realization x(N −1). An important feature of the rst order
Markov process is that only the most recent observation, x(N −1), is needed to infer on
x(N).
An important class of Markov processes is the AR process. The general denition of an
AR process is that an AR process of order p, denoted as AR(p), is dened as
x(n) + ϕ1x(n −1) + ... + ϕpx(n −p) = ϕ(B)x(n) = ϵ(n)
(3.44)
where {ϵ(n)} is a white noise process and ϕ(B) = Pp
k=0 ϕkBk where ϕ0 := 1 and B is the
one-step backward shift operator, hence Bx(n) = x(n −1) and Bix(n) = x(n −i). It is
clear that the AR(p) is a Markov process of order p. The reason that the process in Eq.
3.44 is called autoregressive is that x(n) is obtained by a regression of the past realization
of the random process {Xn} [Madsen, 2008].
To return to the Markov process of rst order, let us look at the AR(1) process. This
process is dened as
x(n) = ϕx(n −1) + ϵ(n)
where {ϵ(n)} is a white noise process with V ar[ϵ(n)] = σ2
ϵ . By successively application of
the denition (in order to substitute the past observation) yields
x(n) = ϵ(n) + ϕϵ(n −1) + ϕ2ϵ(n −2) + ... + ϕiϵ(n −i) + ...
This implies that
E[x(n)] =
∞
X
n=0
E[ϵ(n)] = 0
V ar[x(n)] = σ2
ϵ
 1 + ϕ2 + ϕ4 + ...


∞
X
n=0
ϕ2n = σ2

The last equality is true if and only if |ϕ2| = ϕ2 < 1 since this constrain ensures that the
innite sum converges to the given fraction [Christensen, 2012]. Notice that ϕ2 < |ϕ| if
|ϕ| < 1 and ϕ2 = |ϕ| if |ϕ| = 1 which implies that the last equality is true as long as
|ϕ| < 1. Similarly, it can be shown that if |ϕ| ≥1 then the variance is unbounded and
hence the process is non-stationary.
In the above stationary stochastic processes were discuss, however, natural phenomenons
are not always stationary. An example from the auditory system is that the attentional
state of a listener can be switched which implies that the process is non-stationary because
one can change the neural processing. In this project the stochastic processes that are
used to model non-stationarity are the integrated autoRegressive processes, denoted as
ARI(p,d) [Madsen, 2008]. An ARI(p,d) process is dened as
ϕ(B)∇dx(n) = ϵ(n)

==PAGE:69==

MATHEMATICAL BACKGROUND
where {ϵ(n)} is a white noise process and ∇dx(n) = (1 −B)dx(n) where B is the one-step
backwards shift operator. Hence, ∇is the so called dierence operator. An important
ARI process is the ARI(1,1) process
∇x(n) = ϵ(n) ⇔x(n) = x(n −1) + ϵt
(3.45)
which is known as random walk [Madsen, 2008]. Notice that the random walk is the AR(1)
with ϕ = −1 recall. The discussion on the AR(1) model implies that the IRA(1,1) has
zero mean and an unbounded variance, which is the reason that the random walk is non-
stationary.
In the case of AR(p) and ARI(p,d) processes, the optimal predictor is the conditional mean
(under the same assumption as used in Sec. 3.2) [Madsen, 2008]. Hence, the optimal one-
step prediction, xn|n−1, for an ARI(1,1) model is
xn|n−1 = E[x(n)|x(n −1)] = x(n −1)
(3.46)
3.4
State Space Modelling
State space models (also known as latent variable models) are commonly used in control
engineering and as a signal processing paradigm [Smith and Brown, 2003] [Akram et al.,
2015] [Bishop, 2006]. In control engineering the state space models is used to describe
the dynamics of a stochastic (dynamical) system [Hinrichsen and Pritchard, 2005]. The
state-space models describes the dynamics of the stochastic (dynamical) system by two
components [Akram et al., 2015]. These components can either be equations (as in the
case of the Kalman lter) or probabilistic models (as in the case of state-space model
in Sec. 4.2). The rst component is the observation model (also known as the forward
model) which relate the observable process to the unobserved process [Hinrichsen and
Pritchard, 2005]. The unobserved process is denoted as the state space process (or the
latent process) and the evolution of latent process through time is governed by a model
over the state dynamics. This second component of the state-space model is known as
the system equation (or system model) and it describes the dyanmics of the latent space
[Smith et al., 2004] [Madsen, 2008].
The two components of state-space model gives
rise to a probabilistic frame by which the state-space parameters can be inferred based
on data [Hinrichsen and Pritchard, 2005].
Furthermore, the state space model can be
used to make prediction, do reconstruction and design control mechanism [Hinrichsen and
Pritchard, 2005] [Madsen, 2008]. However, in this report the state-space model will only be
used to do predictions and reconstructions. Notice, that the state-space model (similarly
to the AR models) is used to model stochastic process, however, that the state-space model
does this by inferring an internal (unobserved) state of the dynamical system whereas the
AR model only deals with the dynamical system in the observable space [Madsen, 2008].
3.5
Expectation Maximization Algorithm
In Sec. 3.4 the concept of state space modelling was introduced. Let the nth observed
variable be denoted as xn and let the corresponding latent variable be denoted as zn.
Furthermore, let a set of i.i.d. observed variables be combined into the observation matrix,
X, in which the nth row contains xT
n. Similarly, let Z be the latent matrix with rows zT
n.
3.4 State Space Modelling
==PAGE:70==
CHAPTER 3.
MATHEMATICAL BACKGROUND
55
If the set of all model parameters is denoted as Ωand the joint probability is dened as
p(X, Z|Ω) then the summation rule of probability yields
p(X|Ω) =
X
Z
p(X, Z|Ω)
under that assumption that the latent variable is discrete (if the latent variables were
continuous then the sum should be replaced by an integral over Z). In general, the marginal
distribution over the observed variables, p(X|Ω), is more complex and hence less tractable
than the joint distribution, p(X, Z|Ω). The reason is that the input space is expanded to
contain both the observation space and the latent space in the joint distribution [Bishop,
2006]. However, even if the joint distribution is from the exponential family of probability
distribution the log likelihood over the observed variables does still not have a closed form
solution since
ln(p(X|Ω)) = ln
 X
Z
p(X, Z|Ω)
!
(3.47)
Notice that the summation is inside the (natural) logarithm and hence the (natural) log-
arithm cannot counteract the exponential function, which is why there is no closed form
solution. Another problem is that the unobserved state can not be observed directly from
the data. Hence, instead of having a complete data set {X, Z} only the incomplete data
set X is accessible through an experiment. The term incomplete data simply reference to
the fact that there exist two sample spaces but only the sample space that relates to the
observable variables can be sampled [Dempster et al., 1977]. The two samples spaces are
related by a many-one mapping from the latent space to the observable space [Dempster
et al., 1977]. This implies that the expected value of a latent variable z can be obtained
from an observed variable x.
The Expectation-Maximization (EM) algorithm was developed by [Dempster et al., 1977]
to obtain the maximum likelihood solution in a latent space model given the incomplete
data set. If it is assumed that the complete data set {X, Z} is given then the maximum
likelihood of the complete data set is obtained by maximizing
log (p(X, Z|Ω))
(3.48)
Let it be assumed that this maximization has a closed from solution. However, in practice
only the incomplete data set is given and hence to evaluate Eq. 3.48 an estimate of Z
must be obtained. This estimate can be inferred by take the expected value under the
posterior distribution for the latent variables, which is denoted as p (Z|X, Ω). This is a
part of the E-step in the EM algorithm. Notice that to estimate the latent variables the
current parameters values, Ωold, are needed. Hence,
EZ [Z|X, Ω] =
X
Z
Zp (Z|X, Ω)
This expectation is then used to obtain the expectation of the complete-data log likelihood
evaluated given another set of parameter values, Ω. The expectation of the complete-data
log likelihood is denoted as Q(Ω, Ωold) [Bishop, 2006] [Dempster et al., 1977] and it is
given by
Q(Ω, Ωold) =
X
Z
p(Z|X, Ωold) ln(p(X, Z|Ω))
= EZ [ln(p(X, Z|Ω)] = ln(p(X, E [Z] |Ω))
(3.49)
3.5 Expectation Maximization Algorithm
==PAGE:71==
56
CHAPTER 3.
MATHEMATICAL BACKGROUND
Furthermore, if zn is K-dimensional and X consists of N i.i.d. data points then
Q(Ω, Ωold) =
N
X
n=1
K
X
k=1
ln(p(xn, E [zn,k] |Ω))
(3.50)
The subsequent M-step is then to maximum Q(Ω, Ωold) with respect to Ω, which lead to
a new set of parameters
Ωnew = arg max
ΩQ(Ω, Ωold)
(3.51)
Recall that the maximum log-likelihood of the complete data set is assumed to be closed
form and hence it is possible to nd a closed form solution for the parameters Ωby
maximizing Q(Ω, Ωold). The new set of parameters Ωnew can then be used to re-estimate
the expectation of the latent variables, which then lead to a re-estimation of Q which
lead to yet another new parameter set Ωnew. It can be shown that each successive E and
M steps (each EM-cycle) lead to a parameter set Ωnew that either increases or at least
gives the same incomplete-data log-likelihood as the parameter set Ωold [Dempster et al.,
1977] and that the incomplete-data log-likelihood is the same if and only if the incomplete
data log-likelihood has reached a local maximum [Bishop, 2006]. Hence, the incomplete
data log-likelihood can be used as a stopping criteria.
To summarize the steps of the
EM-algorithm
1. Initialize the parameter set Ωold = Ω0 where Ω0 is an initial guess of state-space
parameters.
2. The E-step obtains the expectation of the complete-data log likelihood, Q(Ω, Ωold).
3. The M-step maximizes the expectation of the complete-data log likelihood
Ωnew = arg max
ΩQ(Ω, Ωold)
4. Check for convergence by evaluating either the change in complete or incomplete
log-likelihood or the change in parameters [Bishop, 2006] [Dempster et al., 1977]. If
the convergence criterion is not satised
Ωold ←Ωnew
and go to step 2 again. Do this until convergence or a threshold of iterations are
reached [Bishop, 2006].
It was mentioned in Sec.
3.1 that the maximum-likelihood models is biased.
In the
case of latent models these ML-biases can in some cases lead to singularities in the so-
lution [Bishop, 2006]. Hence, to avoid such singularities the MAP solutions should be
used [Bishop, 2006]. The EM-algorithm can also be used to nd MAP estimates for the
parameters in Ω. In the case of MAP-estimates, the E-step is the same as in the ML-
estimates, however, the quantity that the M-step maximizes is altered to
Q(Ω, Ωold) + ln(p(Ω))
where p(Ω) is the prior over the parameter set [Bishop, 2006]. Again, the EM-algorithm
will converge on a local (if not global) maximum on the incomplete-data log-posterior
[Dempster et al., 1977].

3.6
Principle Component Analysis and Data Whitening
Principle Component Analysis, PCA, is widely used for feature extraction, dimensionality
reduction, data visualization and data whitening [Bishop, 2006]. PCA can be dened in
multiple ways. The denition applied here is that PCA is the orthogonal projection of
data onto the principal subspace so that the variance of the projected data is maximized.
The principal subspace is a lower dimensional linear space and it is spaned by a set of the
orthogonal projections called principle components [Bishop, 2006]. It should be mentioned
that the principal space might have the same dimensionality as the data space, which is
the case when during a data whitening.
Let xn denote an Euclidean variable with dimensionality D and let the objective be to
project the data set, {xn}n=1,...,N, onto the principal subspace, which is characterised by
maximizing the variance of the projected data. The principal subspace has dimensionality
M(≤D) and for the moment it is assumed that M is known [Bishop, 2006].
Let us start of by nding the rst principal component. The rst principal component is an
projection onto a one-dimensional principal subspace (M = 1) [Bishop, 2006] constructed
so that the projection maximizes the variance of the projected data the most. Let the rst
principal component be denoted as u1. This component is a D-dimensional vector because
it maps a D dimensional space onto a one-dimensional space. Furthermore, since the rst
principal component is used as an projection it is the direction (and not the magnitude) of
the principal component that is of interest. Hence, without loss of generality, the principal
component is chosen to be an unit vector (uT
1 u1 = 1). With this denition it is clear
that each data point xn can be projected onto a scalar value, uT
1 xn. If we let x denote
the sample mean of the set {xn}n=1,...,N then mean of the projected data is uT
1 x and the
variance of the projected data is
1

N

n and apply the orthogonality constraints). Hence
λK+1 = uK+1ΣxxuK+1
which is maximized if uK+1 is chosen to be the eigenvector that corresponds to the largest
eigenvalue of Σxx that not yet has been used to obtain a principal component. This in-
ductive proof shows that if the principal subspace is M dimensional it will be spanned by
the M eigenvectors u1, ..., uM of the data covariance matrix Σxx corresponding to the M
largest eigenvalues λ1, ..., λM. This implies that the PCA order the mutually orthogonal
principal components by decreasing power [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008]. It was men-
tioned that M can be chosen to be equal to D, however, it would require the rank of Σxx
is full because it is the only way Σxx has D nonzero eigenvalue [Bishop, 2006]. If Σxx
has full rank and M = D then the PCA causes a rigid rotation of the coordinate axes to
align with principle components [Bishop, 2006]. This rigid rotation has some advantageous
eects that are used in data whiting.
The objective of data whiting is to transform a data set into its normalized version. The
normalized data has zero mean and unit covariance. Notice that the unit covariance implies
that the signals/components are uncorrelated [Särelä and Valpola, 2005]. Data whiting is
important for a successfully application of certain pattern recognition algorithms [Bishop,
2006]. Data whiting is also known as data sphering because the contour line of whitened
Guassian data will be a hyper-sphere. If M = D then the eigenvector equations can be
written in matrix form as
ΣxxU = UΛ
where Λ is a D× diagonal matrix with element λi and U is a DxD orthogonal matrix
with columns ui. With this notation the data whiting is accomplished by transforming
each data point, xn, by
bxn = Λ−1/2UT (xn −x)
The mean of {bxn} is zero because U lead to a rigid rotation of the coordinate system and
since Λ−1/2 simply causes a scaling of each of the projections. The scaling by λi of the ith
3.6 Principle Component Analysis and Data Whitening
==PAGE:74==
CHAPTER 3.

59
projection does that whitened data set {bxn} has a unit covariance matrix
1
N
N
X
n=1
bxnbxT
n = 1
N
N
X
n=1
Λ−1/2UT (xn −x) (xn −x)T UΛ−1/2
= Λ−1/2UT ΣxxUΛ−1/2 = Λ−1/2ΛΛ−1/2 = I
(3.53)
This implies that if a K-channel EEG is spatially whitened using this method it will result
in a dataset that has spherical symmetry and hence with no privileged direction of variance
in the whitened space [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008].
Often whitening is combined with dimensionality reduction of the data by selecting the
size, M, of principal subspace so that the principal subspace contains most of the power
from the original data space.
3.7
Spatial Filters based on a Denoising Source Separation
algorithm
It was shown by [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008] that the Denoising Source Separation
(DSS) algorithm can be used to reduces biological noise in ERP. Examples of biological
noises are heart activity, eye-blinks and task-irrelevant brain activities. The DSS algorithm
by [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008] synthesizes spatial lters that partition the recorded
activity into stimulus-related and stimulus-unrelated components. The partition is based
on a criterion of stimulus-evoked reproducibility. More specic, the algorithm discards
non-reproducible components by only projection reproducible component back onto the
original space. The selective back-projection leads to a clean data set [de Cheveigné and
Simon, 2008]. The spatial lters obtained by [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008]'s method
are dierent from the spatial lters obtained with an ICA. In general, spatial lters are
synthesized using a blind, or semi-blind, source separation method. The DSS algorithm
proposed by [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008] is a semi-blind source separation method
whereas ICA denotes a set of pure blind source separation methods that can be used to
obtain spatial lters [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008]. The advantages of the DSS algorithm
by [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008] over ICA algorithms is that this DSS algorithm requires
milder assumptions to partitioning the data because it 'only' tries to seperate data into
two sets of components whereas the ICA tries to obtain a complete analysis of all neural
sources [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008].
In all cases, the spatial lters are used to replace the recorded data by a set of linear
combinations. This implies that each output channel bxk′(t) will be a linear combination
of the input channels xk(t);
bxk′(t) =
K
X
k=1
akk′xk(t)
where t is time, K is the number of channels and akk′ is the mixing coecient between
input xk(t) and output bxk′(t). In vector notation it yields
bx(t) = Ax(t)
where x(t) = [x1(t), ..., xK(t)]T , bx(t) = [bx1(t), ..., bxK(t)]T , and A = [akk′]. In the following
A is denoted as the ltering matrix. The objective of the blind source separation algorithm
is to obtain the ltering matrix. In the case of the DSS proposed by [de Cheveigné and
3.7 Spatial Filters based on a Denoising Source Separation algorithm
==PAGE:75==
60
CHAPTER 3.
MATHEMATICAL BACKGROUND
Simon, 2008] the source separation is guided by the proportion of evoked power. This im-
plies that the objective for the DSS algorithm is to optimize A so that the evoked power is
preserved while noise power is suppressed. The main assumptions behind this DSS method
is that the brain activity is reproducible and that the mixing matrix between brain sources
and sensors is stationary [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008].
In advance to the application of the DSS algorithm by [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008]
all environmental and sensor noises (like power line and machinery noises) must have be
suppressed by other preprocessing techniques. The reason is that the DSS algorithm only
deals with the biological noises. In this thesis, temporal ltering was applied to deal with
environmental and sensor noises. This is dierent from the approach by [Akram et al.,
2015] since they applied the Time-Shift PCA (TSPCA) algorithm by [de Cheveigné and
Simon, 2007] to deal with environmental noise before the spectral ltering. The TSPCA
needs J orthogonally orientated reference channels that are so far from the brain that they
only measure the enviromental noise. In both [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2007] and [Akram
et al., 2015] they used J = 3. The TSPCA works by time-shifting the reference channels N
times and apply a PCA to these shifted signals, which causes the time shifted signals to be
orthogonal. The orthogonalization does that the TSPCA is a J · N dimensional ortonor-
mal basis for the environmental noise in the time-domain. The idea is then to project
the EEG data onto this TSPCA-plane to nd the enviromental noise in the EEG-data.
These projection can then be substracted from the EEG data to obtain 'cleaned' EEG
data [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2007]. Notice that this procedure does not involve ltering
of the EEG data and hence temporal distortions (like temporal smearing and rejection of
frequency information above cut-o frequency) are eliminated [de Cheveigné and Simon,
2007]. The discard of temporal ltering is the main advantageous of the TSPCA-plane,
however, since temporal ltering is needed in this project (based on Sec. 2.6) the main
advantageous of the TSPCA is withdrawn. A second reason for not using the TSPCA
is that it requires further analysis to conrm that no brain signals are embedded in the
reference channels. Lastly, the computational load is increased both due to additional
electrodes and computational resources needed to nd the TSPCA-plane.
The DSS algorithm that is applied in this project is based on the DSS algorithm by
[de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008], however, there is some minor alternations like the struc-
ture of the input signals and the way it deals with eye-artifacts. In [de Cheveigné and
Simon, 2008] it was stated that trials containing eye-artifacts and trials where channels
have a high power (measured as RMS2) should be discarded from the training set to
prevent eye-artifacts and 'power'-outliers from dominating the solution. However, since
our trials are long and we only have a few trials, this were not applicable in our setting.
Instead, high/low power channels is replaced by spherical interpolation (see Sec. 4.1.3).
Furthermore, to deal with eye-artifacts the ICA was used. It is however shown in App.
A that an eye-artifact subtraction prior to the DSS algorithm might be sucient do deal
with eye-artifacts.
If X denotes the temporal ltered EEG-data with the dimensions R (number of trials), K
(number of channels), and N (the number of sample in each trial) then DSS algorithm is
as follows
1. Concentrate data to Xcon, which has dimensional K × RN.
2. Check if that each channel of Xcon has zero mean. If a channel does not have zero
mean the sample mean (of the channel) is subtracted from the channel.
3. Each channel of Xcon is normalized by dividing each of them with its ℓ2-norm.
3.7 Spatial Filters based on a Denoising Source Separation algorithm
==PAGE:76==
CHAPTER 3.
MATHEMATICAL BACKGROUND
61
Actually, this initial normalization is not critical for the DSS algorithm but it ensures
that all channels have equal weight regardless of their initial gain [de Cheveigné and
Simon, 2008]. The resulting matrix is Xnorm.
4. Let the normalized data, Xnorm, undergo a dimensionality reduction and a data
whitened. The data whitening is accomplished by rotation the data by its principal
components (since the data has zero mean). The dimensional reduction is done by
discarding principal components with negligible power. The ad-hoc criterion used
to dene components with negligible power is (by default) that any component with
an eigenvalue smaller than 10−3 ·λ1 (where λ1 is the largest eigenvalue of covariance
matrix of Xnorm) is a negligible power component. The dimensional reduction is
applied to reduce computations and avoid numerical problems [de Cheveigné and
Simon, 2008]. The L remaining principal components are then used to generate a
rotation matrix R1 in which each row is one of the L principal components. The
whiting is then accomplished by the following mapping
Xwhite = Λ−1/2
1
R1Xnorm
where Λ1 is the principal eigenvalue matrix.
5. The Xwhite is epoched to R trials. The output is denoted as Xtrial,white.
6. This is the denoising step [Särelä and Valpola, 2005]. The denoising step sends the
epoched whitened data, Xtrial,white, through a bias function B. The bias function
can be constructed in numerous way [Särelä and Valpola, 2005]. However in this
report, the bias function is the same as used by [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008].
This bias function is designed to be guided by the proportion of evoked power in the
data. This guiding is accomplished by letting the bias function B be the proportion
of epoch-averaged evoked activity, hence
B [Xtrial,white] = 1
R
R
X
r=1
Xr,white
The assumption behind this is that stimulus-evoked activity is reinforced by averag-
ing while noise and task-irrelevant neural activities are reduced by such an averaging-
procedure.
7. The output of the bias function, B [Xtrial,white], is then submitted to a second PCA,
which is used to nd the principal components that maximizes the variance of the
bias function in the principal subspace. This leads to a second rotation matrix R2
that orders the components in terms of decreasing susceptibility to the bias.
8. The rotation matrix, R2, is applied to the non-epoched whitened data Xwhite which
yields
XBioNoiseReduced = R2Λ−1/2
1
R1Xnorm
This result in L time series where the noise is reduced due to the orientation of the
second principal subspace.
9. Next d of the L times series are assigned as the signal components and the remaining
L −d components are denoted as noise components. The noise components will not
be recovered by spatial lters. This discrimation is based on the assumption that
3.7 Spatial Filters based on a Denoising Source Separation algorithm
==PAGE:77==
62
CHAPTER 3.
MATHEMATICAL BACKGROUND
a principal component with low power is a noise signal.
The number of signal
components, d, can either be choosen directly or it can be determined indirectly
based on a threshold to the bias score. We will use the directly method and set
d = 1, which does that R2 is reduced to a L element row vector r2. r2 is the rst
principal component of the second rotation matrix and it lead to a signal component
that represents the most reproducibly stimulus-driven linear combination of EEG
channels [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008]. Notice, if d was dierent from one then
the spatial lter would lead to d signal components that are mutually orthogonal.
Furhthermore, these components would be order by decreasing evoked-to-total power
ratio [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008].
Hence the output of the DSS algorithm is the spatial lter Dspatial =
r2Λ−1/2
1
R1
min(norms),
where min (norms) is the minimum of the ℓ2-norms over channels from step 2. The
reason that the minimum of these norms are used is that the channel with smallest
norm should be taken into account.
Notice, that the spatial lter, Dspatial, obtained by our method, maps a K-channel-EEG
onto a one-dimensional signal component. The lter should be applied to each trial of the
original (zero-mean) temporal-ltered EEG-data X.
It was shown by [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008] that when dealing with stimulus-evoked
activity obtained with MEG around 80% of the noise power was removed by the denoising
algorithm and that the algorithm only gave minimal distortion of the evoked response.
They also showed that more than 50% of reproducible power could be obtained when
d = 1, which is the argument for choosing d = 1. Lastly, [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008]
showed that their DSS algorithm was competitive or even better than other biological noise
suppression methods (based on spatial lters) such as (specic) ICA methods.
The algorithm by [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008] is build on the DSS algorithm by [Särelä
and Valpola, 2005]. The exact intuition behind DSS algorithms (that Särelä and Valpola
gav) is quit mathematical. A less mathematical intuition (behind our DSS algorithm) is
that it starts of by whiting the data so that the data is rotational invariant (it can freely
rotate in any direction).
The bias function (the proportion of epoch-averaged evoked
activity) is then used to distort the spherical symmetry of the data so that the data no
longer is rotational invariant but instead has an increased variances in the directions with
larger epoch-averaged evoked activities (the directions with greater biases). The second
PCA then is used to nd these directions of greater biases. The direction with greatest
bias is found and it is utilized (together with the rst PCA) to map the EEG-data onto the
signal component. All in all this leads to a signal component that is the linear combination
of EEG channels that best maximizes the epoch-averaged evoked activity. Notice, that
the DSS algorithm enhances the component that is most consistent across trials while
suppressing all other components without any prior knowledge about the stimulus or the
timing of the task [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008].

==PAGE:78==

Method
4.1
Experimental Design
4.1.1
Procedures and Stimuli
In this project two experiments were conducted.
The rst experiment was implemented to obtain TRFs for three dierent speech segments,
which is why the experiment is denoted as the TRF experiment. The TRFs are the key
feature of the state-space model's forward model, see Sec. 4.2.1. The TRF experiment
consisted of 3 blocks. Each block was assigned with a 180 sec speech segment that was
repeated three times (three trials) with a break between trials. The length of the break
was self-paced as the subjects had to click the mouse to continue to the next trial. After
each block a comprehensive multiple choice test was given to the participants about the
passage that they heard. Participants were notied about the multiple-choice tests prior to
the experiment. The intention of the multiple-choice tests was to motivate the participants
to keep their attention on the speech streams.
All three speech segments were recordings of comedy shows by three native English come-
dians. The amount of laughter in the recordings were reduced as much as possible and all
periods of silence longer than 300 msec were found manually and shortened to 300 msec.
The shortening of silent periods keeps the speech streams owing continuously [Akram
et al., 2015]. Lastly, the speech streams were low-pass-ltered below 4 kHz.
The rst block contained a recording of male speaker, male speaker 1, the second block
contained a recording of a female speaker, female speaker 1, and the third block contained
a recording of second male speaker, male speaker 2, see Fig. 4.1.
Figure 4.1: This gure shows the experimental design for the TRF experiment. Male speaker 1 is blue, female
speaker 1 is red and male speaker 2 is green.
63
==PAGE:79==
64
CHAPTER 4.
METHOD
In the second experiment (the attentional switch experiment described below) each block
consisted of mixtures of speech streams. Hence, to help the subject attended to the target
stream the speech streams within each mixture were spatially separated by ITD. This had
impact on the TRF experiment because it was hypothesized (prior to the experiments)
that the classication accuracy of the auditory attention decoder would be highest if the
spatial properties of the speech streams were the same in both the TRF experiment and
the attentional switch experiment. In both experimental designs, the auditory spatializa-
tion was accomplished by utilizing ITDs. In the rst condition of the TRF experiment an
ITD of 600 µsec with a lead on the right ear was applied to make male speaker 1 appear
at the right ear. In the second condition an ITD of 600 µsec with a lead on the left ear
was applied so that female speaker 1 would be perceived as begin at the left ear. In the
last condition no ITD was applied, which made male speaker 2 appear in the medial plane
at zero elevation.
As mentioned, the second experiment was an attentional switch experiment. In this ex-
periment the participants were presented with two or three speech stream simultaneously
and the experiment had three conditions. In the rst two conditions a mixture of male
speaker 1 and female speaker 1 was presented to the subject and in the third condition a
mixture of all three speech streams (male speaker 1, female speaker 1 and male speaker 2)
was presented, see Fig. 4.2.
Figure 4.2: This gure shows the experimental design for the attentional switch experiment. Male speaker 1 is
blue, female speaker 1 is red and male speaker 2 is green.
The third condition was designed to extract a pilot data set to test an extension of the
state-space model by [Akram et al., 2015] and as it will be shown shortly some of the
speech streams in this part of the experiment were familiar to the subject, which might
lead to biases. The experiment was designed so that the rst condition was the rst block
of the experiment, the second condition was the second block and the third condition was
the third block. Each block consisted of three trials and the same speech mixture was pre-
sented in all trials of a block. Again, a break of self-paced length was introduced between
trials.
The speech segments used in the attention switch experiment was exactly the same speech
segments as used in the TRF-experiment (the use of the same speech segments are justi-
ed because no subjects participated in both experiments). However, instead of presenting
them individually they were combined into mixtures as described above. To recap, the
stimulus of the rst two blocks was a mixture of male speaker 1 and female speaker 1 and
in the third block, all three speech segments were combined into a mixture. The dierence
4.1 Experimental Design
==PAGE:80==
CHAPTER 4.
METHOD
65
between the rst and second block was the spatially separation of the speech streams. In
the rst block the spatial locations were identical to the one used in the TRF experiments,
hence, male speaker 1 was located on the right and the female speaker was located on
the left. Again, the spatially separation of the speech streams was accomplished by ITDs.
In the second condition, the spatial locations were swapped so that male speaker 1 was
located on the left and female speaker 1 was located on the right. The second condition is
denoted as the swap condition and it was used to test the hypothesis on spatial specicity
of the state-space model from Sec. 4.2. In the third condition male speaker 1 was located
on the right, female speaker 1 on the left, and male speaker 2 in the middle.
The task of the rst condition was to start by attending to either the female or male
speaker. Whether to start by attending the male or female speaker was visually cued at
the beginning of each trial and it was counterbalanced across participants. The counter-
balancing was introduced to reduce any speaker-related biases in the decoding algorithm
by equalizing speaker representations across subjects. Halfway through the trial, the par-
ticipant was visually cued to shift attention to the other speaker. In all trials of the rst
condition, the participant would start by attention to the same target stream. The task of
the second condition was nearly identical to the rst condition, however, this time the rst
attentional stream was determined by the rst attentional stream in rst condition. The
constraint was that if male speaker 1 was the rst attentional stream in the rst condition
then the participant must start of by attention to female speaker 1 in the second condition.
The reasoning behind this is that the unattended part of the speech segments is assumed
to be unfamiliar to the subject and hence the second block will (like the rst block) consist
of unfamiliar speech based on this constraint. After the two rst blocks of the experiment
all segments of the speech streams associated with male speaker 1 and female speaker 1
were familiar to the participant. Hence, in the third condition only the male speaker 2
was unfamiliar to the subject. This might lead to a bias in the AAD, however, since the
data from this part of the experiment was only used for a pilot study this possible bias
was accepted.
In the third condition the participant would always start o by attending to male speaker
1 located to the right. After one third of the trial the subject was cued to shift his/her
attention to female speaker 1 in the left. Lastly, two third into the trial, the participant
was cued to shift his/her attention to male speaker 2 located on the middle. The fact that
the order of attentional states was the same across subject might lead to speaker-related
biases, however, again since this third condition only was used to obtain data for a pilot
study this possible bias was accepted.
Beside from the visual cue, an auditory cue was presented as well. The auditory cues were
that all non-target streams were silenced for one second. Hence, a clean version of the
target stream was present for one second at the beginning of each trial and a clean version
of the stream that the participant should shift his/hers attention to was present for one
second after the onset of the visual cue. The visual and auditory cue was coherent, how-
ever, the visual cue had Gaussian random jitter with mean zero and standard deviation
100 msec so that the activity associated with visual cues was randomly distributed across
trials. This was done to randomize the neural synchronization associated with the visual
cue since this synchronization is a noise factor in this experiment. Furthermore, to help
facilitate the attentional switch the playback was silenced 2 sec prior to the attentional
shift to interrupt the current attentional state of the subject.
Similar to the TRF experiment, the attentional switch experiment used comprehensive
multiple-choice tests (after each block) to maintain the attention of the participants. The
multiple-choice questions were only related to the attended sections and were a subset of
4.1 Experimental Design
==PAGE:81==
66
CHAPTER 4.
METHOD
the questions used in TRF experiment.
All stimuli were diotically delivered at approximately 75 dB SPL using ER2-tube phones
plugged into the ear canals. The ER2s lead to a at frequency response at the tympanic
membrane. Furthermore, the ER2s have a 70 dB isolation between ears and reduce ex-
ternal noise by 30 dB www.etymotic.com.
The stimulus was resampled to 24,414 Hz,
quantized at 16-bit and presented via a TDT RP2.1 processor, which has a at frequency
response. The 16-bits was used due to buer-size limitations (it is common to use 24-bits).
EEG event values denoting auditory stimulus timing information were sent via the digi-
tal output port of the TDT. Since both the TDT and the ER2s have an approximately
at frequency response, the acoustic delivery system gives an approximately at transfer
function, which encompasses the range of the presently delivered stimulus [Akram et al.,
2015]. At the beginning of each block the psychtoolbox was used to present a description
the block. Furthermore, the psychtoolbox was utilized to generate the visual cues during
trials of the attentional switch experiment.
In the TRF experiment, the subjects had an average correct answer percentage of 81.67
% in the multiple choice test with a standard derivation of 3.50 %. However, analysis
showed that one of multiple choice questions was answered wrongly by all subjects (all
other questions were answered correctly by at least three of the six subject). If the bad
question is discarded the average correct answer percentage was 84.47 % with a standard
derivation of 3.62 %.
In the attentional switch experiment, the average correct answer percentage was 76.11 %
in the multiple choice test with a standard derivation of 16.78 %. The larger standard
deviation might be a result of a reducing in total number of questions presented to the
subjects (in the attentional switch experiment each subject was presented with a total of
18 questions whereas subjects in the TRF experiment were presented with a total of 30
questions). In the attentional switch experiment, one of the questions had three partici-
pants to answer correctly. The rest of the questions had more half of the participants to
answer correctly.
4.1.2
Participants
In the TRF experiment six clinically normal hearing subjected (4 females, 3 native En-
glish) were recorded. Subjects aged from 20 to 32 with median of 25. The sample size of
six subjects were arbitrarily picked. However, [Ding and Simon, 2012a] obtained eective
STRFs from MEG based on as low as three subjects. Recall that the STRFs are related
to the TRFs since a 'marginalization' over the spectral structures of a STRF leads to an
analogue to a TRF. However, notice that the method by which the STRFs were obtained
by [Ding and Simon, 2012a] was dierent from the method that is utilized to obtain the
TRFs in this experiment and hence a direct comparison is not possible.
In the attentional switch experiment ten clinically normal hearing subjected (8 females,
9 native English) were recorded. Subjects aged from 20 to 30 with median of 21. The
sample size was based on the eect sizes that [Akram et al., 2015] found.
Both experimental procedures were approved by the Boston University Charles River Cam-
pus Institutional Review Board and a written, informed consent was obtained for each
participant before the experiment was conducted. The participants were nancially com-
pensated for their participation. No subjects participated in both experiments.

4.1.3
Data Recording
The EEG data was recorded with a BioSemi ActiveTwo system that had 64 electrodes.
The electrodes were mounted on a BioSemi head cap in according to the 10/20 system, see
Fig. C.2. The signals at each electrode were recorded at a sampling rate of 2048 Hz. The
BioSemi ActiveTwo system has an online lowpass lter of 1/5 of the sampling rate (hence
410 Hz) to avoid aliasing. Besides from the 64-channels EEG channels 6 external electrodes
were recorded with the BioSemi ActiveTwo system. Two of the external electrodes were
placed on the earlobes and the average of these earlobe electrodes was used as reference.
The remaining four external channels were placed around the eyes to record bipolar vertical
and horizontal electrooculograms, EOGs. All recordings were conducted in a double-walled
sound booth, Eckel Corporation.
4.1.4
Preprocessing
The EEG and EOG data were loaded into Matlab using eeglab version 14_1_0b sccn.ucsd.edu
and the BIOSIG toolbox plugin.
In Matlab each trial was detrended by the function
detrend. The detrend function removed the linear trends from each EEG-channels in each
trial by nding the straight lines (the rst order polynomials) that best tted the data (of
each channel in each trial) in a least-squares sense and subtract these trends from their
corresponding channels in the given trial. After the linear trend subtraction the sample
means over all trials for each channels are found and subtracted from the data. Further-
more, if a channel was denoted 'bad' during recording it was spherically interpolated using
eeglab. Lastly, the data was ltered with an antialiasing lter (cut-o 128 Hz) before it
was re-sampled to 256 Hz with eeglab's resample function.
Temporal Filtering, ICA and Spherical Interpolation
Let X denote the three dimensional matrix that contains the EEG data (that was loaded
into Matlab). Let xr,c,t denote an element in X where t ∈{1, 2, ..., T} is a discrete time
point, c ∈{1, 2, ..., C} denotes the EEG-channel and r denotes the trial r ∈{1, 2, ..., R}.
In both experiments T = 180fs = 46, 080, C = 64 and R = 3.
This EEG data was high-pass ltered at 1 Hz with a zero-phase FIR highpass lter (with
δ2 = −54 dB and rp = 0.0174 dB) before it was submitted toeeglab's ICA. The ICA
was used to remove artifacts introduced by the piecewise linear trend subtraction and to
do an eye-artifacts rejection. In the TRF experiment a total of 12.3 components (with
a standard deviation of 3.3) were projected out of the data on average and 2.7 of these
components were eye components (with a standard deviation of 0.8). In the attentional
switch experiment the total number of out-projected components were on average 9.2 (with
a standard deviation of 2.2) and 3.4 of these components were eye components (std 0.7).
Subsequently to the ICA the data was lowpass ltered at a cut-o frequency of 8 Hz (with
δ2 = −60 dB and rp = 0.0174 dB). The reasoning behind this 'bandpass' ltering (1-8
Hz) is that the temporal envelope of speech is best correlated with the EEG signal in this
frequency range [Fuglsang et al., 2017], see Sec. 2.5. The lters applied are analyzed in
App. E.6.
Lastly, a visual inspection of the bandpass ltered EEG-data was accomplished to detect
4.1 Experimental Design
==PAGE:83==
68
CHAPTER 4.
METHOD
excessively noise channels.
Excessively noise channels were spherically interpolated by
eeglab's interpolation function. In the TRF experiment 2.8 bad channels (std. 3.5) were
on average interpolated (one subject had 10 bad channels).
In the attentional switch
experiment 1 bad channel (std. 1.2) were on average interpolated.
Spatial Filtering
As mentioned in Sec.
3.7, the biological noise can be eliminated by a DSS-algorithm.
However, an ecient elimination occurs only if the EEG data prior to the application of
the DSS-algorithm has been bandpass ltered to reduce environmental noise and if eye-
artifacts have been removed from the data (either by ICA or the eye-artifact subtraction
method given in App. A).
The DSS-algorithm is based on the assumption that the mixing matrix between brain
sources and sensors is stationary for a given stimulus [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008], which
implies that the spatial lter, Dspatial, is constant. Hence, for each stimulus i ∈{1, 2, ..., I}
a spatial lter, Dspatial,i, must be extracted. The results in App. A gave no clear indication
on whether or not spatial lters are dierent across subjects and hence in the following
both individual and common spatial lters are applied to get more insight into this matter
(a common spatial lter is obtained by treating all data (across subjects) as coming from
a single subject).
Recall that [Akram et al., 2015] found that the rst DSS component is sucient to describe
the stimulus-phase-locked neural responses. This implies that if the spatial lter obtained
by the DSS algorithm is applied to X then the output yields a two dimensional matrix Y,
which contains R time series, yr = [y1,r, y2,r, ..., yN,r]T for r ∈{1, 2, ..., R}. Each of these
r time series is denoted as a neural response and they are the components that are to be
analysed.
4.1.5
Speech Envelope Extraction
The speech envelope extraction is similar to those in [Biesmans et al., 2016] and [Fuglsang
et al., 2017]. It starts of by modelling the basilar membrane as a gammatone lter bank.
The lterbank consists of Ns gammatone lters with characteristic frequencies ranging
from flow to fhigh. The default is to set Ns = 32, flow = 150 Hz, and fhigh = 4000 Hz.
The lower center frequency is identical to what [Fuglsang et al., 2017] and [Biesmans et al.,
2016] used. The upper center frequency was choosen to be 4000 Hz because all speech sig-
nals utilized in this project are low-pass ltered at 4000 Hz.
All gammatone lters of the lter bank are set to have n = 4 and ϕ = 0 based on the
discussion in Sec. 2.2.2. The ERB# for both flow and fhigh are found by Eq. 2.2. The
remaining Ns −2 ERB#s are generated in between the lower and higher ERB# such
that they equidistantly span the ERB scale. These ERB#s are then mapped back onto
the frequency-domain by Eq. 2.3 to yield the characteristic frequencies of the gammatone
lters. The only initialization that remains is the determination of K (in Eq. E.24) and the
parameters ak (so that Eq. E.24 comes on the form given in Eq. E.14). In this implemen-
tation, K has been chosen to give unit gain at the characteristic frequencies of the lters
(by utilzing Eq. E.24 it must apply that HAP,gamma(z)|z=ej2πfc/fs = HAP,gamma(fc) =
1 ⇔K = |
 1 −e−b/fs  1 −e(−b−4jπfc)/fs
|). The parameters ak are found by expand-
4.1 Experimental Design
==PAGE:84==
CHAPTER 4.
METHOD
69
ing the denominator of Eq. E.24. The gammatone lter bank has now been created and
can be utilize to partitioning the speech signal. However, as argumented in Sec. 2.2.3, it
can be advantageous to compensate for the time delays, td, that the lters generate. The
default of the algorithm is to do the time-compensation, however, it can be discarded. The
time delay compensation is accomplished by delaying the input signal (to each lter) by
td since a delay in the input causes the same delay in the output (gammatone lters are
LTI systems). The outputs of the gammatone lters, ss(t), are subsequently power law
compressed, |ss(t)|β, where β = 0.6. This compression mimic the compressive response
behaviour of the auditory lters around their characteristic frequency and it is similar to
the method by [Biesmans et al., 2016]. The compressed subband-signals are then nor-
malized so that all channels get equal weight (dierent lters has dierent gain due to
HAP,gamma(fc) = 1 for all lters). The compressed and normalized subband-signals are
then averaged to obtain the envelope of the speech signal ((based on nding by [Biesmans
et al., 2016]). The envelope is then anti-aliasing ltered before it is submitted to a down-
sampling. The resampled evnelope has a sample rate of 256 Hz. It was stated in [Power
et al., 2012] that the speech envelope and the EEG-data is linear related (when dealing
with TRFs) hence the speech envelope should be low-pass ltered with a cut-o frequency
of 8 Hz [Power et al., 2012] [Ding and Simon, 2012b] [Fuglsang et al., 2017]. The lter
applied is the same as applied on the EEG-data. Lastly, the speech envelope signal is
converted into dB [Akram et al., 2015].
The above envelope extraction method dierence from the method utilized by [Akram
et al., 2015]. In their framework, the envelope was extracted by taking the modulus of the
analytic signal of the speech signal. This envelope was then smoothed by a lowpass lter
with a cut-o frequency of 20 Hz and converted into dB.
To test whether or not the auditory-inspired envelope extraction increases the performance
of the state-space model in Sec. 4.2 an analytic envelope extraction was also implement in
this project. The analytic envelopes were obtained with a method nearly identical to the
one used by [Akram et al., 2015], the dierence is that the cut-o frequency of the low-pass
lter was altered from 20 Hz to 8 Hz. The results of applying the auditory-inspired and
analytic envelope extraction are shown in Fig. 4.3.
Figure 4.3: The blue curve is the speech signal from male speaker 1 (notice that it includes the ne structure).
The red and green curves are the extracted envelopes found by applying the analytic approach and
the auditory-inspired approach, respectively.
4.1 Experimental Design
==PAGE:85==

CHAPTER 4.

4.2
Modelling
The modelling framework is (similarly to the framework by [Akram et al., 2015]) divided
into three stages
1. The forward model maps the temporal features of the attended and unattended
speech streams onto the neural response.
2. The attentional state space model governs the state space, hence it is a model
over the latent space. In the following this state space is denoted as the attentional
state space.
3. The inference stage uses the forward model and the attentional state space model
to infer the attentional state of the listener. The inference procedure is based on the
neural responses and the speech envelopes
4.2.1
The forward model
The forward model is of a linear mapping of the speech envelopes onto to the neural
responses. The speech envelopes are obtained as described in Sec. 4.1.5 and the neural
responses as in Sec. 4.1.4. In the case where only one speech stream is presented to the
listener, the linear mapping is given as a simple noisy convolution. Hence
yt,r = τt ∗Et + ϵt,r
(4.1)
where Et denotes the speech envelope at time t measured in dB, ϵt,r is the noise at time
t in trial r, τt is the TRF and yt,r is the neural response at time t in trial r. The length
of the TRF (in samples) is denoted Lτ (in this project Lτ = 0.5s · 256Hz = 128 samples,
which is same length as [Akram et al., 2015] utilized). The noise term ϵt,r accounts for
the trial-dependent task-irrelevant components that are embedded in the neural response
at time t in trial r. The model in Eq. 4.1 can be rewritten into
yt,r = τ T Et + ϵt,r
(4.2)
where Et = [Et−L+1, ..., Et−1, Et]T and τ = [τ1, ...., τL−1, τL]T . Notice that τ is the TRF
in vector form. Hence, each element of yt,r is a linear combination of the current envelope
value and all of the Lτ −1 previous envelope values to which a noise is added. Hence, Eq.
4.1 is a linear regression model (see Sec. 3.2) or as denoted in Sec. 2.6; a forward model.
Recall from Sec. 2.6 that many studies have used forward model to predict attentional
state of listeners.
Two examples are [Ding and Simon, 2012a] and [Ding and Simon,
2012b] who used TRFs to predict the attentional state of a subject using MEG data.
These two studies have shown that TRFs are sparse lters and that they have signicant
components (analogous to the M50 and M100 responses) if the subject is attenting to
a speech stream [Akram et al., 2015].
Based on the assumption that EEG and MEG
data are generated by the same neural potentials (see App. C), it is plausible to assume
that the TRFs obtained with EEG data are also sparse lters with signicant components
(analogous to the P1 and N1). This assumption is support by ndings in [Fuglsang et al.,
2017] and the TRFs obtained in this study, see Sec 5.1.
To estimate the TRF in a single speaker case, it is assumed that {ϵt,r} is an i.i.d. Gaussian
4.2 Modelling
==PAGE:86==
CHAPTER 4.
METHOD
71
noise sequence. This assumption does that a sparse solution can be extracted if the ℓ1-
regularized SSE is minimized (see Sec. 3.2). Hence, the optimization problem is
ˆτ = arg min

(4.3)
where λ is the regularization parameter, T is the total number of sample in each trail and
R is the total number of trials. Notice that time starts at sample fs + Lτ. The reason
for discarding the rst fs samples is that it has been shown that the onset of a stimulus
generates large initial neural responses (especially N1 peaks) that are large and indepen-
dent of attentional state of the listener [Choi et al., 2014]. The additional delay of Lτ
is needed because Lτ −1 prior envelope values are needed to estimate the current value
of the neural response. The optimization problem in Eq. 4.3 is solved using the Matlab
function lasso. The lasso function has the ability to do a Q-folded cross validation (based
on MSE) to determining the regularization parameter, λ. In this experiment a two-folded
cross validation was used to obtained the optimal λ (for each TRF).
The method above was used on the data from the TRF-experiment to obtain three individ-
ual TRFs, one for each speaker. In general, if I TRFs is given and they are all obtained in
an active listening task then they are denoted as τ a,i
t , where i ∈{1, 2, ..., I} is the speaker
index. This procedure for obtaining the TRFs is similar to the one used by [Akram et al.,
2015].
Notice, that the model in Eq. 4.1 assumes that only one speaker is present in the audi-
tory environment, an assumption that is violated in the attentional switch experiment and
hence the actual forward model is not the one in Eq. 4.1.
Recall from Sec.
2.4 that a listener only can attend to one auditory object at any
time, hence only one speaker in a competing speaker environment causes attentional re-
sponses. However, it has been shown that the non-attentional speech stream generates
non-attentional response in both EEG [Fuglsang et al., 2017] and MEG [Ding and Simon,
2012a]. Hence, to include these non-attentional response the linear model is expanded to
yt,r = τ a

i,t and Ei,t is the attended TRF and the envelope of the attended speech stream,

j,t and Ej,t is the unattended TRFs and the envelopes of the
unattended speech streams, respectively. To estimate the unattended TRFs ndings by
[Ding and Simon, 2012a] and [Fuglsang et al., 2017] was used. [Ding and Simon, 2012a]
showed that the unattended TRFs will be signicantly suppressed beyond the M50 evoked
eld and hence beyond the P1 evoked potential. This implies that the unattended TRFs
for each speaker can be obtained by truncation their attended TRF beyond a lag of 90 ms
[Akram et al., 2015]. Similar results was found by [Fuglsang et al., 2017] (in their anechoic
condition) and hence this denition of unattended TRFs is utilized. Furthermore, these
assumption is supported by the fact that the P1 and N1 are automatic responses, hence,
these component are related to bottom-up saliency eects. In contrast, later component
(including the N1) are top down controlled [Choi et al., 2014]. The fact that N1 is partially
top-down controlled can be seen by the fact that it is enhanced (it amplitude is increased)
in response to an attended stimulus [Choi et al., 2014].

4.2.2
Selective Attention: A Nonstationary Multidimensional
Bernoulli Process
Let it be assumed that within an observation window of size, W, the attentional state
of the speaker is constant (in this project W = 0.25s · 256Hz = 64 samples, which the
same window size as [Akram et al., 2015] utilized). Recall from Sec. 2.4 that human
listeners only can attend to one auditory stream at a time, hence, the listener can only
attend to one of the I speakers. Furthermore, it seems plausible the listener sometimes
(unintentionally) switch her/his attention to a competing stream. These two factors implies
that the attentional state within each window, k, can be modelled by the multidimensional
generalization of the Bernoulli distribution (see Sec. 3.1.1). If ni,k,r denotes the binary
variable1 that describes the attentional state of the listener in window k and trial r;
ni,k,r =

In Sec. 2.4, it was stated that the attentional state of a listener can be altered across
time, however, that this shift of attention requires time. This implies that the current
attentional state of the listener depends on the previous attentional state or states. To
model this it is convenient to expand the support of the variable pi,k. In [Bishop, 2006], it
is shown that the natural parameters of the multidimensional Bernoulli, zi,k, are related to
the success probabilities, pi,k, by the softmax function. The softmax relation is based on
the fact that only I −1 of the success probabilities are independent and hence, the last pi,k
can be obtained from the remaining I −1. However, to keep the mathematical notation
compact a 'simpler' relation between the natural parameter and the success probability .
The (simpler) relation is given as
pi,k :=

(4.8)
Notice that the constrains in Eq. 4.7 are satised by Eq. 4.8. Eq. 4.8 has an one-to-one
monotonic relation between the success probabilities and the natural parameters. It is a
monotonic relation since an increase of zi,k from −∞to ∞caueses pi,k to monotonically
1Recall, from Sec. 3.1.1 that a two state binary processes can be described completely with
(only) one binary random variable governed by a Bernoulli distribution. Based on this a simpler
model can be given for the two competitive speaker condition. This model is derived in App. B.1.

denoted as the attentional state space parameter for the attentional state i in window k.
The argument for introducing the variable change is that this change allows the use of
AR-models and ARI-models (for more information on this type of models see Sec. 3.3).
Let it be assumed that the attentional state i in window k only depends on the attentional
state i in window k −1 and that it does so without damping (notice, that this assumption
implies that time evolution of a given attentional state space variable is independent of
the evolutions of all other attentional states, however, that this does not cause that the
success probabilities of the dierent attentional states to be independent due to Eq. 4.8).
Thus, the attentional state i can be modelled as a random walk (see Sec. 3.3 for more
information on the random walk),
zi,k = zi,k−1 + ωi,k
(4.9)
where {ωi,k} is a noise sequence. Notice that Eq. 4.9 lead to a continuity constrain that
does that the listener not instantaously can shift the selective auditory attention but that
the attentional shift takes time [Smith et al., 2004]. This continuity constrain seems to be
plausible based on the fact that the auditory system has an adaptation period related to
an attentional switch (see Sec. 2.4). If {ωi,k} is assumed be an i.i.d zero-mean Gaussian
process with variances, ηi,k, then zi,k is governed by

non-stationary multidimensional Bernoulli process (governed by Eq. 4.6 and 4.10) prior
distributions for ηi,k are needed. In Sec. 3.1.1, it was shown that the conjugated prior for

for i = 1, .., I and k = 1, 2, ..., K. As stated in Sec. 2.6, it was shown that the neural
response in window k is more correlated with the predictor ei,k if the listener is attending
to the ith speaker. In order to obtain a correlation measure some of the element of basic
mathematics is needed. Recall that in the Euclidean space the inner product between two
vector x1 and x2 is dened as
⟨x1, x2⟩= xT
1 x2 = ||x1||x2|| cos(θ)
Hence, if x1 and x2 are unit vectors then the angle between them are
θ = cos−1 (⟨x1, x2⟩)
Notice that if θ = 0the vectors are parallel, which implies that the observations are strongly
correlated. Alternatively, if θ = π the vectors are orthogonal and hence totally uncorre-
lated. This empirical measure of correlation is applied to nd the correlation between
the observed neural response yk,r, and the model prediction, ei,k. Hence, the empirical
measure of correlation is the angle between the unit direction vectors yk,r/||yk,r|| and
ei,k/||ei,k||, which yields
θi,k,r := cos−1

and the ith model prediction, ei,k. Notice that this lead to K · R empirical correlations
for each of the i predictions where θi,k,r ≈0 means high correlation and θi,k,r ≈π means
low correlation. Recall from Sec. 3.1.1 that correlations, such as θi,k,r, can be model by I
separate von Mises-Fisher distributions. Hence, the models governing θi,k,r are
p(θi,k,r) =
2κW/2−1

where the parameters in {κi } are the concentration parameters of each of the I von
Mises-Fishers distribution and IW (·) is the W th order modied Bessel function of rst
kind (given by Eq. 3.32). Notice that the distribution in Eq. 4.14 has an exponential
function that is equal to the univariate von Mises distribution with mean direction =
0. It might seem weird that the exponential term seems to belong to a univariate von
Mises distribution and that the normalization coecient belongs to the von Mises-Fisher
distrubtion over a hypersphere SW−1. The reasoning is that the one-dimensional angle is
estimated in a W-dimensional Euclidean space and hence the normalization should reect
this. However, since only one (correlation) parameter is obtained it is convenient to choose
θ0 = [1, 0, ..., 0]T . (it is a valid choice of θ0 = [1, 0, ..., 0]T since the constraint ||θ0|| = 1
is satised) and the rst element of x to be cos(θ) (inspired by the univariate von Mises
distribution).
Recall from Sec.
3.1.1 that an univariate von Mises distribution has a
mean direction, which set equal to zero in this model. The reason for setting the mean
direction to zero is that the empirical correlation measure is a relative measure between
two vectors and thus θi,k,r = 0 must lead to the highest probability (this implies that
the concentration parameters κi is used to capture the concentration of θi,k,r around the
4.2 Modelling
==PAGE:90==
CHAPTER 4.

75
mean direction θi,k,r = 0 for each of the I distributions). Lastly, it should be noted that
the distribution in Eq. 4.14 has a normalization coecient that is twice as large as the
normalization coecient of the von Mises-Fisher distribution in Eq. 3.33. The scaling by
a factor of two comes from the fact that θ is restricted to the interval [0, π], which is half
of the interval that the von Mises-Fisher distributions was dened on and hence since the
distribution is symmetric around the mean direction a scaling by a factor two causes a
correct normalization of the von Mises-Fisher distribution.
It is important to understand that the correlation probability, p(θi,k,r), of the i attentional
state is not the same as the success probabilities pi,k in the Bernoulli process. The success
probability, pi,k, is the probability that the model assign for beginning in state i in window
k whereas the p(θi,k,r) is the probability that the observed data of trial r in window k
is coming from the attentional state i based on the given model. Hence, the correlation
probability is used to infer the suitable state i in each window. To clarify, let us assume
that it is known that p2,k = 1 and hence pi,k = 0 ∀i ̸= 2.
It implies that only the
correlation probability of interest (in respect to training the model) is p(θ2,k,r). Hence,
the importance of each of correlation probabilities p(θi,k,r) during training depends on the
success probability of the state pi,k. This implies that the model for the attentional state
actually is a mixture of I von Mises-Fisher distribution where the success probability of
the state is the discrete latent variables (also known as a mixing coecient). Thus,
p(θi,k,r|κi, zi,k) = pi,k

i = 1, 2, ..., I (4.15)

state space variable zi,k (since zi,k is related to pi,k by Eq. 4.8). Thus, this link makes the
mixture of von Mises-Fisher distributions non-stationary.
To obtain a Bayesian treatment of the inverse problem priors for the concentration param-

The equation simplies in the case where only two

On complication in respect to maximizing Eq. 4.19 is that the term related to the mixture
of von Mises-Distributions has the logarithm outside the sum and hence, the logarithm
can not be applied to each individual von Mises-Fisher distribution. This does that the
logarithm cannot linearise the optimization on κi and hence a direct maximization of the
log-posterior is complicated bacuse the maximization becomes a high dimensional convex
optimization problem. This optimziation probem is further complicated by the temporal
coupling of the state parameters [Akram et al., 2015]. To address the complications an EM
algorithm is used. Recall from Sec. 3.5 that the EM algorithm is an ecient method for
estimating parameters of a state-space model. To apply the EM algorithm the log-posterior

Notice that the state-space parameters have a superscript (l). This superscript indicates

since this is an important result, this expectation is denoted as E(l) [ni,k,r]. Actually, it is
the E-step of the outer-EM algorithm because (as will be shown shortly) the optimization
of the expectation (the M-step) is eciently accomplished by a second EM-algorithm (an
inner EM-algorithm).

by the non-stationary Bernoulli process governed by a Gaussian autoregressive model. This
model is a special case of the model that [Smith and Brown, 2003] used to predict neural
spiking activity. Smith and Brown showed that an ecient approximate solution to the un-
observed state-space process and corresponding state-space parameters can be achieved by
an approximate EM algorithm. The approximate EM-algorithm that [Smith and Brown,
2003] developed has an E-step that is a point process smoothing algorithm. The point pro-
cess smoothing algorithm by [Smith and Brown, 2003] is decomposed into three recursive
steps. The rst step is a point process recursive non-linear lter algorithm, which is based
on the maximum a posterior derivation of the Kalman lter algorithm [Smith and Brown,

The above EM-algorithm (and the EM-algorithm in App. B.1) has a very low overall
computational complexity, which makes it an applicable real world AAD [Akram et al.,
2015]. The state-space parameter inference is summarized in Fig. 4.4.
The illustration in Fig. 4.4 does not state that maximum number of outer EM iterations,
lmax, the maximum number of inner EM iterations, mmax, tolerance of dierence in log-
posterior, tol, and the type I error, α, must be submitted to the algorithm. These variables
are per default lmax = 6, mmax = 50, tol = eps (eps is a very small number generated
by the Matlab function eps) and α =
(
0.3
R = 1
0.1
R > 1. Furthermore, the algorithm will after
each outer E-step check that non of the elements in E(l+1)[ni,k,r] is NaN, if any of the
element are NaN these elements are replaced by the value of corresponding elements in
E(l)[ni,k,r]. Furthermore, the algorithm will after each outer M-step check that all κ(l+1)
i
is less than 100, if that is not the case the κi that is greater than 100 is truncated to
be 100. The checks on the outputs of outer EM-algorithm are done to ensure that the
log-posterior does not become innity since this will cause a too fast convergence of the
framework. The reason that this was not a problem for [Akram et al., 2015] is that they
did not implement a stopping criterion based on the log-posterior. Fig. 4.4 does state
that Eq. 4.21 (or Eq. B.12) is used to both evaluate the inner and outer change in log-
posterior. Actually, this is not exactly true but the evaluations are based on this equation.
In the outer step, log-posteriors (one for each iteration) are calculated by Eq. 4.21 (or Eq.
4.2 Modelling
==PAGE:97==
82
CHAPTER 4.
METHOD
B.12) where ni,k,r is substituted by E[ni,k,r]. In each iteration of the outer EM-algorithm
the dierence between the current outer log-posterior and the outer log-posterior from the
previous iteration is evaluated to see if there is a dierence in the log-posterior. If this
dierence is smaller than ϵ then the algorithm terminates. In the inner step, the procedure
is the same, however, only terms of Eq. 4.21 (or Eq. B.12) that are depending on zi,k
and ηi,k is considered (to decrease computational complexity). Another way to decrease
the computational complexity of this inference state in an on-line application would be to
save the state-space parameters, the state-space variables and θ-values so that only new
θ-values needs to be calculated and so that the prior state-space variables and parameters
can used as initial guesses in the inference procedure.
Another thing that Fig. 4.4 does no show is that the condition I > 3 has two extra stopping
criterion on the outer EM algorithm. The criterion is that if either z(l+1)
i,k|i,K = z(l)
i,k|i,K or
z(l+1)
i,k|i,K = z(l−1)
i,k|i,K the algorithm will terminate. The rst criterion is a common stopping
criterion for EM-algorithm (see Sec.
3.5).
However, the second criterion is not that
common. It was implement because the algorithm during testing on high SNRs showed
a bistability. The reason for this bistability might be interesting to investigate from a
theoretical point of view, however, in real EEG-application such high SNR is not achievable.
The last thing that Fig. 4.4 does not show is how the condence is calculated. In the case
of I = 2 the condence interval is
pk(upper) =
ezk+σkZ(1−α
2 )
1 + ezk+σkZ(1−α
2 )
and
pk(lower) =
ezk+σkZ( α
2 )
1 + ezk+σkZ( α
2 )
∀k ∈{1, ..., K}
where Z is the inverse CDF of the standardized normal distribution. In the case where
I ≥3 the condence intervals are
pi,k(upper) =
ezi,k+σi,kZ(1−α
2 )
ezi,k+σi,kZ(1−α
2 ) + P

∀k ∈{1, ..., K} and ∀i ∈{1, ..., I}
4.2.5
Obtaining hyperparameters
Based on two simulation study (similar to the ones in Sec. 5.2) the hyperparameters were
optimized so that classication performance of the state space model was most robust
at both very high and low SNRs.
Both of these studies led to the same result.
The
optimization was performed with the nonlinear programming solver fminsearch in Matlab
and its optimization criterion was to maximize the hit rate while miniziming the false
alarm rate (all SNR levels were weighted equally). The start point of the optimization was
the best hyperparameter found by [Akram et al., 2015] and not surprisingly, it was found
that these hyperparameters actually was the optimal parameter set (it is not surprising
since the data that we generate is the similar to the data [Akram et al., 2015] generated).
This implies that the hyperparameters in the conjugated priors for {κi} are d = 50 · R · K
and c0 = 0.01. Notice, that the choice d = 50·R·K causes the ctitious prior observations
to be more prominent than the empirical estimates of κi in the conjugated prior for κi.
Furthermore, the optimization caused the hyperparameters of the inverse-Gamma prior
4.2 Modelling

Results
5.1
TRF Experiment
The EEG data obtained in the TRF experiment was used to create the TRFs needed in
the forward model of the state-space model (see Sec. 4.2.1). However, there are numerous
ways in which the TRFs can be obtained. Firstly, the TRFs might dierence based on
the envelope extraction method and hence dierent TRFs must be extracted based on the
envelope extraction method. Secondly, the TRFs might dier based on way the data is
handle during training. One possible training approach is to feed the training paradigm
with all data across subjects (in a given speaker condition) to obtained what is denoted as
the common TRF (for the given speaker condition). The alternative is to nd a TRF for
each of the subjects individually (in the given speaker condition), which is accomplished
by only feeding the algorithm the data of the given subject (in the given condition). These
individual TRFs are then averaged to yield the individual TRF (for the given speaker
condition). Lastly, the TRFs might depend on the spatial lter used to obtain the neural
activity. In App. A it is stated that the spatial lters either can be common spatial lter
(the spatial lter obtained based on all TRF data) or individual spatial lters (the spatial
lter obtained based on the TRF data from on of the subjects). Based on these dierences
some preliminary testing on generalization of the obtained TRFs was conducted. Two gen-
eralization tests were conducted both of which were based on the empirical correlations,
θi,k,r, given by Eq. 4.13. In both tests R · K = 3 · 720 = 2160 empirical correlations for
each subject in each condition were obtained. The three empirical correlations in window
k of trial r (for a given subject in a given condition) were found as the empirical corre-
lation between the actual neural activity (of the subject in the given condition) and the
three neural predictions of window k and the trial r, where the neural predictions were
given by Eq. 4.1 with the corresponding TRF and speech envelope (notice that the unat-
tended parts are not included because no distractor streams were presented). The speaker
i that has lowest empirical correlation (hence has the highest correlation between model
prediction and actual data) in window k of each trial r is denoted as the attentional target
prediction of the subject in the given window and trial of the speaker condition. It is these
attentional target predictions that are used to test generalization.
The rst test were to generate confusion matrices based on the attentional target predic-
tion, the results are shown in Tab. 5.1-5.4. The confusion matrices are constructed by
nding the subject's individual confusion matrix and then average across each cell in the
confusion matrices to yield the numbers without parenthesis in the confusion matrices.
The number in parenthesis is one standard deviation and it is an indication of the subject
85
==PAGE:101==
86
CHAPTER 5.
RESULTS
variability of the estimations. The individual confusion matrices were found by taking the
sum of attentional target predictions for each speaker in a given speaker condition in order
to yield the row of the confusion matrix. Thus, the correct estimate of each condition is
the diagonal element of the confusion matrix. This approach was used to construct eight
confusion matrices, one for each of the combinations of neural activity extraction (common
or individual spatial lter), training approach (common or individual TRF) and envelope
extraction method (auditory-inspired or analytic). In each table the neural activity ex-
traction and training approach are the same and thus the only dierence between the left
and right confusion matrix is the envelope extraction method, the left uses the auditory
inspired gammatone lter bank extraction method and the right uses the analytic envelope
extraction method. The two rst tables, Tab. 5.1 and 5.2, show the confusion matrices
when individual spatial lters are applied to obtain the neural activity.
