Table 5.1: This table shows two confusion matrices under the conditions state above the matrices. The numbers
without parenthesis are the mean of the sum of attentional target predictions across subjects (for a given
speaker in a given speaker condition) and the number in parenthesis are one standard deviation of the
sum of attentional target predictions across subjects (for a given speaker in a given speaker condition).
The sum of attentional target predictions for speaker i of a given subject in a given condition is found
by counting the number of times that θi,k,r < θj,k,rj ∈{1, 2, 3} \ i for the given subject in the given
speaker condition.
This is done for all speaker conditions and all possible values of i to yield the
individual confusion matrices.
These individual confusion can then be used to generate the overall
confusion matrices shown in this table.
In both Tab. 5.1 and 5.2 it should be noticed that on average the male speaker 1 condition
is never classied correctly and that the mean of the sum of attentional target predictions
always seems to be lower for male speaker 1 than for the two other speakers. However,
it should also be noted that the standard deviations across subjects are quit large (spe-
cially in the case of auditory-inspired envelope extraction) and hence there might be some
subjects that actually perform well. To test this a second test of generalization was con-
structed. The performance measure in this test was to say that a condition (for the given
subject) is correctly estimated if for all windows k across all trial r (for the given subject)
causes a correct estimate most times (hence if P
i=target θi,k,r > 720). This generalization
test showed that individual spatial lters caused most individual subjects to classify the
male speaker 2 condition correctly (four to ve subjects got this conditions correctly in
each of the four individual spatial lter conditions). It was a little bit worse for the female
speaker 1 condition, however, at least half of the subjects would get this correct (three to
ve got this conditions correctly in each of the four individual spatial lter conditions).

The main problem is in the case of the male speaker 1 condition where (in each of the
four individual spatial lter conditions) only two subjects got the condition correct, hence
under half of the subjects would get this condition correct. One possible reason is that the
individual spatial lter are not able to capture the neural activity that are phase-locked
to the stimulus (for more discussion on this see App. A). The fact that the neural activity
that are phase-locked to the stimulus are not captured might be support by Fig. 5.1. The
upper row of Fig. 5.1 shows in the left plot the subjects' TRFs of male speaker 1 (in the
case where the applied spatial lters are the individual spatial lters and the envelope was
extracted by the auditory-inspired approach). The middle and right plots of the upper row
show the subjects' TRFs for female speaker 1 and male speaker 2, respectively (under the
same activity and envelope extraction method as used to obtain the subjects' TRFs of male
speaker 1). Notice that the subjects' TRFs (for each speaker condition) are very dierent
from one another, especially compared to the lower row of plots, which shows the same as
the upper row with the small change that the lower row has used common spatial lters
to obtain the neural activity (the same was true when the analytic envelope extraction
was used). This might suggest that the common spatial lters are better at nding the
neural activity that is phase-locked to the stimulus modulations since similar TRFs is a
consequence of similar neural activities (the assumptions behind applying common spatial
lters are given in App. A). If the hypothesis that the common spatial lters are better
at extracting the neural activity phase-locked to the stimulus modulations then the appli-
cation of common spatial lters should improve the generalization of the obtained TRFs.
This was tested by applying the empirical correlations in the same manner as it was done
in the case of individual spatial lters. The analysis across subjects are summarized by
the confusion matrices in Tab. 5.3 and 5.4. Notice that if the common spatial lters are
apply then the diagonal elements are always highest independent of envelope extraction
method and training paradigm. Thus, the application of common spatial lters will on
average across subjects cause the correct speaker estimate to be made more frequently
than incorrect speaker estimates.
The individual subject performances was also tested and it showed that all conditions
with common spatial lters would cause all subjects to have all three speaker conditions
correct with the one exception. The exception was in the case of individual TRF with
analytic extraction where ve of the subjects (all expect subject 6) got male speaker 2
condition correct.

These results suggest that the common spatial lters causes a greater overall classication
accuracy than the individual spatial lters. However, since the TRFs are trained on this
data set the results might suer from overtting so an actual conclusion must be drawn
from the attentional switch experiment (see Sec. 5.3). Furthermore, these generalization
estimates use the data across trials and does not state anything about individual trials. It
might be that one trial actually has a very high classication accuracy and that one has
a low. This should be test in the further studies. Furthermore, one should test if the data
used to do the cross-validation and the actual training data have dierent results in these
test.
It was mentioned that the attentional switch experiment were more suitable to draw con-
clusion on the spatial versus individual spatial lters.
The analysis of the attentional
switch experiment are given in Sec. 5.3 but to summaries the analysis did not showed any
dierence in the classication performance of the state-space model if the model applied
individual versus common spatial lter. This does not necessary mean that the common
spatial lters are not a little better than the individual spatial lters at extracting the
phased-locked neural activity. The reasoning behind this is that if the distributions of
empirical correlations in the attentional switch experiment lead to the confusion matrices
similar to those of Tab. 5.1-5.4 then the number of correct estimates might be too similar
to the number of incorrect estimates in order for the state space model to make predictions
that are dierent from chance (a preliminary test showed that this might actually be the
case).
An alternative reason that the classication performance did not increase in the case of
common spatial lter might be that the common spatial lters applied are the common
spatial lters extracted from TRF-data, which might be dierent from the spatial lters
in the attentional switch experiment. The intuition behind this is that the spatial lters

As mentioned it is in Sec. 5.3 shown that the TRFs obtained from neural activities ex-
tracted by spatial lters do not give an applicable performance of the state space model in
Sec. 4.2. In an attempt to mitigate the potential problem of spatial lters multi-channel
TRFs were obtained. The multi-channel TRFs are C × Lτ matrices where C is the total
number of EEG-channels and Lτ is the length of the TRFs in samples. The multi-channel
TRF maps the speech envelope onto an estimate of the C-channel EEG (more on this
in Sec. 5.3). The common multi-channel TRF (for female speaker 1) is depicted in Fig.
5.4. Notice, that the late components (from 330 to 370 msec) have a tendency to be most
pronounced in the frontal electrodes, which is consists with ndings by [Fuglsang et al.,

5.2
Simulation Study
To analyse the output the state space model denitions of hits and false alarms are needed.
The denitions in the two and three speaker conditions are exemplied in the upper and
lower plots of Fig. 5.5, respectively. The two speaker condition in the upper plot of Fig.
5.5 is constructed so that the attentional target of the listener is speaker 1 in the rst
half of the trial and then halfway through the trail an attentional switch occurs (indicated
by the yellow line), which causes the attentional state of the listener to be speaker 2. In
this condition, the attentional state prediction is a hit if in the rst half of the trial the
lower condence boundary is above chance (hence if the lower dashed blue line is above
the black line) and in the second half of the trial if the upper condence boundary is
below chance (hence if the upper dashed blue line is below the black line). Alternatively,
the attentional state prediction is a false alarm if in the rst half of the trial the upper
condence boundary is below chance (hence if the upper dashed line is below the black
line) and in the second half of the trial if the lower condence boundary is above chance
(lower dashed blue line is above the black line). Respectively, false alarms and hits are
indicated by gray areas and green areas in Fig. 5.5. Notice that there area in which the
5.2 Simulation Study
==PAGE:107==
92
CHAPTER 5.
RESULTS
Figure 5.5: This gure exemplies the classication criteria in a two speaker condition and a three speaker condi-
tion, upper and lower plot respectively. Dark green areas indicate that the algorithm classied correct,
grey areas indicate that the algorithm gave a false alarm and white areas indicate that the algorithm
did not assign the attentional state to any of the possible classes. Lastly, red areas are exempted from
classication (the reason for the exclusion is given in the text). The yellow lines indicate attentional
switches and the black line is the chance level. Lastly, the solid colored lines are the probabilities of
being in a given attentional state (dierent colors dierent state) and the dashed lines in the same
colors are the upper and lower limits of the (70% or 90%) condence interval for the given attentional
state.
condence interval includes chance, in these intervals the algorithm does no classication.
The reasoning is that algorithm are not able to make a statistically condent prediction
in these areas (these areas are indicated by white). Lastly, Fig. 5.5 has red areas. These
red areas are excluded from the analysis. The red areas are in the two speaker condition
the two intervals; [0; 1] sec and [88; 91] sec. The reason for excluding these intervals is that
in the attentional switch experiment these intervals are used either to help the listener
detach the current attentional state (no stimulus present) or to cue the listener on the
next attentional state. Thus, these areas might feed no or wrong features to the model.
In the three speaker condition, the classication of the attentional state is based on the
lower condence limits of all three speakers. The classication criterion is that the atten-
tional state prediction is the attentional state that belongs to the speaker that has the
highest lower condence limit under the constrain that this highest lower condence limit
must be above chance (in the lower plot of Fig. 5.5 the chance level is 1/3). If none of the
lower condence limits are above the chance level no decision is made.
Based on these denitions, it possible test the model. In this section the model is tested
on a synthesised data set (hence it is a simulation study) and in Sec. 5.3 the model is
tested on real EEG-data.
In the simulation study two data sets were generated. One data set had I = 2 (hence two
speakers) and the second data set had I = 3. Both data sets were generated by Eq. 4.4 and
both sets consisted of 3 trials each of which were 180 sec long. The window length was 0.25
5.2 Simulation Study
==PAGE:108==
CHAPTER 5.
RESULTS
93
sec and the sampling rate was 256 Hz hence T = 180 · 256 = 46080, W = 0.25 · 256 = 64,
and K = ⌊T/W⌋= 720.
In the two speaker case the attended TRFs were the common TRF obtained with a com-
mon spatial lter for male speaker 1 and female speaker 1. The TRFs were 0.5 sec long
hence Lτ = 0.5 · 256 = 128.1 The envelopes were the corresponding envelopes obtained
with the auditory-inspired envelope extraction. The unattended TRFs were obtained by
truncating the attended TRF at around 90 msec. The data was simulated so that the
underlying model corresponded to attending to male speaker 1 in the rst half of each trial
and to female speaker 1 in the second half of each trial. In the following male speaker
1 is denoted as speaker 1 and female speaker 1 is denoted as speaker 2. The underlying
model is added with an i.i.d. Gaussian noise sequence to yield yt,r. The variance of the
noise sequence was varied to introduce dierent SNRs. The noise sequences simulate envi-
ronmental noises and nuisance neural activities that are still present in the data after the
preprocessing. The reasoning for varying the SNR was to test the robustness of the model
with respect to noise sources. Examples of outputs of this generative procedure are given
as the green dashed line in the upper plot of Fig. 5.6 and 5.7. The dierence between
the two plots are that the SNRs are 15 and -19 dB, respectively. In these upper plot,
Figure 5.6: The upper plot shows one trial of the simulated data set (dashed green line) together with the two
model predictions. The model prediction for speaker 1 is the blue line and the model prediction for the
speaker 2 is the red line. In the middle plot the blue line is the probability of being in the attentional
state of speaker 1 and the dots are the 90% condence interval (the model has used all three trials to

of variables have dierent variance). In all three plots the yellow line indicate the point where the

Notice, that the correlation between the model prediction and the synthesised data yt,r is
clear observable in Fig. 5.6 and unobservable in Fig. 5.7. The middle plots of Fig. 5.6
and 5.7 show the output of the state-space model, which is the probability of attending
to speaker 1 (solid blue line) and its condence interval limits (dot lines). Notice, that
the attentional state prediction seems have been shifted backwards in time in Fig. 5.6.
Actually, the attentional state prediction is shifted backwards and the reason is that the
backwards smoothing smears the prediction too much backwards if the data is too clean.
This conclusion is drawn based on the lower plot of Fig. 5.6, which have applied the exact
same state-space model but instead of using zk|K as the output state-space, z, it utilizes
zk|k (the posterior mode prediction). Recall from Sec. 4.2.4 that the posterior mode of the
state-space variable is the prediction of the state space variable just before the backwards
smoothing is applied. In the case where the posterior mode is applied the backwards shift-
ing does not occurs, which implies that the backwards smoothing causes problem if the
SNR is high. Notice in Fig. 5.7 that the backwards smoothing is not a problem if the
SNR is low. It should also be noticed that the model is able to capture the transitions of
the attentional state with a high temporal accuracy both under high SNR (if the posterior
mode prediction is outputted) and low SNR and that the condence interval increases in
width as the SNR increase.

by the model in Sec. 4.2. The lower plot is nearly identical to the middle plot. The only

in the lower pot whereas the backwards smoothed estimates are outputted in the middle
plot. Similar to the two speaker condition the gures (Fig. 5.6 and 5.7) show that the
backwards smoothing has an adversely eect if the data has a high SNR. Furthermore,
the I = 3 state space model is still able to capture the transitions of the attentional with
a high accuracy both with a high SNR (if the posterior mode prediction is outputted)
and a low SNR. In the two speaker condition is was observed that the condence interval
increases in width as the SNR decrease, this also happens in the three speaker condition,
however, not nearly as pronounced as in the two speaker condition. This suggests that
the variance of the state-space parameters in the three speaker condition is wrong. It is
suspected that this is a result of the assumption that each of I attentional states can be
model independent.

able to make no prediction. The reason might be that variance of the attentional state
is correct in the two speaker state-space model but that the variances of the attentional
state variables are too low in the I speaker state-space model (this was observable from
Fig. 5.6-5.9). This hypothesis is plausible since the I speaker state-space model assumes
that the state-space variables are independent and furthermore that their variances are
independent. It might be that the evolution of each state-space variable is correctly model
as independent but that their variances should be modelled as independed seems doubtful.

model, however, this approach led to an algorithm that did not convergence and thus the
trivially expansion is not feasible. A feasible method might be to model zi,k as a non-
diagonal Gaussian (hence with a model where the noises are correlated), however, it might
be easier to nd a simple way to account for this (possible) underestimated variance. All
of this should be addressed by further analysis.

a prediction. On one side it is advantageous for neuro-steered hearing aid to get as much

since it nearly always classies correct). On the other hand if the uncertain of a decision is
high it might be better to interpolate on the time-point that had a certain prediction. The
interpolation can be accomplished by implementing a criterion that if a point can not be
assigned with a class (due to low statistical condence) but one (or more) of the previous
point had a statistical certain class then the current unassigned time point should get the
same class as the class of the most recent time point that had a statistical certain class
(this is probability not the optimal way to do the class-assignment-interpolation but it
illustrates the idea).
5.3
Attentional Switch Experiment
In this section, the chance level is 0.5 for both the two and three speaker conditions. The
reason for choosing a chance level of 0.5 in the three speaker condition is that a chance
level of 1/3 imposes a forced choice constrain on the model (a possible explanation for
this is given in Sec. 5.2). These denitions of chance levels are used to investigate the
performance of the state space model from Sec. 4.2 when actually EEG data is feed to the
model. Thus this section will investigate the state space model ability to infer the atten-
tional state in the three conditions of the attentional switch experiment. Recall from Sec.
4.1.1 that condition 1 denotes the two speaker condition where male speaker 1 and female
speaker 1 is spatially located as in the TRF-experiment. Condition 2 denotes the swap
condition of the two speaker case and condition 3 is the condition where three speakers
are present in the auditory scene.
Based on the results from Sec. 5.1 it might be that dierent combinations of envelope ex-
traction method (auditory-inspired or analytic), neural activity extraction method (com-
mon or individual spatial lter) and training paradigm for the TRF (common or mean of
individual TRFs) cause dierent results and hence in each analysis all combination was
tried.
The rst analysis was the multi-trial analysis which uses the state-space model to infer
based on all three trials. The results of the multi-trial analysis with common TRFs and
individual spatial lters are shown in Fig. 5.12 (the results were nearly identical when
the mean of individual TRFs was applied as TRFs). The gure shows the false-alarm
rates versus the hit rates for dierent speakers (each speaker is denoted with a color and
a shape). This plot is often denoted as a Receiver Operating Characteristic (ROC) curve.
Notice that in the two speaker cases (condition 1 and 2), the state-space model nearly
never makes a prediction and that the cluster of points seems to have a mean around
chance. In condition 3, it might seem that the algorithm classies below chance, however,
it is simply because chance is not 0.5 but 1/3. Furthermore, it should be noticed that the
state-space model for I ≥3 has a higher tendency to make prediction, which is expected
from the simulation study (see Sec. 5.2).

in the same window k across trials does not interfere with one another. However, the
model still makes decisions too infrequent to be a real world AAD and furthermore, the
classication accuracy are around chance level across subjects (the cluster of data points
is located around the straight red line).
To increase the frequency of decision making the state space model was forced to make a
choice. The forced choice condition in the two speaker condition is that if pk ≥0.5 then
the state-space model predicts that the attentional state is belonging to male speaker 1
and otherwise the attentional state is belonging to female speaker 1. In the three speaker
condition the forced choice condition is that maxi (pi,k) is the attended speaker.
The

common spatial lters are shown in Fig. 5.15. The forced choice (single trial) analysis
showed that all combination of TRFs and spatial lters led to results similar to those in
Fig. 5.15.

Again it seems that the model classies at around chance level (across subjects) and thus

should be noted that Fig. 5.15 suggest that some subjects performance better than other.
The reason for this is yet to be identied. It might be that there is a correlation between
performance of a subject and his/hers multiple choice performance. However, since the
multiple choice was not performed on a single trial basis it can not be used to obtain
correlation on a single trial basis, which would have been informative.
So far the state space model does not seems to classify above chance level. It might be that
the window length, W, is too short to capture the correlations. To test this the window
length was four-fold from 0.25 sec to 1 sec. The reason for not using a window length of
more than one second is two-folded. The rst reason is that the model assumes that the
attentional state is stationary within each window and that this assumption is likely to
be violated if the window is longer than 1 sec. The second reason is that the numerical
inversion of A is impossible for windows longer than 1 sec. The bug in the code has been
identied to be that the longer window causes the starting point of κi to be out of the
convergence range of the numerical solver. However, increasing κi will currently cause
other parts of the algorithm to fail.
The results of applying longer windows in the case of common spatial lters and the mean
over individual TRFs are shown in Fig. 5.16. All combination of TRFs and spatial lters
led to similar results.

a performance that would allow the model to a real world AAD for them. However, an

Notice that non of the above approaches reach the performance level that [Akram et al.,
2015] did. As discussed in Sec. 6.2 the dierence in performance might be due to the fact
that Akram et al used colocated speech streams and that the current study uses spatial sep-
arated streams. It was shown by [Kerlin et al., 2010] that auditory spatialization induces
a lateralized alpha power suppression, which is though to suppress the neural processing
of task-irrelevant information. Hence, it might be that speech separation introduces a new
set of features (including alpha power lateralization and probably changes in other induced
activities like the gamma power) that the model can not capture since these features are
eliminated by the low-pass ltering. In an attempt to include the alpha power feature all
element of the procedure from Sec. 4 were kept the same, expect from an alteration of the
lowpass lter's cut-o frequency. This cut-o frequency was increased from 8 Hz to 12 Hz.
Recall from Sec. 2.5 that the alpha band is dened as 8-12 Hz.
The analysis where the alpha band was included did not show any clear improvement
across subjects in any setting under the multi-trial analysis, however, it suggested that
there might be dierence between the rst and second condition, hence, that the model
might perform worse in the swap condition. If this is not due to chance (which might be
the case since both condition had a coding performance of around chance level) then it sug-
gests that the inclusion of the alpha band might help capture the spatialization of speech
streams. This tendency was also observed in the single-trial analysis (both with 'short' and
'long' windows) and these single-trials analysis also showed a performance across subjects
similar to the analysis where the alpha power band was excluded. An example of a single-
trial analysis where alpha power is included is shown in Fig. 5.18. Notice from Fig. 5.18
that some subjects never classify below chance level and that the performance of condition
1 is better than condition 2 in most trials for most subjects. Anyhow, the performance
of the model is still to close to chance level (especially across subjects) for it to be a real
world AAD algorithm. If the forced choice constrain was apply (both on 'short' and 'long'
trials) the results were similar to what was observed with the exclusion of the alpha band.
This was expected because other studies (like [Kerlin et al., 2010]) have shown that the
theta band is the band that is best correlated with the inherent modulations of speech and
that the alpha power features are initial and transient.
Another reason that the above approaches do not reach the performance level that [Akram
et al., 2015] did might be that the DSS algorithm does not capture the (stimulus phase
locked) neural activity as well in EEG as in MEG (for a more adequate discussion on this
matter, see Sec. 6.2) and hence causes the empirical correlations to appear too random
for the model to inference the attentional states. To test this another way of nding the
empirical correlations was introduced. Instead using the DSS algorithm to nd the neural
activity, a multi-channel TRF was applied. In this project a multi-channel TRF is dened
as a matrix that has a TRF for each EEG-channels, hence, it is a 64 × Lτ matrix. Based
on these multi-channel TRFs (one for each speaker) a prediction of the neural activity in
each channel can be found (the channel prediction for channel c is found by apply the
cth columns of the multi-channel TRF matrices as the TRFs in Eq. 4.4). This channel
prediction and the actual activity can be epoched and the empirical correlation can be

found in each window of each trail in each channels for each stimulus. This implies that
the empirical correlations can be expressed as θi,c,k,r where i is speaker index, c is the
channel index, k is window index and r is trial index. It is possible to obtain the lowest
empirical correlation across channels as θi,k,r := minc θi,c,k,r. It is assumed that this is the
best estimate to feed to the state-space model, however, it might be that 'lucky' outlier
predictions cause 'wrong' lowest empirical correlations and hence to avoid this it might
be benecial the take the average over the ve channels with lowest empirical correlations
and use this mean as the empirical correlation in the model. The choice of ve channels
are ad-hoc. In further applications one should look into the optimal number of channels
and how to select them.
Actually, two analysis was made based on the above method. The rst method is to nd
the channel(s), c, that gave the smallest θi,c,k,r across speakers i and apply this channel to
all speaker predictions. The other approach was to nd the channels that gave the smallest
θ for each individual speaker i hence introducing the possible of evaluating each speaker
in dierent channels. The analysis showed that both of these approaches led to similar
classication performances, which makes sense since in both approaches the smallest θi,c,k,r
with respect channel c and speaker i will dominate the model's prediction in window k of
trial r.
The multi-trial analysis showed that the common multi-channel TRFs and mean of in-
dividual multi-channel TRFs (both of which obtained with an auditory-inspired envelope
extraction) gave similar results.
Furthermore, the multi-trial analysis showed that the
performances were similar to performance of the approach with spatial lter, however,
with the exception that condition 3 had a lower classication frequency, which seemed to
cause a slight improvement in classication accuracy, however, not nearly at a level where
it makes model real world applicable. Actually, it might be argued from the data (not
shown) that condition 1 is better than condition 2, however, it might be due to chance
since both of them on average across subjects performed at chance level. In the single-trial
analysis it was shown that the classication frequency was decreased markedly and that
the classication accuracy is around the same level (maybe a bit higher but it might be

due to chance) as in the case where the spatial lters were applied. This was supported

6
Discussion
6.1
Improvement to the Experimental Designs
The current experimental designs have at least two problems. The rst problem is that
the experimental design causes the piecewise linear detrending (the linear detrending of
each trial) to introduce artifacts in some trials. These artifacts would not be a problem if
the artifacts were introduced in the part of the data that are not used for actual analysis.
However, in the current versions of the experimental designs the time from the beginning
of recording to the stimulus presentation and the time from the stimulus ends to the
recording stops are both short which does that some of the detrending-artifacts reach into
trials and hence ICA was needed to remove these detrending-artifacts. It was observed
that the time points at which the stop and start triggers are located vary a bit between
trials. This suggest that the TDT is not consistent in the timing of trigger sending or that
there is a problem with eeglab (if it is eeglab (I would suspect the resampling function to
be the problem). In any case it has no implication in this study since the exact positions
of triggers are not essential.
The second problem with the experimental design is that the questions are presented
after each block and not each trial.
This makes it impossible to test whether or not
the behavioural performance was consistent across trial and hence it hard to obtain an
indication of whether or not the subject attended in all trials. Furthermore, since subjects
know in advance that the questions only were presented after each block it might have
caused some subjects to only do a proper top-down attention in one of the three trial
(propably the last trial). Originally, the argument for only presenting questions after each
block was that it was what [Akram et al., 2015] did, however, in retrospect it should have
been changed.
In the current designs ITDs were used to accomplish the auditory spatialization, however,
it might be that HRTFs had been a better choice. The main reason is that some subjects
reported that it was extremely hard to attended to one speaker (especially in the three
speaker condition) because all speech streams were located inside the head of the subject.
This internalization of sounds is a known feature of the ITDs and it can be counteracted by
proper HRFTs because these both spatialize and externalize stimuli. Recall from Sec. 2.3
that the HRTFs depends on the shape and size of the torso, head and pinna and that the
spectral peaks and notches introduced by the pinna primarily is located in the frequency
band above 4000 Hz due to size of the pinna. Thus, instead of lowpass ltering the speech
signals with a cut-o frequency of 4000 Hz it might be benecial to low-pass lter the
107
==PAGE:123==
108
CHAPTER 6.
DISCUSSION
speech signals with a cut-o frequency of 8000 Hz.1 The fact that the HRTFs depend on
the shape and size of the torso, head and pinna make them subject depended. Hence,
even though generalized HRFTs can be obtained (with a test dummy of average human
size) it might be better to obtained the individual HRTFs and use these to generate the
auditory scene. The disadvantages of individual HRFTs are that it is time consuming to
obtain these. Thus, it might be informative to do a study that investigates the eect of
individual HRFTs in comparison to generalized HRFTs to see if the eects of individual
HRTFs counterweight the time it takes to obtain them. In such a study individual HRFTs
and two generalized HRFTs (one for each sex) should be obtained. A possible experiment
would be to look at evoked responses and alpha power topology generated by a 'ba-da-
ga' experiment (the ba-da-ga experiment could be similar to the one by [Mehraei et al.,
2017]) in two dierent conditions. One of the conditions is the application of individual
HRFTs and the other condition is to applied generalized HTRFs. The results of these two
conditions should then be compared to test if there is a signicant improvement in the
features when individual HRFTs are apply. Actually, it might be interesting to include a
ITDs condition to test if the HRFTs generates larger evoked responses and/or alpha power
responses than ITDs.
6.2
Why did Framework by Akram et al (2015) Not Work
on EEG?
6.2.1
Co-located Versus Spatially Separated Speech Streams
A possible explanation for the dierence in classication performance of the model between
the current project and the one by Akram et al (2015) is that Akram et al used co-
located speech streams in their experimental design whereas spatially separated speech
streams were applied in the current experiment designs. This dierence might be a problem
because spatialized speech streams has been shown to activate other parts of the brain
than colocated speech [Michalka et al., 2015]. Furthermore, it might be that other neural
features like alpha power lateralization become as important as the neural phase-locking
to speech modulation in the decoding of the attentional state when the speech streams
are spatially separated and hence in an attempt to easy the task for the subjects the
robust and accuracy of the state-space model suers because it does not have the ability
to incorporate the new neural features. To test if induced activity (the alpha power) is
the reason that the model fails one should nd a way to include it since colocation of
speech streams are uncommon in real life and hence for the model to be an applicable
real world AAD algorithm it must be able to deal with spatially separated speech streams.
One way to include the alpha power would simply be to increase the frequency band under
analysis to include the alpha band, hence changing the cut-o frequency of the lowpass
lter to 12 Hz. This was done in Sec. 5.3 and it showed no clear improvement of the
classication performance of the model. This was expect since [Kerlin et al., 2010] showed
(in s spatialized auditory scene) that the modulation tracking were best correlated with
the theta band (4-8 Hz). Some argue the delta and theta band (1-8 Hz) [Power et al.,
2012] [Ding and Simon, 2012a] [Mirkovic et al., 2015].
1Actually, it might be ecient to apply a cut-o frequency of 4000 Hz because the pinna eects
primarily is used to perceive the elevation of the speech signal [Plack, 2014] and that the elevation
is the same for all speech signals.
6.2 Why did Framework by Akram et al (2015) Not Work on EEG?
==PAGE:124==
CHAPTER 6.
DISCUSSION
109
Another approach (in regards to dealing with spatially separated speech stream) might
be to use the alpha power features to do a channel selection in the multi-channel TRF
approach.
The channel selection would relay on the fact that alpha activity has been
suggested to be a gating mechanism for selective attention and that it works by supressing
the cortical regions that neurally encodes task-irrelavent information [Banerjee et al., 2011].
Hence in our case an increased alpha activity would occur in cortical areas that processes
unattended speech streams. This implies that the subset of channels that have the lowest
alpha powers might be selected for analysis since low alpha power suggest that the cortical
areas (that the electrode captures) are not suppressed by the gating mechanism and hence
an enhancement of the neural processing occurs in these areas, which suggest that these
areas are most likely to processing the attended stream [Banerjee et al., 2011]. To exemplify
the gating mechanism of the alpha power let us assume that a listener is in an auditory
scene with two competing speaker; one located to the left and one to the right. If the
listener is attending the left speech stream then the alpha power increases in left temporal
lobe because the auditory input to the right ear is processed in the contralateral hemisphere
[Banerjee et al., 2011]. The fact that alpha power might be used to do channel selection is
support by nding by [Kerlin et al., 2010] who showed that alpha power had a hemispheric
lateralization at the parietal sites similar to what had been found in visual tasks. This
lateralization does not only indicate the direction of auditory attention to speech but since
it is located the same place in all senses it also supports the idea there is a supramodal
attention mechanism that can be used to mediates spatial segregation of objects from any
sensory modality. Kerlin et al did also show that the alpha lateralization occurred initially
and disappeared before the end of the sentence. The transient nature of the supramodal
attentional mechanism suggests that it is a mechanism that the brain uses to distribute
neural resources to the cortical areas that best processes the incoming sensory inputs.
This was support by the fact that Kerlin et al (2010) showed that the theta band phase-
locking had an initial tuning (might be the supramodal attentional mechanism allocates
the neural resources) that sustained even after the alpha lateralization disappeared, hence
the supramodal attentional mechanism is not needed to maintain attentional focus only to
direction it. If this is the case then it might be that channel selection based on alpha power
should not be based on the alpha power in general but on alpha power when it reaches a
certain level since this might suggest activation of the supramodal attention mechanism
and thus that an attentional switch happening. Furthermore, in time epochs where the
alpha power is low in all channels the selected channels should be the set of channels that
was selected during the last activation of the supramodal attention mechanism.
Some
work must be put into this hypothesis, however, other studies (like [Fritz et al., 2007]
and [Michalka et al., 2015]) have also argued that spatial attention is supramodal. Even
though, the alpha power lateralization seems to be important for channel selection other
factor might also be important. For example, it was shown by [Abrams et al., 2008] that
a strong right-hemisphere dominance exist for the coding of speech envelope and that this
asymmetry occured regardless of the spatial location of the speech stream, even though
contralateral eect (like shown by [Kerlin et al., 2010]) was observable.
The application of alpha power to do channel selection can not be accomplished in the
current attentional switch design because the visual cue and auditory cue are presented
simultaneously and hence even if the alpha activity is an applicable channel selector it
would be impossible to decide whether it was due to the visual or auditory cue because
the supramodal attentional mechanism is transient. This problem could be addressed by
separation the the two types of cues or by discarding the visual cue.
Furthermore, it
should be noticed that the above discussion are limited to top-down auditory attention
6.2 Why did Framework by Akram et al (2015) Not Work on EEG?
==PAGE:125==
110
CHAPTER 6.
DISCUSSION
and hence it might be interesting to look into the possible bottom-up interference in alpha
power introduced by features with high bottom-up saliency (like introducing the name of
the listener in the distractor stream).
6.2.2
Neural Data Obtained with MEG Versus EEG
The biggest dierence between the current framework and the framework by Akram et al
(2015) is that they applied the model on MEG whereas this framework apply the model on
EEG. Theoretically, these two measuring techniques record the same neural activity (see
Sec. C), however, in real applications the EEG signal will be smeared by the skull an eect
not seen (as prominent) in MEG. Hence, in EEG the DSS algorithm might not be able
to obtain optimal spatial lters (the spatial lters nd the neural activity phase-locked to
the modulations of speech) based on only three trials because the data is too noisy. In
contrast, it might be that the optimal spatial lters can be obtained based on only three
trials of MEG data. The fact that the EEG data is too noisy to nd the optimal spatial
lter based on three trials was supported by the discussion in App. A. However, ndings
in Sec. 5.3 showed that the common spatial lters did not improve the performance of
the model (compared to applying individual spatial lters). One reason might be that the
common spatial lters are obtained on TRF data and that the spatial lters change when
multiple speakers are present in the auditory scene. Another reason might be that the
spatial lters actually are subject-depended.
In regards to the DSS algorithm, it should be tested if an increase in number of signal
components, d, from the DSS-algorithm would increase the performance of the proposed
AAD. Furthermore, one should try to nd a way to implement the alpha power features
into the inference of spatial lter. The author believes that the best way to do this is
to change the bias function so that it includes both the phase-locking feature and the
alpha power feature. A possible change in the DSS algorithm could be to apply the 6 rst
step of the DSS algorithm on each of the features individual (hence with an individual
bias function in each features) and nd an appropriate linear combination of the outputs
of these bias functions. However, this would require the construction of a suitable bias
function for the alpha power and a method for nding the best linear combination of
the outputs of the two bias functions. Alternative, one might try to add the alpha band
information into the Dspatial directly. Recall from Sec. 3.7 that Dspatial is a 64 element
vector where each element is the channel weight. Hence, it might be that the alpha power
can be incorporated simply by subtracting or adding (depending on the sign of the weight)
the alpha power of channel c in the element of Dspatial that corresponds to channel c and
then rescale the new spatial lter so that it has the same ℓ2-norm as the original spatial
lter.
6.3
Possible implications with the TRFs of the Forward Model
In Sec. 5.1, it was observed that there might be a dierence in the latency of later com-
ponents of the TRFs between native and non-native speakers. It might be due to the
fact that the cognitive processing of non-native speech is harder (and hence takes longer)
than the cognitive processing of native speech. The intuition is that the later components
of the TRFs seems to reect the later components of the evoked potential and that the
6.3 Possible implications with the TRFs of the Forward Model
==PAGE:126==
CHAPTER 6.
DISCUSSION
111
late components might be related to cognitive processes (based on the multi-channel TRF
topology). If this is true (and is not due to a small sample size) it might not only be
another reason why the framework by Akram et al (2015) did not work on the attentional
switch data but it will also have an impact on the state-space model as an real world AAD
in hearing aid because TRFs of a speaker would change depending on the language that
the speaker is using. To test whether or not the TRFs are nativity depended the following
experiment should be conducted. Let a group of subjects be presented with two block of
stimulus. In each block multiple trials of the same stimulus is presented to the listener. In
the rst block the stimulus is a story spoken in the native language (like Danish). In the
second block the same speaker tales another story this time in a non-native language that
the subject understand uently (like English). In order for this experiment to work the
author suspects that the speaker should be able to generate speech that sound like a native
speaker in both situation. More importantly, this experiment would require that dierent
(short) stories do not generate dierent TRFs if the stories are spoken by the same speaker.
Hence, before the 'native versus non-native'-experiment is conducted it should be tested
that the a speaker generate the same TRF independent of the story he/she tells. The
results of the 'dierent story'-experiment would be important for the application of TRFs
in hearing aids because if a speaker has a robust and unique TRF it would be possible
to store the TRF of that person in the hearing aid or even better on a smart phone. Let
us assume that the hearing impaired listener is a setting where three friends (with known
TRFs) and one unknown possible target are present (the rest of the auditory objects in
the scene are considered noise and are not important for the hearing listener). In such a
scenario it would be smart to have an app that the hearing impaired listener can use to
inform the hearing aid that the three known friends and one unknown person of interest
is present. The app could then send the known TRFs to the hearing aid together with the
information that an unknown TRF should be inferred. This lead to another problem, how
to infer TRFs in an auditory scene. The rst problem is whether or not TRFs obtained in
a competitive auditory scene are identical to those obtained in a non-competitive scene.
Furthermore, if there is a dierence will this dierence then cause a performance dierence
in the competitive auditory scene? -if yes which is best? All of these question must be
addressed in a further study. Lastly, one should investigate whether or not the inference
of TRFs on the individual subjects results in overtting. An indication of this could be
extracted by obtaining the attended (and unattended) TRFs for the subject on the rst
trials in the attentional switch experiment (with a xed regularization parameter, for rea-
son discussed below) and then validate the model with the two following trials.
Currently, another problem with the TRFs is that the procedure for obtaining TRFs are
too slow to apply in an real world AAD. The most time consuming part of the code is the
cross-validation used to nd the optimal regularization parameter and hence it should be
tested whether nor not a xed regularization parameter can be obtained across subjects
and speakers. Furthermore, one should consider changing the method for obtaining the
TRFs. The inference method is currently to use the lasso function in Matlab, however,
it is suspected that the convergence speed of the iteratively re-weighting least squares al-
gorithm proposed by [Ba et al., 2014] is higher than the convergence speed of the lasso
function in Matlab.
It is also interesting to see if the decoding performance of the state space model would be
improved if the length of the TRFs were determined by the method proposed in [Alickovic
et al., 2016]. In this project, they end up showing that 16 parameters in their TRF is
the optimum and since their sampling frequency is 64 Hz, their TRFs ended up being 250
msec long.
6.3 Possible implications with the TRFs of the Forward Model
==PAGE:127==
112
CHAPTER 6.
DISCUSSION
Notice that all of the time-eciency improving suggestions above are not needed if the
TRFs can be obtained prior to application, which is the case if the framework is applied as
a BCI system for control a wheel chair. The framework would be to have four (dierent)
speakers that say either "left", "right", "front" or "back" repeatedly and the attentional
state could then be used to move the wheel chair in the direction that the attended speaker
repeats.
Actually, the TRF analysis suggested that the attentional switch experiment would have
a classication performance at around chance level because the confusion matrices on the
TRF-data showed little evidence that the speakers were separable based on the TRFs.
The problem might be that the TRFs are trained so that the TRF for speaker i gives the
lowest ℓ1-regularized SSE with respect to data of the speaker i condition without incor-
porating the data from other speaker conditions. This seems to be problematic because
the classication accuracy of the state space model strongly depends on the fact that
the empirical correlations are lowest for the attended speaker in the windows where this
speaker is the attentional target and hence the TRFs of unattended speaker should not
give a lower empirical correlation than the target TRF in these windows. This suggest
that another training paradigm might be benecial in respect to improving the classica-
tion performance of the state space model. The training paradigm should acknowledged
correct classications and have a penalty on wrong classications. A optimization criterion
might be based on θi,k,r. For example the criterion could be that if the attentional target
state is i = 1 and θ1,k,r < θj,k,r∀j ∈{2, ..., I} then the training paradigm acknowledges
the window k of trial r and otherwise the paradigm penalties this window of this trial.
Notice, that such a optimization criterion alters the optimization problem completely by
coupling the inference of the dierent TRFs. Hence, one should spend some time nding
an optimization criterion that is easy to solve in a fast manner while simultaneously giv-
ing a high classication performance. The fact that this approach causes the TRFs to be
coupled would probably do that the TRFs must be re-estimated each time the objects of
the auditory scene change because a change in objects probably would due that a new set
of TRFs would lead to an optimal separation (this re-estimation is not a problem if the
state-space model is used as a BCI system for controlling a wheel chair since the speakers
are not changed).
The last consideration in regards to TRFs is that speech perception is governed mostly
by the envelope modulations of the auditory signal whereas music perception is based
mostly on the ne-structure of the signal (see Sec. 2.5). Hence, it might be benecial
to apply a STRF, however, as far as the author knows only MEG studies have been able
to successfully apply STRF, which might be due to the practical dierence between the
two measurement techniques. Hence, it might be interesting to investigate if TRFs are
sucient to separate music from speech and furthermore, whether or not it is of great
important that the algorithm is able to separate music from speech.
6.4
Envelope Extraction
In the current framework, the speech signals are assumed to be known and hence the enve-
lope extraction is easily accomplished by either of the methods from Sec. 4.1.5. However,
in real world applications envelopes are not as trivial to obtain since the speech signals
are not known in advance. It might be possible to generate a single speech detector based
on a noise suppression, like the MWF with VAD introduced in [Das et al., 2016]. Recall
from Sec. 2.6 that the MWF with VAD is able to enhance a single speech stream based
6.4 Envelope Extraction
==PAGE:128==
CHAPTER 6.
DISCUSSION
113
on the many statistically uncorrelated pauses in dierent speech streams. Furthermore, it
was stated in [Das et al., 2016] that the performance of their AAD algorithm (which is
somewhat similar to our state space model (both evaluates the correlation between neural
predictions and actual neural data)), was quite robust to uncorrelated noise in the en-
velopes. Hence, it is sucient to have a noise suppressor (and not a noise cancellator) for
a robust AAD performance.
Even though this noise suppression might be sucient, an alternative approach is to mimic
the use of cross-modality features. An example could be that the spatial locations of speak-
ers are extracted from cameras (hence mimicking vision). More specic, the cameras might
be used generate a map of the surrounding environment, including potential sources and
there characteristics. It could be used to extract stationary sources (e.g. a loudspeaker)
and living objects. The living objects could further be grouped according to their physi-
cal appearance, which would make it possible to extract humans from non-humans. The
humans could then be classied into male/female and adult/child, which would give the
hearing aid the advantage of knowing what sounds to expect from dierent directions.
The hypothesis is that all of these classications could be accomplished by a deep neural
network that used the pictures from the cameras.2 Notice, that the deep neural network
would be computational costly and hence it might be advantages to use the processing
power of a smart phone to increase the battery lifetime of the hearing aids. Besides from
characterizing the surrounding environment, this 'map' of surrounding environment could
also be used to point the noise channelling in the directions of possible targets to obtain
their speech signals and hence their envelopes. It might also help the hearing aid deter-
mining the number of potential targets.
In regard to the envelope extraction, the results in Sec. 5.3 did not show any dierence in
classication performance of the state space model based on extraction methods, however,
this might be due to the fact that both method lead to a classication accuracy at around
chance level. Even though, the auditory-inspired envelope extraction can not be proven to
be advantageous in this AAD algorithm, other AAD algorithms have shown an improve-
ment (like the one by [Biesmans et al., 2016]), hence, a few notes on the auditory-inspired
envelope extraction are given here. The rst note is that it is common to x the compres-
sion coecient even though the auditory system is known to have dierent compression
along the basilar mebrane which does that the 'auditory' lters with a low CF have little
or no compression and auditory 'lters' with a high CF have a clear compression (see
Sec. 2.1.3). This implies that it might be benecial to have dierent compressions to the
outputs of dierent gammatone lters (since the gammatone lters are used to model the
tonotopic properties of the basilar membrane). The second note is that one should look
into the possible advantageous eect of applying the auditory-inspired gammatone wavelet
by [Venkitaraman et al., 2013] to extract the envelopes.
6.5
Other Considerations
Instead of applying the model given in Sec. 4.2 alone it might be advantageous to combine
it with another (slower) models like the AAD proposed by [Biesmans et al., 2016]. This
hybrid could use the high temporal resolution of the state space model to detect attentional
switches while use the model by Biesmansy et al to perform more robust predictions in
2It might be that small cameras on the shoulder or on the ears would be sucient to due the
analysis. These cameras should have to apply to rotate in order to take pictures of the entire scene.

periods of time where the attentional target are kept constant. Acutally, the model by
Biesmansy et al could be applied with an adaptive window size where the adaptation
would depend on the time since the last attentional switching in such a manner that the
longer the time the larger the window size.
It might also be interesting to apply the framework in Sec. 4.2 where the forward model
were to be replaced by a backward model like the one used by [O'Sullivan et al., 2014]
to test if the estimate of the speech envelope based on neural activity would correlated
better with the actual speech envelope than the neural prediction correlated with the
actual neural activity. Notice that the mapping from neural activity to speech envelope
will lead to possible implications similar to the those seen in case of TRFs and the envelope
extraction is still a problem.
Lastly, even though [Akram et al., 2015] showed no improvement by using a higher order
AR model to describe the state-space variable zi,k it might be that the application of such
an approach might increase the performance in EEG. Recall from 3.3, that the higher order

attentional switch data since the simulation study (see Sec. 5.2) seems to lead to the same
conclusions as the simulation study did in [Akram et al., 2015] (at least it did in the case
of the optimization of hyperparameters). However, since the two studies do not show the
same performance in the attentional switch experiment it might be that the attentional
switch data would lead to a higher classication performance if a higher order AR model
was used to model zi,k in the current study.
6.5 Other Considerations
==PAGE:130==
7
Conclusion
The results in Sec. 5.3 showed that the framework by Akram et al (2015) had a decoding
accuracy of around chance level (across subject) when it is applied on EEG data. The
reason might be that the neural recordings are dierent between the two measurement
techniques, however, the dierence in performance might also be due to a dierence in the
experimental designs. In the study by Akram et al the speech streams were co-located
whereas this study used spatially separated speech streams.
The separation of speech
streams might be the reason that the tested state space model fails. The hypothesis is
that the spatialization of speech streams causes the cortical regions that process the task-
relevant information to be altered depending on the location of the attended speaker.
Hence, the spatialization of speakers causes the subset of electrodes that best capture the
task-related neural activities to be altered depending on the location of the attentional
target of the listener. If this hypothesis is true it might have been the reason that the
tested AAD algorithm failed because this algorithm uses the same linear combination of
electrodes independently of the location of the attended speaker. A potential improvement
might hence be to implement an adaptive channel selection into the AAD algorithm. This
channel selection might be accomplished by the alpha activity since this induced activity
is known to increase in cortical areas that are processing task-irrelevant informations.
Another reason that the tested model did not reach a classication performance similar to
the one by Akram et al might be that our TRFs were obtained based on EEG data from
both native and non-native listeners. This is a potential problem because the data seems
to suggest that non-native listeners have longer latencies of the late components of their
TRFs. The current hypothesis for these longer latencies is that the non-native listeners
require an increased cognitive processing to interpret the attended speech stream.
Lastly, the TRF analysis in Sec. 5.1 suggested that another training paradigm to obtain
TRFs might increase the decoding performance of the tested state space model.
It is
hypothesised that this training paradigm should be a coupled training of TRFs that aims
to optimize the separation of a given auditory scene based on empirical correlations between
the neural predictions and the actual neural activity.

A
Analysis of Spatial Filters
In Sec. 3.7 it was stated that prior to applying DSS algorithm the EEG data must be
eye-artifact subtracted (or an ICA must have been applied). The results that lead to this
conclusion is given below together with an analysis of the spatial lters from the data
obtained in the TRF-experiment.
Fig. A.1 is obtained by the algorithm suggested by [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2008] where
the DSS algorithm is apply to a synthesised data-set without eye-artifact subtraction. The
data set is synthesised by mixing a source signal (upper left plot in Fig. A.1), an eye-
artifact signal (lower middle plot in Fig. A.1) and 62 noise signals (one example in lower
left plot of Fig. A.1). The mixing matrix is a triangle for the source signal, a straight
line that goes from 0.4 to 0.7 for the eye-artifacts and it is (uniformly) random for the
62 (Guassian) noises. The EOG is generated as 5 times plus or minus the eye-artifact
channels to which Gaussian noise is added. Both EEG and EOG are spectral ltered with
the FIR low-pass lter and the FIR high-pass lter (shown in App. E.6). The ltered
EEG and EOG data are shown in the upper and lower right plot of Fig. A.1, respectively.
Figure A.1: The upper left plot shows the actual source and the source estimates (for each trial) given by the DSS
algorithm. The upper right plot shows the synthesised 64-channel EEG in which only one source is
present. The lower row of plots shows from left; an example of a noise source, the eye-artifact source
and lastly the vertical and horizontal (bipolar) EOGs. The data is generated as described in the
above text.
In Fig. A.1, it is clear that the eye-artifact is embedded in the neural activity component
(see source estimate for trial one (the red line) in the upper left plot). The reason might
be that the eye-artifact is so large that it can not be projected out of the data by a linear
combination (furthermore no channel was eye-artifact free). To address this problem an
eye-artifact subtracted was applied to the data set prior to the use of the DSS algorithm.
The results of applying the eye-artifact subtraction is shown in the right plot in Fig. A.2.

activity component in Fig. A.2. It is clear that the source estimation is best if the input

Figure A.3: Shows the same as left plot of Fig. A.2 with a dierent actual source.
The eye-artifact subtraction was accomplished in the following way. Data is epoched into
1 sec epochs. Then all epochs containing vertical or horizontal EOGs (measured bipolar)
with an amplitude greater than 70µV is denoted as an eye-artifact contaminated epoch.
Each of these eye-artifact epochs are then classied as either a vertical or horizontal ar-
tifact epoch. The epoch is assigned to the class that has the largest amplitude within
the epoch.
The eye-artifact subtraction is accomplished by rescaling the EOG of the
eye-artifact-epoch to the maximum of each EEG-channel in the eye-artifact-epoch, hence,
==PAGE:138==
APPENDIX A.
ANALYSIS OF SPATIAL FILTERS
123
64 dierent rescaled EOG-epoch is obtained. Each of these recalled EOG-epoch is then
subtracted from the corresponding EEG-channel in the given eye-artifact-epoch.1 Lastly,
since there is random noise in both the EEG and EOG signals, the eye-artifact subtracted
epochs are normalized to the RMS of the epoch without eye-artifacts. The normalization is
accomplished by dividing each eye-artifact subtracted epoch with its RMS and multiplying
it with the RMS of all the non-eye-artifact epochs. The data is then de-epoched to yield
the clean data.
Figure A.4: All columns show in their upper plot one of the EEG-channels before (blue line) and after eye-artifact
rejection (red line). The lower plot of the columns contain a plot of the corresponding DSS-components
where the red line is the rst DSS-component obtained if no eye-artifact rejection were applied to the
EEG data and the blue line is the rst DSS-component if the EEG data were eye-artifact rejected
(Notice, the counter-intuitive colour-coding). The left column is for data in the case of male speaker
1, the middle column is for female speaker 1 and the right column is for male speaker 2.
The next set of test were conducted to cast light on whether or not the spatial lters
were identical across dierent subjects. The tests were performed on the data from the
TRF-experiment prior to the attentional switch experiment. The rst set of results are
shown Fig. A.5. The term common spatial lter means that all data is treated as coming
from one subject (hence all 18 trials is used to obtain the spatial lter). Alternatively,
individual spatial lters are obtain on each subject (hence based on 3 trials).

A.6.
This test showed that there is a large subject-dependency within both
groups (native and non-native English subjects) and hence the subject-dependency is not
necessarily coming from this nativity of the listener. To summarize, the tests in Fig. A.5
and A.6 might suggest that the spatial lters are individual across subject. However, note
that the individual spatial lters are constructed based on only three trials, which might
be a too sample size to nd an appropriate spatial lter. The reason is that an outlier trial
will eect the appearance of phase-locking to stimulus much since one-third of this trials
show a wrong phase-locking. In contrast the common spatial lter was obtained based on
18 trials, which will minimize the eect of outliers and make the spatial lters more robust.

if the bias function is chosen correctly [Särelä and Valpola, 2005]. This implies that (like in ICA)
one should be cautious not to put too much emphasize on the exact topology of the spatial lters.

Figure A.6: This plot shows the common spatial lter (green solid line) together with the mean of the individual
spatial lters from native English subjects (solid red) and Non-native English subjects (solid blue).
All the spatial lters belongs to male speaker 1.
The dashed lines are condence intervals (two
standard deviations).
The common spatial lter relies on the assumptions that the brain regions responsible for
processing the given stimulus are identical across subjects and that the electrodes are
placed over the same brain regions across subjects. Both of these assumptions seems to
be reasonable. An assumption that might be violated is that the electrodes must capture
the same signal and hence has the same noise across subjects.
This violation of this
assumption is mitigated by spherical interpolation of bad channels, however, it is still the
most vulnerable of the three assumptions. If it is shown that the decoding accuracy of the
state space model increases if the common spatial lters (found based on the data from the
TRF experiment) is applied it would have help converting the algorithm into an on-line
system. The reason is that the DSS algorithm requires multiple trials to nd the linear
combination and hence is not applicable in real time implementations. However, if spatial
lters can be obtained prior to the experiment (by using the data of the TRF-experiment)
it would be possible to apply them in an on-line setting. The hypothesis the common
spatial lter is more robust was support by the results in Sec. 5.1.
The idea that common spatial lter seems to be advantageous does that an actual analysis
of native and non-native speakers is not possible on this data set because only 9 trials of
native and non-native speakers are available. Hence, another study should be conducted.
However, I believe that it is more important to study whether or not common spatial lters
are identical to individual spatial lter. A possible way to test this would be to have 10
native subjects (to avoid the possible nativity eect to aect the results) to listen to 15
trials of a 60 sec (native spoken) story and then obtained the individual spatial lters and
the common spatial lter. The 15 number of trials is chosen ad hoc and the literature be
consulted to nd a proper number of trials.
It was also stated in Sec. 3.7 that dierent stimuli generate dierent spatial lters. This
occurs in our data, see upper row of plots in Fig. A.7. Furthermore, this gure shows that
dierent speech stimuli generate dierent DSS-components (see lower plot in Fig. A.7).
One problem with all of the common spatial lters in Fig. A.7 is that they do not show the
stereotypical quadpole pattern of neural activity that [Akram et al., 2015] state that they
should, which might suggest that the common spatial lters are not correct. In addition,
==PAGE:141==
126
APPENDIX A.

Figure A.7: The upper row shows the scalp maps for the common spatial lters in dierent speaker-conditions;
male 1 speaker, female 1 speaker and male 2 speaker, respectively. All scalp maps are generated with
the eeglab function topoplot. The lower plot shows the (across trials) mean of the neural activities
(DSS-components) for one (randomly chosen) sample subject in the three dierent conditions. The

are given dierent o-sets of visualization purposes.
Notice, that dierent speech stimuli lead to
dierent (mean) DSS-components, hence dierent neural activity.
none of the individual spatial lters (with three trials) showed the stereotypical pattern
and hence they might also be unt to do a proper analysis.
A last speculation in regard to spatial lters is whether or not a competitive auditory scene
causes changes to the spatial lters. In other words, do spatial lters change if the auditory
goes from beginning non-competitive to competitive? It is hard to answer with the current
data because the sizes of the data sets (the one from the TRF-experiment and the one from
the attentional switch experiment) are dierent. However, it is still interesting to look at
the spatial lters from the attentional switch experiment. These spatial lters are depicted
in Fig. A.8. It should be noticed from Fig. A.8 that all three conditions lead to the spatial
lters that have similar topology (especially in the common lters). This implies that in all
three conditions the rst DSS-component weights the same areas highly. Notice that areas
with high weights are at the partial lopes near the auditory centers, which makes sense
based on the discussion in [Kerlin et al., 2010]. Fig. A.8 also shows that the individual
spatial lters are dierent across subjects. In further studies it would be interesting to
see if the common spatial lters found on the attentional switch data would increase the
classication accuracy of the state space model from Sec. 4.2. Furthermore, it might also
be interesting to see if the common spatial lters change during each trials. It might be
that the spatial lter that is best in the rst half of condition 1 is dierent from the spatial
lter that is best in the second half of condition 1 and that the spatial lter also depends
on whether or not the listener starts by attending to the male or female speaker (actually,
this was suggest by the prior analysis). To test all of these speculations one must extract
new TRFs based on these spatial lters.

B
More on the State Space Model
B.1
State Space Model for a Two Speakers Condition
The biological consideration and arguments for the auditory-inspired state space model is
given in Sec. 3.4, hence this appendix is primarily a mathematical deviation of the corre-
sponding state space model that can be applied to an auditory scene with two competing
speakers (I = 2). Notice that the forward model is not presented in this appendix because
this part of the state space model is identical to the forward model given in Sec. 4.2.1.
B.1.1
Selective Attention in a Two Speaker Condition:
A
Nonstationary Bernoulli Process
In Sec. 3.1.1, it was stated that in the case of two (attentional) states, these two states
can either be represent by a binary Bernoulli variable or a 1-of-2 scheme. The most math-
ematically convenient approach is the Bernoulli representation and hence in the following
the Bernoulli representation is applied. If all windows k in all trial r are assigned with a
binary variable

B.3
Solving the Nonlinear Equation of the Nonlinear Recur-
sive Filter Using Newton's Method
Newton's method (also known as the Newton-Raphson method) is one of the most widely
used algorithm to nd roots in a one-dimensional function [Press et al., 1992]. Hence to
B.3 Solving the Nonlinear Equation of the Nonlinear Recursive Filter Using Newton's Method
==PAGE:150==
APPENDIX B.
MORE ON THE STATE SPACE MODEL
135
nd the roots of
f(x) = 0
The method requires that both f(x) and its derivative df(x)
dx
can be obtained at any arbi-
trary point x [Press et al., 1992]. If f(x) is linear then the optimum is easily obtained,
however, if f(x) is non-linear an iterative process is needed. Newton's method is an iter-
ative process that is commonly used due to its fast convergence [Press et al., 1992]. The
idea behind Newton's method is to nd the tangent at the current point xi and then nd
the point where the tangent is zero, xi+1. In the next step the tangent at xi+1 is found and
its abscissa-crossing is denoted as xi+2 from which yet another iteration takes place [Press
et al., 1992]. The procedure is illustrated in Fig. B.1.
Figure B.1: This gure illustrates Newton's Method. The bold black curve is f(x). If the initial guess is the blue
dot, then its tangent is blue line. The zero-crossing of this rst tangent (the blue line) is indicated
