C
Measuring Neural Responses
The neurons in the cortex are all bioelectric sources, which give rise to potential distri-
butions. This implies that the neural activity can be investigated through electrophysi-
ologcal signals from extracellular recording. The extracellular recording is used because
it is nearly impossible to record transmembrane potentials and currents in a clinical set-
ting [Transquillo, 2009]. Typically, the electrophysiological signals are obtained by placing
electrodes on the scalp [Plonsey and Barr, 2007]. This scalp recording of brain activity is
called electroencephalography (EEG). In EEG, the electrodes will be placed in a consid-
erable distance from the bioelectric sources (the neurons) that generate the signals. This
implies that a signicant signal transformation must be expected to occur over the distance
from the source to the recording electrode.
The membrane potentials of neurons can be considered as current sources. Hence, the
interpretation of electrophysiological recordings requires understanding of the electrical
behaviour of cells, the conduction of signal throughout the body and the eect of electrode
conguration. In order to explain the electrode potentials, a mathematical theory has
been developed. The mathematical model attempts to establish a relationship between
extracellular potentials and membrane currents by Maxwell's equation that explains how
an electric eld gives rise to the creation of a distribution of electric potentials [Tran-
squillo, 2009].
When these electric elds are generated by neurons they can be called
neuroelectic elds [Williamson and Kaufman, 1990]. The membrane activity contributes
to the potential elds based on the two distinct phenomena in the active neurons. These
two phenomenons are the postsynaptic current and the action potentials. The action po-
tentials are propagating responses, which are generated in the axons. The current of the
action potentials has a time scale on the order of 10 msec. In comparsion, the postsynaptic
potentials are diusive response that takes place in the dendrites of the active neurons. The
diusive characteristic of the postsynaptic potentials does that their current extend over
a relatively long time scales, which can reach several 100 msec [Williamson and Kaufman,
1990]. Another dierence is that the postsynaptic membrane current generate a dipole
moment and the action potential initially generate a (depolarization) dipole moment that
becomes a qaudrupol relatively fast (on the order of few msec) [Williamson and Kaufman,
1990]. Mathematically, it can be shown that the electric eld generated by a dipole atten-
uates as one over distance squared and that eld by a quadrupole attenuates as one over
distance cubic. Eectively, this strong attenuation of a quadrupol's eld will do that only
the eect of initial dipole of the action potential is observable in the far-eld potential
and that the bioelectric sources must be models as dipole. The (commonly used) dipole
model (based on Maxwell's equation) shows that the eect of each neuron is depending in
its orientation and how far into the brain it is located. If the dipole moment is orientated
139
==PAGE:155==
140
APPENDIX C.
MEASURING NEURAL RESPONSES
orthogonal to the radial axis its contribution to the extracellular potential will be zero.
Notice, that the model is only an approximation of the real system. One of multiple limi-
tation of the model is that it does not correct for the fact that the scalp is an insulation
boundary (no ux over this boundary), which hence causes a perturbation potential.
As mentioned an EEG is the recording of extracellular potentials with electrodes on the
scalp and hence the time series of the EEG represent the neural activities [Seeley et al.,
2010]. The time series in the EEG is called evoked potential (EP) or event-related po-
tential (ERP) if the electrical responses of the nervous system are elicited by a sensory
stimulus like a sound [Williamson and Kaufman, 1990]. If the ERP is generated by an
auditory stimulus it is called auditory evoked potential (AEP) and the measurement will
sometimes be referred to as an electric response audiometry (ERA). The ERA are divided
into three major classes [Winer and Schreiner, 2011] [Crumley, 2011]:
1. Electrocochleography
2. Brainstem response audiometry
3. Cortical response audiometry
The electrocochleography, ECoG, measures the electrical activities of the cochlear.
It
measures the receptor potentials, presynaptic potentials and the action potentials in the
auditory nerve. The time scale of the ECoG is on the scale of a few msec poststimulus
[Crumley, 2011].
The brainstem response audiometry measures the auditory brainstem responses (ABRs).
Figure C.1: This gure sketches the ABR, MLR and ACR in response to a click.
The gure is from
www.researchgate.net.
Generally, the click-evoked ABR waveform will consists of seven peaks that all occur within
10 msec poststimulus [Dau, 2003], see Fig. C.1. The rst peak in the ABR corresponds
the largest peak in the auditory nerve response. An important feature of the ABR is that
it is independent of attention [Winer and Schreiner, 2011]. The time scale of the ABR
indicates that it measures the action potentials in the brainstem.
The cortical response audiometry dierent from the previous subdivisions of ERAs because
==PAGE:156==
APPENDIX C.
MEASURING NEURAL RESPONSES
141
the cortical responses are results of postsynaptic activities.
The cortical responses are
subdivided into the middle-latency response (MLR) and the long-latency response (LLR).
The MLR is a sequency of lower amplitude AEP deection in the time range from 12 to
50 msec poststimulus. The deection can be seen in Fig. C.1. The N0-peak occurs at
around 8 msec poststimulus and it is believed to be a result of sub-cortical acitivity. The
most reliably recorded MLRs are the Na-peak (that has a latency of around 15-25 msec
poststimulus) and the Pb-peak (that occur at around 25-30 msec poststimulus) [Winer
and Schreiner, 2011]. The MLRs are assumed to be associated with the primary auditory
cortex while the LLRs are assumed to be a result of activity in the secondary auditory
cortex [Winer and Schreiner, 2011]. The LLR is the most extensively studied exogenous
AEP waveforms [Winer and Schreiner, 2011] and it also these deection that are commonly
used in brain-computer interfaces based on auditory evoked potentials, especially the P300
(that in Fig. C.1 is denoted as P2) [Amiri et al., 2013]. The LLR consists of four deection;
P1, N1, P2 and N2, see Fig. C.1. The N1 is also known as N200 and has been shown
to have a latency of up to 200 msec [Schreuder et al., 2010] [Cai et al., 2012]. The last
LLR deection is a positive deection, which occours at around 300-500 msec poststimulus
according to [Schreuder et al., 2010]. [Schreuder et al., 2010] denotes this positiv deection,
the P300 [Schreuder et al., 2010]. However, the Fig. C.1 has no positiv deection in these
range, instead it has a positive deection around 150200 msec post-stimulus and it is
denoted P2. This P2 is consistent with the model by [Choi et al., 2013]. "Although study
of the LLR has been invaluable in probing the physiology of auditory cortex, the signicance
ascribed to changes in its waveform, especially N1, are often overinterpreted" [Winer and
Schreiner, 2011]. The MLR and LLR potentials are sensitive to attention.
In this study a multi-channel EEG is used to obtain the cortical responses. The reason for
using multiple electrodes is that each electrode (on the scalp) predominately record the
neural activity of the cerebral cortex in its proximity. This can be argued by the dipole
model. However, the dipole model also suggested that the orientation of the neurons is
important. The pyramidal neurons are organized in a dense layers radially to the surface
of the cortex [Seeley et al., 2010] and hence (based on the dipole model) most of the neural
activities recorded with EEG come from the postsynaptic activities of pyramidal neurons
(notice that both excitatory or inhibitory responses generate a dipole moment). The AEP
are generated when the pyramidal neurons become highly synchronized in response to the
auditory stimulus [Pfurtscheller and da Silva, 1999]. The more active and synchronized
the pyramidal neurons are the larger the AEP [Transquillo, 2009].
The fact that the
EEG signals are correlated to the activity of the cortical regions just below the electrode
implies that the placement of the electrodes are important [Transquillo, 2009].
There
exists multiple electrode congurations but the most widely used is the 10/20 international
system [Roman-Gonzalez, 2012], see gure C.2. Each electrode in the 10-20 conguration is
assigned with a letter and a number. The letter identies the inferior lobe and they are used
to specify the coronal planes. The letters are Fp, F, T, C, P, and O which are abbreviations
for prefrontal, frontal, temporal, central, parietal, and occipital respectively (notice that
there anatomically does not exist a central lobe). The numbers are used to specify the
sagittal plane. Notice that electrodes in the medial plane have the 'number' z [Roman-
Gonzalez, 2012]. The numbering is done so that uneven numbers belong to electrodes
on the left hemisphere and even numbers belong to electrodes on right hemisphere. The
number will increase laterally.
Generally in a clinical setting, the EEG recording is done in an electrically and magnetically
shielded environment to minimize external noises.
The process of obtaining an three-
electrode EEG is depicted in Fig. C.3. The EEG recording starts at the electrodes that
==PAGE:157==

Figure C.2: This gure illustrates the 10/20 international system on a 64 + 2 EEG-cap from BioSemi. The gure
is from www.biosemi.com.
transduce the ionic current of the brain to the electric current in the wires (an ionic current
is carried by ions and an electric current is carried by electrons [Transquillo, 2009]). In
EEG, the most common electrodes are the silver/silver chloride electrodes (An active form
of Ag/AgCl electrodes are used in the Active BioSemi system www.biosemi.com). The
transducing function is that the electrical conductors Ag is coated with a nonsoluble layer
of the salt AgCl. This nonsoluble layer is in constant with aqueous ionic solution and
hence a redox reaction occurs at this interface [Neuman, 2000]. The Ag/AgCl electrode
is a perfect nonpolarizable electrode, which means that current can pass freely across
the electrode-electrolyte interface and that it has a resistive impedance that is nearly
frequency independent (especially at low frequencies) [Neuman, 2000]. When placing the
electrode there will be an air-lled space between the electrode and the skin. Hence, to
establish contact between the skin and the electrode an electrolyte gel is lled into the
space between them [Neuman, 2000] [Transquillo, 2009]. This electrolyte gel will besides
for establishing constant also reduce the impedance of the skin by moisturing it. Besides
from the benets from the nonpolarizable characteristic of the Ag/AgCl electrodes, it is
commonly utilized as scalp electrodes in EEG recording because they have fast electrical
responses and that they are relative cheap while being compatible with biological tissues
[Transquillo, 2009]. With passive Ag/AgCl electrodes the impedance of the electrodes
should be as low as possible, however, with active Ag/AgCl electrodes (like those apply in
the ActiveTwo Biosemi system) the impedance of the electrodes are not important due to
the construction of the electrodes. The electrodes have individual ampliers, which do that
the system tolerates high electrode impedances www.biosemi.com (the exact mechanism

In the ActiveTwo Biosemi system, a common mode signal is used to eliminate the use of
an ground electrode. The common mode signal is the average potential of the subject and
its main purpose is to prevent power line noise from interfering with the bioelectric signals
www.biopac.com. In the ActiveTwo Biosemi system the common model signal is recorded
with a combination of two electrodes the Common Mode Sense (CMS) active electrode
and the Driven Right Leg (DRL) passive electrode www.biosemi (see Fig. C.2 for their
placement). The CMS and DRL electrodes form a feedback loop that drives the common
mode voltage as close to the reference voltage in the AD-box as possible. The AD-box
receives current from subject by the CMS electrode and it transmits current to the subject
via the DRL electrode. The return current is limited electronically at 50 µA to prevent
hazards to the subject due to amplier and/or electrode defects. The feedback loop result
in a 40 dB extra common mode rejection at 60 Hz compared to a normal ground electrode
with the same impedance www.biosemi.com.
Beside from the common mode rejection
some sort of referencing is needed. In the case of an unipolar setup one reference electrode
will be used as reference for the remaining electrodes www.biopac.com.
The reference
electrode must be placed in a region where the noise features (of the active electrodes) are
present while the target signals (of the active electrodes) are greatly reduced or absent. If
these constrains (on the reference area) are obeyed the output of a dierential amplication
between the electric potential at the active electrode and at the reference electrode yields
an amplied voltage of the target signal [Williamson and Kaufman, 1990]. Hence, the
role of the reference electrode is to record the background activities so that these activitie
can be subtracted from the recording signal [Transquillo, 2009]. Typically, the reference
electrode is placed in an electrically connected region of the tissue, however, with a certain
==PAGE:159==
144
APPENDIX C.
MEASURING NEURAL RESPONSES
distance to the recording electrode(s) [Transquillo, 2009]. A common reference 'electrode'
is the average between electrodes on the earlobes www.biosemi.com. The reason that the
earlobes (nose and mastoids) often are utilized as the reference 'electrode is that they
are close to ideal at capturing the global voltage-changes while not capturing the target
signal [Roman-Gonzalez, 2012] [Al-ani and Trad, 2010].
In EEG, the magnitude of the signals are small because of low electrical conductivities of
both the skull and scalp [Transquillo, 2009]. Hence the dierential amplier must have a
gain that enhance the magnitude of the EEG signal properly [Williamson and Kaufman,
1990]. After the amplication of the neural recording, they are generally passed through
an analog bandpass lter to reduce environmental noise and motion artifacts [Williamson
and Kaufman, 1990]. Afterwards, they are digitalized (by an A/D-converter) so that they
can be stored on a computer for further signal processing and analysis [Transquillo, 2009].
In regards to measuring the neural activity, it should be mention that neurons will not
only generate electric potentials, they will also generate magnetic elds. The magnetic
counterpart to a neuroelectric elds are known as a neuromagnetic eld [Williamson and
Kaufman, 1990]. In physics, Biot and Savart's law is used to describe the magnetic eld
that a dipole generates. The law states that the magnetic eld lines are in a plane that
is orthogonal to direction of the dipole moment. Hence, theoretically the neuromagnetic
and neuroelectric elds are generated by the same neural activity which might suggests
that MEG and EEG essentially record the same neural activities. However, practically
one should expect that the skull and brain tissue interfere with the two types of elds
dierently.
==PAGE:160==
D
Speech Production
Sound waves are pressure uctuations, which are produced by a sound sources. It is known
that if a glass is struck it produces a sound wave that has a frequency equal to the resonant
frequency of the glass. The resonant frequency is determine by the mass and stiness of
the glass [Plack, 2014]. Many natural object has a resonant frequency [Plack, 2014]. In the
cases of tonal musical instruments and human vocal there are multiple vibrating sources,
which lead to the production of a complex tones.
Speech production starts when air is pressed out of the lungs and through the larynx. The
larynx direct the pressure waves through the mouth cavities and/or the nose cavities and
the sound can from these cavities radiate out through the mouth and/or nose [Borden
and Harris, 1980]. In the larynx two bands of muscular tissue are located and these are
denoted as the vocal folds. The vocal folds will vibrate at a resonance frequency of ap-
proximately 120 Hz for males and approximately 220 Hz for females, however, there is a
big individual variation in the resonance frequency of the vocal folds [Plack, 2014]. The
vibration of the vocal folds will produce a complex tone that has a rich set of harmonics.
The fundamental frequency of the complex tone is the resonance frequency and it is the
resonance frequency that is percieved as the pitch of the speaker [Plack, 2014]. As men-
tioned the resonance frequency depends on the mass and stiness of the object, hence the
fundamental frequency can be varied by increasing or decreasing the stiness of the vocal
folds, which is important for the production of dierent voiced sounds [Plack, 2014]. It is
important to understand that not only the vocal folds modies the air owing of the lungs.
The structures in the pharynx, mouth and nose (collectively denoted as the vocal tract)
will also modify the outowing air. The vocal tract consists of mobile articulators such
as the pharynx, soft palate, lips, jaw, and tongue, which can be controlled to modify the
sound coming from the larynx. It is these modication that gives dierent speakers their
characteristic timbres [Plack, 2014]. The fact that the articulators are mobile does that
the resonance frequencies of the vocal tract can be altered by changing the conguration
of the articulators, mainly the tongue. The pronounced resonances produced by a given
conguration of articulators will lead to a specic transfer function for the vocal tract.
This implies that the spectrum of the sound radiated from the mouth and/or noise can
be obtained by multiplying the complex tone from the larynx with the transfer function
of the vocal tract. The process is schematically illustrated in Fig. D.1. It can be seen
that dierent transfer functions will lead to dierent peaks (called formants) in the speech
spectrum. These formants are important because they leads to the perception of dierent
vowel sounds. The articulators can also cause constrictions in the vocal tract. These con-
striction will restrict or prevent the release of air which are perceived as consonants [Plack,
2014]. Hence, human speech is essentially a sequence of vowel sound (complex tones) that
145
==PAGE:161==
146
APPENDIX D.
SPEECH PRODUCTION
Figure D.1: Schematic illustration of the speech production. The left gure shows the magnitude spectrum of the
sound wave just before entering the vocal tract with the vocal cord tuned to a specic fundamental
frequency. The middle gure shows the transfer function of the vocal tract at a specic setting of
the mobile articulators. The right plot shows that magnitude spectrum of the sound radiated from
the mouth and/or noise under the given circumstances.
The gure is a modication of a gure
from [Borden and Harris, 1980].
are interrupted by consonants.
As mentioned the magnitude spectrum of each vowel sound will have a specic set of
formates depending on the placement of the articulators. These formates are ordered as
F1, F2, F3, ... where F1 is the formate with the lowest frequency. In general free-owing
speech, the articulators will move almost constantly because the formants needed to pro-
duce dierent speech sound are dierent and hence the congurations of the articulators
will be dierent. These formant transitions are continuous. The constant transition of
formation does that the vowel sound spectrum is not stable since it depend on the previ-
ous speech sound [Plack, 2014]. This phenomenon is called coarticulation, which simply
means that sound waveform of the current phoneme is heavily depending on the phonemes
before and after (in the following, phonemes will be indicated by a slash before and after a
letter or a symbol). An example of coarticulation is given in Fig. D.2. The coarticulation
implies that consonants shapes the vowel sound spectrum.
Figure D.2: Schematic illustrations of the formate transi-
tions that occurs due to coarticulation. The
gure shows the formate transitions during
the utterances 'ba' and 'da'. Notice that the
vowel is the same in both utterances.
The
gure is from [Plack, 2014].
As mentioned, consonants are related to
the restriction of the outow of air from
the vocal tract. The consonants are clas-
sied according to how the restrictions of
the outow is generated. Stop consonants
(or plosives, e.g. /p/ and /t/) are gener-
ated when the outow is completely con-
stricted so that no air outux can occur.
If the constrictions are very narrow frica-
tives (e.g.
/s/ and /v/) are generated.
Fricatives is a noisy burst of sound. The
third type of consonants are produced by
partial constrictions (e.g. /w/ and /r/)
and they are denoted as approximants.
Lastly, nasals (e.g. /m/ and /n/) are gen-
erated by only allowing airow through
the nasal passages, which happens when
the soft palate is lowered [Plack, 2014].
The constrictions of the vocal tract that lead to the production of consonants cause the
sound pressure level of the speech to decrease and hence the consonants lead to temporal
==PAGE:162==
APPENDIX D.
SPEECH PRODUCTION
147
modulations in the sound pressure level. The dierence between the utterances 'say' and
'stay' is a brief (approximately 10 msec) silent gap after the phoneme /s/. In other to dis-
tinguish utterances the auditory system must exihibit a high temporal resolution, which is
also the case. It has been shown that the human are able to detect level changes of about
2-5 ms [Plack, 2014].
The formant transitions and level uctuations associated with consonants implies that
speech is dynamic. Furthermore, due to the periodicity at which consonants and vowels
occurs much speech will be voiced and periodic [Plack, 2014]. Especially, the periodicity is
important for the auditory system during speech extraction and speech segregation [Plack,
2014]. It has been showed that speech intelligible is preserved at a rate of 400 words/minute
which is equivalent to 30 phonemes/second, which support that the human auditory sys-
tem has a remarkable temporal resolution [Plack, 2014]. In general, english speech will
have a syllable rate of approximately 4-10 Hz [Plack, 2014] [Giraud and Poeppel, 2012].
The temporal uctuations in the sound pressure level of the speech that are associated
with the pronunciation of dierent syllables will introduce slow temporal variation in the
amplitude of the sound wave. These slow temporal variation of the speech is known as
speech envelope. The frequency of the speech envelope is the denoted as the envelope
frequency (or the modulation frequency). The auditory system is highly sensitive to these
low frequency modulation of sound and it can detect amplitude modulation with a very
low modulation depths [Plack, 2014]. Furthermore, it has been shown that modulation
frequencies up to 16 Hz contributed to speech intelligibility in conditions where there is
noise interference [Plack, 2014]. The modulation frequencies are important for speech seg-
regation when multiple speakers talk simultaneously the reason is that dierent speech
streams have dierent rates of envelope uctuations [Plack, 2014].

APPENDIX D.

E
Temporal Filtering
Typically, EEG signals are contaminated with noise. Generally, the noise is assumed to
be white (See Sec. 3.2 for the mathematical denition of white noise). The assumption
that the noise is white does that the noise n(t) has an innity bandwidth (contain all
frequencies) whereas the target signal, st(t), (commonly) is band-limited. This implies
that the contaminating noise, n(t), can be reduced in the observed (contaminated) signal,
s(t), if the frequencies outside the target signal's frequency band are removed or reduced.
However, to do this suppression of non-target frequencies, one will need to represent the
signal in the frequency domain.
E.1
Fourier Transform
The denition of the Fourier transform is [Christensen, 2010]
S : R →C by S(f) =
Z ∞
−∞

should noticed that the spectrum of the Fourier transform is continuous in frequency and
that S(f) is a continuous complex function that can be split into a continuous magnitude
|S(f)| and a continuous phase θ(f). Notice, that the temporal information of the signal is
lost in the both Fourier domains.
In signal processing it is common to interprete the Fourier transform as an operator, F.
The Fourier transform is a linear operation [Lathi, 2009] [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007].
The linearity implies that the superposition property holds hence if s1(t) and s2(t) are two
non-periodic signals then
F (αs1(t) + βs2(t)) = αS1(f) + βS2(f)
(E.2)
It can also be shown that a time-shifting of the input signal, s(t), causes a linear phase
shift in frequency, hence
F(s(t −t0)) = e−j2πft0S(f)
(E.3)
which implies that the magnitude spectrum is unchanged but the phase is altered from θ(f)
to θ(f) −2πft0. Similarly, a modulation in the time domain will lead to a frequency-shift
F(ej2πf0ts(t)) = S(f −f0)
(E.4)

Another important property is the time-scaling properties
F(s(t/a)) = |a|S(af),
a ∈R \ 0
(E.5)
Eq. E.5 implies that a compression in the time-domain yields an expansion in the frequency
domain whereas an expansion in the time-domain causes a compression in the Fourier
domain. This property is known as the time-frequency scaling property.
The Fourier transform also has a duality property
F(s(t)) = S(f) ⇒F(S(−t)) = s(f)
(E.6)
The last property (mentioned here) is known as the convolution theorem. If s1(t) and s2(t)
are two non-periodic signals then
F((s1 ∗s2)(t)) = F((s2 ∗s1)(t)) = S1(f)S2(f)
(E.7)
where ∗is the convolution operator [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007].1 Eq. E.7 shows that
convolution in the time domain becomes a multiplication in the frequency domain. Notice,
that Eq. E.7 utilizes the fact that (s1 ∗s2)(t) = (s2 ∗s1)(t) [Christensen, 2010]. The
convolution theorem also states that [Lathi, 2009]
F−1((S1 ∗S2)(f)) = F−1((S2 ∗S1)(f)) = s1(t)s2(t)
(E.8)
hence, a convolution in the frequency domain becomes a multiplication in the time domain.
The application of the Fourier transform to periodic signals might give some problem
since a periodic signal has discrete frequencies whereas the Fourier transform shows the
frequencies content as a continuous distribution.
To account of this the dirac impulse
function, δ(t), is introduced. The impulse function is dened as

s(t)δ(t −t0) = s(t0)δ(t −t0)

E.2
Analog system
In signal processing a system is anything which operates on a signal. A common example
is a lter [Lathi, 2009]. An analog system operates on signals that are continuous in both
time and amplitude. Such a lter is not applicable in a computer since computers only can
handle digital signal (signals with discritized in both time and amplitude). However, the
analog systems are important for two reason. Firstly, in hardware it is possible to make
analog lters. Secondly, the analog lters might be useful in the generation of digital lters
(see App. E.8).
Both digital and analog system have some characteristics that are be used to classify
systems. The rst characteristic is whether or not the system D is linear. The system is
linear if and only if the superposition principle holds. The second characteristic is whether
or not the system is time-invariant. The system is time-invariant system if and only if
a time-shifted of the input causes the output to be time-shifted by the same amount of
time [Madsen, 2008] [Lathi, 2009]. The third common characteristic is whether or not the
system is causal (physically feasible). A system is causal if its output at any time point
only depends on the present and past values of the system inputs (and outputs) [Madsen,
2008]. The last characteristic of system is whether or not it is stable (the theory behind
stability are given in App. E.4). In this project only stable Linear Time-Invariant (LTI)
systems are implemented and the theory below regards only stable LTI systems.
When describing a stable LTI system there are multiple possibilities. Firstly, a system can
be described by an input-output equation that shows the relationship between the inputs
and outputs [Lathi, 2009] [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007]
y(t) = −
N
X

aky(t −k) +
M

k=0
bkx(t −k)

E.3
Sampling and Digital Signals
Most signal encountered in practice are analog in nature, including the signal from the
brain [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007]. However, computers and digital signal processors can
E.3 Sampling and Digital Signals
==PAGE:168==
APPENDIX E.
TEMPORAL FILTERING
153
only analysis and/or processes digital signals. Hence, to utilize the digital computational
power the analog signal must be converted into a digital signal. The digitalization process
has two steps. The rst step is sampling, which discretize the signal in time. The second
step is the discretization of the signal's amplitude, which is accomplished by a process called
quantization. A device that performs the sampling and the quantization is known as an
Analog-to-Digital (A/D) converter. The main concern when designing the quatization step
is to minimize the loss of accuracy in the signal amplitude while keeping the computational
memory (needed to storage the data) low.
Typically, 8-bit is used to store each digit
[Proakis and Manolakis, 2007]. The sampling process is to obtain a sampled signal, ss(t),
from analog signal, sa(t), which is archived by
ss(t) = sa(t)
∞
X
n=−∞
δ(t −nTs) = sa(t)i(t) =
∞
X
n=−∞
sa(nTs)δ(t −nTs)
where sa(nT) := s(n) is the samples of sa(t) and i(t) is the Ts-periodic impulse train.
Utilizing Eq. E.11 and the shifting property of the impulse function yields
Ss(f) = Sa(f) ∗I(f) = fs
∞
X
k=−∞
Xa(f −kfs)
Hence, Ss(f) is a linear combination (where all coecients are equal to one) of periodically
shifted copies of Sa(f). Notice, that the periodically shifted copies of Sa(f) will overlap if
fs is not at least 2 times the bandwidth of sa(t). This leads to Nyqeist' sampling theorem,
which state that if sa(t) has a bandwidth, B, (the bandwidth is the highest frequency in the
spectrum of the signal) then the sampling rate fs must satisfy fs ≥2B to avoid spectral
aliasing [Madsen, 2008] [Lathi, 2009]. If spectral aliasing occurs it is impossible to obtain
the original analog signal sa(t) from the sampled signal x(n) [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007].
Actually, the sampling frequency must be higher than 2B to avoid spectral aliasing (in
practical scenarios) because an ideal low-pass lter can not be generated in practise [Proakis
and Manolakis, 2007].
Recall that the contaminating noise typically is assumed to be white and hence to have an
innite bandwidth [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007] [Lathi, 2009]. This innite bandwidth
will lead to spectral aliasing, so to overcome this problem an anti-aliasing lter can be
applied before sampling. The anti-aliasing lter is a low-pass lter that limit the bandwidth
of the signal so that it obeys Nyquist' theorem. It should be noticed that the anti-aliasing
lter causes an approximation of sa(t) denoted as ˆsa(t) and that only this approximation
can be perfectly reconstruct. Hence, it will still be impossible to reconstruct sa(t) perfectly
from the samples of ˆsa(t), however, as long as the bandwidth of the target signal is below
the cut-o frequency of the anti-aliasing lter it does not matter since the part of sa(t)
that are not reconstructable is noise. In general, the anti-aliasing lter is always applied
before sampling. The cut-o frequency of the anti-aliasing lter is always smaller than
half the sampling frequency to relax the roll-o characteristics of lter that is used to
bandlimit the signal [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007] (for more information see App. E.5).
The specic choice of fs/2 should be as low as possible to minimize the computational
memory required to store the data, to reduce the computational power required to process
the data at high-speed, etc. [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007].

E.4
Digital System
In the analysis of digital signal and digital system it is often desirable to have a denition
of the digital version of the dirac impulse function. The digital impulse function δ(n) is
dened as
δ(n) =
(
1
for n = 0
0
for n ̸= 0
which is also known as the Kronecker delta function [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007] [Mad-
sen, 2008].
Another important analysis tool, when dealing with digitial systems, is the Z-transform.
The Z-transform is the digital eqviualent to the Laplace transform in the analog do-
main [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007]. The Laplace transform is a generalization of the
Fourier transform and the Laplace transform is dened as
L (s(t)) = S(x) =
Z ∞
−∞
s(t)e−xtdt
where x := σ +j2πf = σ +jω, which is a complex number. From this denition, it is clear
that the Fourier transform can be obtained from the Laplace transform by evaluated the
Laplace transform at σ = 0 hence L (s(t)) |x=j2πf = F (s(t)). The Laplace domain lead to
another representation of an analog signal which is
H(x) = Y (x)
S(x)
where H(x) is known as the transfer function of the analog system. The transfer function
will have a set of the roots in both the denominator and the nominator. The roots of
the nominator is called zeros and the roots of the denominator is called poles, p [Madsen,
2008] [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007] [Lathi, 2009]. This implies that the roots of S(x) are
poles and the roots of Y (x) are zeros. A system is asymptotically stable (or simply stable)
if all poles are located in the left half plane. The system is marginal stable if no poles
are located in the right half plane but it has one or more roots on the jω-axis that all
have algebraic multiplicities equal to one. If the system is either asymptotically stable or
marginal stable, it is unstable [Lathi, 2009].
The relation between the Z-transform and the Laplace transform is that all points in the
x-plane with σ < 0 are mapped within the unit circle in the z-plane and that all points in
the x-plane with σ > 0 are mapped outside the unit circle in the z-plane. Lastly, all points
with σ = 0 are mapped onto the unit circle in the z-plane [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007].
The z-transform is given by Laurent series [Madsen, 2008] [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007]
Z(s(n)) = S(z) =
∞
X
n=−∞
s(n)z−n
The Z-transform is dened for ∀z ∈C for which the Laurent series is convergent [Madsen,
2008]. The relation between the Z-transform and Laplace transform indicates that a system
dened by H(z) is asymptotically if all poles has a magnitude less than one (|p| < 1). The
system will be marginal stable if all poles are less than or equal to one under the contrained
that all poles in the set {∀p | |p| = 1} has algebraic multiplicity equal to one [Proakis and
Manolakis, 2007] [Madsen, 2008]. The relationship between the x-plane and z-plane also
indicates that the frequency response of the system can be obtained by evaluation the

dealing with the Z-transform) one should indicates its regions of convergence [Proakis

The region of convergence of a given Z-transform is important
because two digital signals can have the same Z-transform but with dierent regions of
convergence [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007]. Furthermore, the region of convergence makes
it easy to check whether or not the (discrete-time) Fourier transform of the signal exists
since the (discrete-time) Fourier transform exists if and only if z = 1 are included in the
region of convergence [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007]. Lastly, it should be mentioned that
the properties of the Z-transform are similar to those of the Fourier transform [Proakis and
Manolakis, 2007] [Madsen, 2008]. For example, it can be shown that Z-transform is linear
and that Z(s(n −k)) = z−kS(z) where z−k is known as k-sample delay operator [Proakis
and Manolakis, 2007] [Madsen, 2008]. Notice, that the second property is the Z-transform
equivalent to the time-shift property of the Fourier transform.

E.5
The Considerations in Regards to Designing Digital Fil-
ters
As mentioned in the introduction of this section, temporal ltering is an ubiquitous step
in the preprocessing of EEG (and MEG) data in order to attenuate the noise component
of the recorded signal. However, the lter can also lead to various unintended adverse
lter eect. In the following we will discuss how to counteract some of these adverse lter
eects.
Recall that ideal lters are physically unrealisable for real-time signal processing applica-
tions. The reason is that ideal lters are non-causal. It can be shown that a lter is causal
if and only if the frequency response H(F) of the lter are limited to being zero at a nite
set of points in the frequency range [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007], which is not the case for
an ideal lter. An example of an realisable lter is given in Fig. E.1. Another problem with
ideal lters is that their magnitude spectrum have an innitely sharp cut-o from pass-
band to stop-band, which is not implementable in practice [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007].
In the case of a LTI system, the lter design process is the procedure of determining {ak}
and {bk} in the lter's transfer function so that the frequency response of the lter closely
approximating the desired frequency response specications. The degree to which the mag-
nitude spectrum of the lter approximates the specications depends partly on the number
lter coecient N +M and the criterion used to obtain the M +N coecients [Proakis and
E.5 The Considerations in Regards to Designing Digital Filters
==PAGE:172==
APPENDIX E.
TEMPORAL FILTERING
157
Manolakis, 2007]. Furthermore, it depends on the specications. Typically, the specica-
tion regards the magnitude spectrum of the lter. In Fig. E.1 it can be seen that a realiz-
able lter has pass-band ripples (denoted as δ1) and stop-band ripples (denoted as δ2) and
lastly that a realizable lter has a transition band (ωs−ωp), which is absent in an ideal lter.
The lter specications is hence that size of transition band and the amplitudes of δ1 and δ2.
Figure E.1: Magnitude spectrum of a physically realizable low-pass lter.
The gure is taken from [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007].
The rst subject for discus-
sion is the transition band or
more specic the lter roll-
o, which is the slope of the
transition band.
The lter
roll-o is related to the tran-
sition band in such a way that
a narrowing of the transition
band causes a steeping of the
lter roll-o [Widmann et al.,
2015]. Notice that the steeper
the roll-o the better sepa-
ration of adjacent frequency
bands.
However, the lter
roll-o is a function of the l-
ter order, where the lter order is dened as the number of lter coecients (the lter
length minus one) [Widmann et al., 2015]. Thus, a steep lter roll-o result in a long eec-
tive impulse response. The increased length of the impulse response produces stronger sig-
nal distortions, which include ringing artifacts and wider temporal smearing that can lead
to spurious dependencies of stimulus detectability in the pre-stimulus phase [de Cheveigné
and Simon, 2007]. The rigning artifact occurs at sharp step-like signal transients. Thus to
counteract the rigning artifacts ltering should never be applied across signal discontinu-
ities [Widmann et al., 2015]. To avoid strong signal distortions of this kind it is preferable
to apply wide transition bands whenever possible [Widmann et al., 2015]. Furthermore,
in some situations it is preferable to avoid ltering and instead apply other methods to
reduce noise [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2007] (in some situation noise reduction can be
accomplished by utiliziting the time-shifted PCA or the DSS algorithm (see Sec. 3.7)).
The eect of step lter roll-o also indicates that band-stop lters should not be applied
to suppress the power line noise and that the implementation of high-pass lters should
be considered carefully, since a very steep roll-o often is needed in the case of high-pass
ltering. Specially highpass ltering of ERP signals should be considered carfully since
it is common to use high-pass with a cut-o freqency of 0.1 −1 Hz [Widmann et al.,
2015] [de Cheveigné and Simon, 2007]. One might argue that high-pass ltering should
be avoided but generally in EEG data the strongest source of noise is low-frequency noise
and hence the high-pass lter can make a signicant improvement of the SNR [Widmann
et al., 2015]. The high order of the high-pass lter indicates that such a temporal lter
preferably is applied to the full data set instead of epoched data and that the signal should
be zero-padded at its edges by the same number of samples as the length of the impulse
response minus one [Widmann et al., 2015].
The reason that the stepness of the lter roll-o often is higher for high-pass lter than for
low-pass lter is that the cut-o frequency of the high-pass lter is intended to be low and
specic whereas the low-pass lter typically is higher and more diuse [Widmann et al.,
2015]. The cut-o frequency of an ideal lter is the frequency at which the transition from
pass-band to stop-band occurs [Lathi, 2009]. In the case of a realizable lter cut-o fre-
E.5 The Considerations in Regards to Designing Digital Filters
==PAGE:173==
158
APPENDIX E.
TEMPORAL FILTERING
quency is not that clear dened due to the transition band, however, the cut-o frequency
is always located in the transition band. There are dierent denition to where the cut-o
frequency is located in a realisable lter but the general rule is that the −3 dB cut-o
frequency is utilized for IIR lter and −6 dB is utilized for FIR [Lathi, 2009] [Widmann
et al., 2015]. However, if a forward-backward ltering (see App. E.7) is applied then cut-o
frequency will be increased to −6 dB for IIR lter and −12 dB. Throughout the report
these denitions are used when referring to cut-o frequencies.
In general, since the steepness of the high-pass lter's roll-o is higher than the roll-o
of lowpass lters, it is preferable to utilize two separate lters (a high-pass lter and a
low-pass lter) instead of a bandpass lter because it is uncommon that one is able to
dene dierent roll-o of the two transition [Widmann et al., 2015] whereas the separation
will allow for shallower low-pass lter with less spread in the time domain.
The next consideration when designing realizable lters is what value to assign δ1 and δ2.
δ1 is amplitude of the passband ripple and it is typically assigned a value between 0.001 and
0.002 if the lter has unit gain in the passband. This implies that the lter output is not
amplied or attenuated by more than 0.1-0.2 % in the passband [Widmann et al., 2015].
The passband ripple are in Matlab dened as the peak-to-peak ratio: rp = 20 log10

1+δ1
1−δ1

,
hence in Matlab it is common to assign rp with a value in between 0.0174 and 0.0347 dB.
In regards to the minimum stopband attenuation δ2, it is reasonable to apply a stopband
attentuation between -54 and -60 dB for many ERP applications [Widmann et al., 2015].
This implies that the frequency component within the stopband is attenuated with at
least a factor of between 500 and 1000. However, for high amplitude low-frequency noise
it might be necessary to apply a δ2 that is equal to - 100 dB or stronger [Widmann et al.,
2015].
The last consideration is whether to use a FIR lter or an IIR lter. The FIR has a linear
phase characteristics within the pass-band of the lters. This linear phase characteristic
is absent in the IIR lter [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007]. The linear phase is important
because it leads to a constant group delay across frequency. The group delay is dened as
the negative of the derivative of the phase-response with respect to frequency; dθ(f)
df
where
θ(f) is the phase spectrum of the lter. Alternative, the group delay can also be dened
as the delay of the envelope of the signal at a particular frequency [Lathi, 2009]. Hence,
if the phase characteristic is linear the group delay is constant, which means the each
frequency component of the signal is delay by the same among of time by the lter and
thus the lter does not alter the time domain shape of the signal [Proakis and Manolakis,
2007]. It can be shown that the FIR lter has a perfectly symmetric or antisymmetric
impulse response if the order N is odd. This implies that if the order of the FIR lter is
odd, then the group delay of the lter is N−1
2 . Due to the constant group it is relatively
easy to compensate for the group delay by simply shifting the output back in time by the
group delay. If the output of the lter is backwards shift by the lter's group delay then
the lter is called a zero-phase lter. Notice, that this bachwards shifting in the time do-
main does not require additional computations and that the time-shifting actually makes
the system non-causal because the system will depend on N−1
2
future samples [Widmann
et al., 2015]. The main advantage of the zero-phase lter is that if the signal has a peak
(such as the N2 of an ERP) then a normal FIR lter will delay the peak time whereas the
non-causality of zero-phase FIR lter will compensate for this delay. On the other hand,
this compensation might introduce articial pre-stimulus component in the signal (due to
non-causality and smearing) that might lead to a spurious interpretation of pre-stimulus
phase and a systematically underestimation of onset latencies [Widmann et al., 2015].
The problem with IIR lters are that their group delay varies with frequency within the
E.5 The Considerations in Regards to Designing Digital Filters
==PAGE:174==
APPENDIX E.
TEMPORAL FILTERING
159
bandpass region of the lter. This implies that the IIR lter distort the temporal shape
of broadband signals (like EEG signals). However, it is possible to generate a zero-phase
delay with an IIR lter if the output of the IIR lter is send backwards through the l-
ter (see App. E.7). This type of ltering is known as forward-backward ltering. The
forward-backward ltering will result in a non-causal lter with a symmetric impulse re-
sponse [Widmann et al., 2015]. It is shown in App. E.7 that the magnitude response of
the lter is squared by this type of ltering, which is the reason why a froward-backward
IIR lter is specied to have a cuto frequency at −6 dB (since this −6 dB frequency
is the same freqeuncy as the −3 dB frequency of the original IIR). Lastly, it should be
mentioned that the order of the forward-backward ltering is twice the order of the original
IIR lter and hence that the eective length of the impulse response is doubled by this
type of ltering [Widmann et al., 2015]. Actually, Widmann et alpoints out that IIR lters
always should be performed with double precision and that they can be unstable and thus
require a stability check.
According to [Proakis and Manolakis, 2007] the advantages of the IIR lter is that the
roll-o of an IIR lter is steeper than the roll-o of a FIR lter if they have the same
order. However, according to [Widmann et al., 2015] it is not possible to compare the
order of FIR and IIR lters due to the recursive implementation of IIR lters. Widmann
et al also state that even though the IIR lters are considered to be the more computa-
tionally ecient of the two lter types it only recommendable to apply an IIR lter if a
sharp and causal lter is needed. The common IIR lters to apply in electrophysiology are
Butterworth and elliptic lters.
When a lter design has been chosen implemented the lter should be test to see what
adverse artifacts the lter produces. The test can be accomplished by sending a test signal
through the system. A suitable test signal for ERP analysis it the Gaussian waveform
because it has a broadband spectrum and hence resembles the ERP competent quit well
in both the time and frequency domain [Widmann et al., 2015]. However, it is also useful
to test the lter on a more realistic signal. For an example of such testing see App. E.6.
In this report FIR lters are utilized based on the analysis in App. E.6. However, the
exact design procedures and implementations of digital lter will not be given here since
Matlab were used to do the lter design and implementation.
E.6
Analysis of Temporal Filters
In this thesis the temporal lter will be applied to data with a sample rate of fs = 256 Hz.
The lters include a high-pass lter at 1 Hz and a 8 Hz low-pass lter. The order of these
are dierent and are hence implemented as two dierent lters due to the discussion in
App. E.5. The high-pass lter is designed to have the peak-to-peak ratio rp = 0.0174 dB
in the bandpass and a minimum stopband attenuation of −54 dB. The peak to peak ratio
in the bandpass is the same for the lowpass lter but the minimum stopband attenuation
is set to −60 dB. The lters are rst tested on a Guassian test signal (as suggest in App.
E.5), see Fig. E.2. The upper right plot in Fig. E.2 suggest the temporal smearing of the
Elliptic lter is stronger than the temporal smearing of the zero-phase FIR lter, which is
due to the sharper roll-o of the IIR highpass lter compared to the FIR lowpass lter.
All in all Fig. E.2 suggests that the FIR lters should be applied.
To better understand the impact of the lters on the EEG-data, a test signal was generated.
The test signal is the addition of four sinusoidal with frequencies 1, 4, 7, and 8 Hz. This
signal is the black curve in Fig. E.3. To this test signal 100 sinusoidal with random phase

plot shows that the temporal structure is approximately preserved after ltering.

E.8
IIR lter Design by Impulse Invariance Technique
There exist several methods that can design IIR lters. A common type of these techniques
is the conversion of an analog lter with a specic lter characteristics into a digital lter.
This conversion schem includes method like the approximation of derivatives technique,
the impulse invariance technique and the bilinear transformation [Proakis and Manolakis,
2007]. In the following the impulse invariance technique will be explained. The impulse
invariance method is an easy and popularly way to generate an IIR lter from an equivalent
analog lter [Yang et al., 2015].
The impulse invariance technique is designed to preserve the shape of the impulse response,
which implies that the impulse response of the digital system, h(n), must be the sampled

high-pass lters [Yang et al., 2015]. However, the technique can be used to generate band-
pass and low-pass lters as long as the sampling frequency fs is chosen appropriately
[Proakis and Manolakis, 2007]. The relationship in the time domain lead to the following

E.10
The Implementation of the Gammatone Filter Bank
During their studies, [Patterson et al., 1987] came up with a recursive implementation of
the gammatone lter bank that makes the gammatone lter bank much more ecient than
its roex equivalent. The recursive gammatone lter bank is a very close approximation
of the gammatone lter bank and it ligthens the computational complexity signicantly
[Patterson et al., 1987]. The computational complexity is determined by the number of
operation that has to be performed per second. The number of operations depends on
three variables; number of channels, the number of coecients in the impulse response
and the sampling rate. Generally, the sampling rate can not be below 20 kHz because the
speech perception requires up to 8 kHz to distinguish some fricatives and Nyquist-Shannon
sampling theorem states that the sampling rate should be at least two times the bandwidth
of the signal. It has also been argued that a competent speech recognition machine must
apply at least 32 frequency channels [Patterson et al., 1987].3 This implies that the only
way to decrease the computational complexity is to reduce the number of coecients per
lter. The reducing in coecients per lter can be done by nding a recursive lter which
provides an adequate approximation to the auditory lter. The recursive implementation
decreases the computational complexity by implementing the gammatone lter bank as a
cascade of identical recursive digital lters [Holdsworth et al., 1988]. The reasoning behind
the recursive implementation requires a recall to Eq. 2.6. It was stated in [Holdsworth
et al., 1988] that if fc/b is so large that the two components in Eq. 2.6 do not overlap then
the rst term can be ignored. This is always the case when modelling human auditory
lter and hence
F (g(t)) ≈1
2
 
a−1

1 + j f −fc
b
−n!


1 + j f −fc
b
−n
(E.22)
Notice that the approximation of the Fourier transform of the gammatone lter (Eq. E.22)
is a power function with exponent, −n, which does that it can be approximated by a cascade
of n identical gammatone lters of order 1. Each of these rst order gammatone lters has
the frequency response;


−1
(E.23)
This rst order frequency-shifted low-pass lter has a well-known recursive digital imple-
mentation [Holdsworth et al., 1988]. The details of the recrusive digital implementation
will not be given since the all-pole gammatone lter approximation will be used instead.
The all-pole approximation of the gammatone lter was introduced by [Lyon, 1997]. It
uses the Laplace transform to obtain the rational transfer function of the gammatone lter
impulse response
Hg(s) = aejϕ[s + b + j2πfc]N + e−jϕ[s + b −j2πfc]N
h
(s + b)2 + (2πfc)2iN
The problem with this transfer function is that the zeros falls on the real axis of the s-plane
and that the location along the real axis depends on all parameters. An example is, that
332 frequency channels are needed because a lower lter densities might cause some frequencies
range to be insucient covered (if the frequency range of the lter bank is from 50 to 8000 Hz)
and hence some of the frequency components of the signal might fall in-between lters [Patterson
et al., 1987].

165
one can alter the bandwidth parameter or the phase parameter so that a zero falls exactly
in origo of the s-plane, which tail gain go to zero [Lyon, 1997]. The solution that [Lyon,
1997] proposed to deal with this problem was to remove all the zeros from the transfer
function to obtain the all-pole gammatone lter
HAP,gamma(s) =
K
h

K
[(s −p) (s −p∗)]N
where p is pole position in the s-plane (that has the rectangular form p = −b + j2πfc)
and p∗is the complex conjugate of p. K is a normalization constant, which normally
will be set so that there is an unit gain at DC, hence, H(0) = 1 [Lyon, 1997]. From
App. E.8, it is known that HAP (s) should be converted into its equivalent sampled form.
The conversion can be accomplished by the impulse invariance design technique. It has
been shown that there are little spectral aliasing when applying the impulse invariance
technique to gammatone lters and that this technique was the most accurate among
several alternatives [Yang et al., 2015]. The impulse invariance design technique yields
HAP,gamma(z) =
K
 1 −ep/fsz−1  1 −ep∗/fsz−1N
(E.24)
The reason that [Holdsworth et al., 1988] did not use this place-domain pole-zero de-
composition was the this type of decomposition rst was widely understood in the mid
1990s [Lyon et al., 2010]. The discarding of the zeros in the all-pole gammatone lter sim-
plied the parameterization and it has useful properties. The useful properties includes a
more ecient implementation due to the reduced number of parameter and a more con-
trolled behavior of the tuning-curve tail [Lyon, 1997] [Lyon et al., 2010]. The all-pole
gammatone lter also makes it easier to model level-dependent gain, bandwidth, asymme-
try, and center-frequency shift. These properties are useful in modelling psychophysical
and physiological data [Lyon, 1997].
The gammatone lter bank has been used to approximate basilar membrane motion in
many application. One is the functional auditory module of the Auditory Image Model,
AIM by [Patterson et al., 1995]. The lters in this model were distributed linearly along
the frequency scaled measured in ERBs. To generate a gammatone lter bank in the AIM
the number of lters in the bank and the minimum and maximum lter center frequencies
must be specied by the user. Afterwards, the AIM simulates the inner hair cells by ap-
plying compression (together with a two-dimensional adaptive threshold) to generate the
outputs of the lters [Patterson et al., 1995].
